_Mother o\xe2\x80\x99 mine, O mother o\xe2\x80\x99 mine!_\r\nI know whose tears would come down to me,\r\n    _Mother o\xe2\x80\x99 mine, O mother o\xe2\x80\x99 mine!_\r\n\r\nIf I were damned of body and soul,\r\nI know whose prayers would make me whole,\r\n    _Mother o\xe2\x80\x99 mine, O mother o\xe2\x80\x99 mine!_\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nPREFACE\r\n\r\n\r\nThis is the story of _The Light that Failed_ as it was originally\r\nconceived by the writer.\r\n\r\nRUDYARD KIPLING\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER I\r\n\r\n\r\nSo we settled it all when the storm was done\r\n    As comf\xe2\x80\x99y as comf\xe2\x80\x99y could be;\r\nAnd I was to wait in the barn, my dears,\r\n    Because I was only three;\r\nAnd Teddy would run to the rainbow\xe2\x80\x99s foot,\r\n    Because he was five and a man;\r\nAnd that\xe2\x80\x99s how it all began, my dears,\r\n    And that\xe2\x80\x99s how it all began.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x94_Big Barn Stories_.\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you think she\xe2\x80\x99d do if she caught us? We oughtn\xe2\x80\x99t to have it,\r\nyou know,\xe2\x80\x9d said Maisie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBeat me, and lock you up in your bedroom,\xe2\x80\x9d Dick answered, without\r\nhesitation. \xe2\x80\x9cHave you got the cartridges?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; they\xe2\x80\x99re in my pocket, but they are joggling horribly. Do pin-fire\r\ncartridges go off of their own accord?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t know. Take the revolver, if you are afraid, and let me carry\r\nthem.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m _not_ afraid.\xe2\x80\x9d Maisie strode forward swiftly, a hand in her pocket\r\nand her chin in the air. Dick followed with a small pin-fire revolver.\r\n\r\nThe children had discovered that their lives would be unendurable\r\nwithout pistol-practice. After much forethought and self-denial, Dick\r\nhad saved seven shillings and sixpence, the price of a badly\r\nconstructed Belgian revolver. Maisie could only contribute half a crown\r\nto the syndicate for the purchase of a hundred cartridges. \xe2\x80\x9cYou can\r\nsave better than I can, Dick,\xe2\x80\x9d she explained; \xe2\x80\x9cI like nice things to\r\neat, and it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t matter to you. Besides, boys ought to do these\r\nthings.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDick grumbled a little at the arrangement, but went out and made the\r\npurchase, which the children were then on their way to test. Revolvers\r\ndid not lie in the scheme of their daily life as decreed for them by\r\nthe guardian who was incorrectly supposed to stand in the place of a\r\nmother to these two orphans. Dick had been under her care for six\r\nyears, during which time she had made her profit of the allowances\r\nsupposed to be expended on his clothes, and, partly through\r\nthoughtlessness, partly through a natural desire to pain,\xe2\x80\x94she was a\r\nwidow of some years anxious to marry again,\xe2\x80\x94had made his days\r\nburdensome on his young shoulders.\r\n\r\nWhere he had looked for love, she gave him first aversion and then\r\nhate.\r\n\r\nWhere he growing older had sought a little sympathy, she gave him\r\nridicule. The many hours that she could spare from the ordering of her\r\nsmall house she devoted to what she called the home-training of Dick\r\nHeldar. Her religion, manufactured in the main by her own intelligence\r\nand a keen study of the Scriptures, was an aid to her in this matter.\r\nAt such times as she herself was not personally displeased with Dick,\r\nshe left him to understand that he had a heavy account to settle with\r\nhis Creator; wherefore Dick learned to loathe his God as intensely as\r\nhe loathed Mrs. Jennett; and this is not a wholesome frame of mind for\r\nthe young. Since she chose to regard him as a hopeless liar, when dread\r\nof pain drove him to his first untruth, he naturally developed into a\r\nliar, but an economical and self-contained one, never throwing away the\r\nleast unnecessary fib, and never hesitating at the blackest, were it\r\nonly plausible, that might make his life a little easier. The treatment\r\ntaught him at least the power of living alone,\xe2\x80\x94a power that was of\r\nservice to him when he went to a public school and the boys laughed at\r\nhis clothes, which were poor in quality and much mended. In the\r\nholidays he returned to the teachings of Mrs. Jennett, and, that the\r\nchain of discipline might not be weakened by association with the\r\nworld, was generally beaten, on one account or another, before he had\r\nbeen twelve hours under her roof.\r\n\r\nThe autumn of one year brought him a companion in bondage, a\r\nlong-haired, gray-eyed little atom, as self-contained as himself, who\r\nmoved about the house silently and for the first few weeks spoke only\r\nto the goat that was her chiefest friend on earth and lived in the\r\nback-garden. Mrs. Jennett objected to the goat on the grounds that he\r\nwas un-Christian,\xe2\x80\x94which he certainly was. \xe2\x80\x9cThen,\xe2\x80\x9d said the atom,\r\nchoosing her words very deliberately, \xe2\x80\x9cI shall write to my\r\nlawyer-peoples and tell them that you are a very bad woman. Amomma is\r\nmine, mine, mine!\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Jennett made a movement to the hall, where\r\ncertain umbrellas and canes stood in a rack. The atom understood as\r\nclearly as Dick what this meant. \xe2\x80\x9cI have been beaten before,\xe2\x80\x9d she said,\r\nstill in the same passionless voice; \xe2\x80\x9cI have been beaten worse than you\r\ncan ever beat me. If you beat me I shall write to my lawyer-peoples and\r\ntell them that you do not give me enough to eat. I am not afraid of\r\nyou.\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Jennett did not go into the hall, and the atom, after a\r\npause to assure herself that all danger of war was past, went out, to\r\nweep bitterly on Amomma\xe2\x80\x99s neck.\r\n\r\nDick learned to know her as Maisie, and at first mistrusted her\r\nprofoundly, for he feared that she might interfere with the small\r\nliberty of action left to him. She did not, however; and she\r\nvolunteered no friendliness until Dick had taken the first steps. Long\r\nbefore the holidays were over, the stress of punishment shared in\r\ncommon drove the children together, if it were only to play into each\r\nother\xe2\x80\x99s hands as they prepared lies for Mrs. Jennett\xe2\x80\x99s use. When Dick\r\nreturned to school, Maisie whispered, \xe2\x80\x9cNow I shall be all alone to take\r\ncare of myself; but,\xe2\x80\x9d and she nodded her head bravely, \xe2\x80\x9cI can do it.\r\nYou promised to send Amomma a grass collar. Send it soon.\xe2\x80\x9d A week later\r\nshe asked for that collar by return of post, and was not pleased when\r\nshe learned that it took time to make. When at last Dick forwarded the\r\ngift, she forgot to thank him for it.\r\n\r\nMany holidays had come and gone since that day, and Dick had grown into\r\na lanky hobbledehoy more than ever conscious of his bad clothes. Not\r\nfor a moment had Mrs. Jennett relaxed her tender care of him, but the\r\naverage canings of a public school\xe2\x80\x94Dick fell under punishment about\r\nthree times a month\xe2\x80\x94filled him with contempt for her powers. \xe2\x80\x9cShe\r\ndoesn\xe2\x80\x99t hurt,\xe2\x80\x9d he explained to Maisie, who urged him to rebellion, \xe2\x80\x9cand\r\nshe is kinder to you after she has whacked me.\xe2\x80\x9d Dick shambled through\r\nthe days unkempt in body and savage in soul, as the smaller boys of the\r\nschool learned to know, for when the spirit moved him he would hit\r\nthem, cunningly and with science. The same spirit made him more than\r\nonce try to tease Maisie, but the girl refused to be made unhappy. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\r\nare both miserable as it is,\xe2\x80\x9d said she. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat is the use of trying to\r\nmake things worse? Let\xe2\x80\x99s find things to do, and forget things.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe pistol was the outcome of that search. It could only be used on the\r\nmuddiest foreshore of the beach, far away from the bathing-machines and\r\npierheads, below the grassy slopes of Fort Keeling. The tide ran out\r\nnearly two miles on that coast, and the many-coloured mud-banks,\r\ntouched by the sun, sent up a lamentable smell of dead weed. It was\r\nlate in the afternoon when Dick and Maisie arrived on their ground,\r\nAmomma trotting patiently behind them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMf!\xe2\x80\x9d said Maisie, sniffing the air. \xe2\x80\x9cI wonder what makes the sea so\r\nsmelly? I don\xe2\x80\x99t like it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou never like anything that isn\xe2\x80\x99t made just for you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick\r\nbluntly. \xe2\x80\x9cGive me the cartridges, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll try first shot. How far does\r\none of these little revolvers carry?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, half a mile,\xe2\x80\x9d said Maisie, promptly. \xe2\x80\x9cAt least it makes an awful\r\nnoise. Be careful with the cartridges; I don\xe2\x80\x99t like those jagged\r\nstick-up things on the rim. Dick, do be careful.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right. I know how to load. I\xe2\x80\x99ll fire at the breakwater out there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe fired, and Amomma ran away bleating. The bullet threw up a spurt of\r\nmud to the right of the wood-wreathed piles.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThrows high and to the right. You try, Maisie. Mind, it\xe2\x80\x99s loaded all\r\nround.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMaisie took the pistol and stepped delicately to the verge of the mud,\r\nher hand firmly closed on the butt, her mouth and left eye screwed up.\r\n\r\nDick sat down on a tuft of bank and laughed. Amomma returned very\r\ncautiously. He was accustomed to strange experiences in his afternoon\r\nwalks, and, finding the cartridge-box unguarded, made investigations\r\nwith his nose. Maisie fired, but could not see where the bullet went.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think it hit the post,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, shading her eyes and looking out\r\nacross the sailless sea.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know it has gone out to the Marazion Bell-buoy,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick, with a\r\nchuckle. \xe2\x80\x9cFire low and to the left; then perhaps you\xe2\x80\x99ll get it. Oh,\r\nlook at Amomma!\xe2\x80\x94he\xe2\x80\x99s eating the cartridges!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMaisie turned, the revolver in her hand, just in time to see Amomma\r\nscampering away from the pebbles Dick threw after him. Nothing is\r\nsacred to a billy-goat. Being well fed and the adored of his mistress,\r\nAmomma had naturally swallowed two loaded pin-fire cartridges. Maisie\r\nhurried up to assure herself that Dick had not miscounted the tale.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, he\xe2\x80\x99s eaten two.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHorrid little beast! Then they\xe2\x80\x99ll joggle about inside him and blow up,\r\nand serve him right.... Oh, Dick! have I killed you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRevolvers are tricky things for young hands to deal with. Maisie could\r\nnot explain how it had happened, but a veil of reeking smoke separated\r\nher from Dick, and she was quite certain that the pistol had gone off\r\nin his face. Then she heard him sputter, and dropped on her knees\r\nbeside him, crying, \xe2\x80\x9cDick, you aren\xe2\x80\x99t hurt, are you? I didn\xe2\x80\x99t mean it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course you didn\xe2\x80\x99t, said Dick, coming out of the smoke and wiping\r\nhis cheek. \xe2\x80\x9cBut you nearly blinded me. That powder stuff stings\r\nawfully.\xe2\x80\x9d A neat little splash of gray lead on a stone showed where the\r\nbullet had gone. Maisie began to whimper.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick, jumping to his feet and shaking himself. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m not a\r\nbit hurt.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, but I might have killed you,\xe2\x80\x9d protested Maisie, the corners of her\r\nmouth drooping. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat should I have done then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGone home and told Mrs. Jennett.\xe2\x80\x9d Dick grinned at the thought; then,\r\nsoftening, \xe2\x80\x9cPlease don\xe2\x80\x99t worry about it. Besides, we are wasting time.\r\n\r\nWe\xe2\x80\x99ve got to get back to tea. I\xe2\x80\x99ll take the revolver for a bit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMaisie would have wept on the least encouragement, but Dick\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nindifference, albeit his hand was shaking as he picked up the pistol,\r\nrestrained her. She lay panting on the beach while Dick methodically\r\nbombarded the breakwater. \xe2\x80\x9cGot it at last!\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed, as a lock of\r\nweed flew from the wood.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet me try,\xe2\x80\x9d said Maisie, imperiously. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m all right now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey fired in turns till the rickety little revolver nearly shook\r\nitself to pieces, and Amomma the outcast\xe2\x80\x94because he might blow up at\r\nany moment\xe2\x80\x94browsed in the background and wondered why stones were\r\nthrown at him. Then they found a balk of timber floating in a pool\r\nwhich was commanded by the seaward slope of Fort Keeling, and they sat\r\ndown together before this new target.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNext holidays,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick, as the now thoroughly fouled revolver\r\nkicked wildly in his hand, \xe2\x80\x9cwe\xe2\x80\x99ll get another pistol,\xe2\x80\x94central\r\nfire,\xe2\x80\x94that will carry farther.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere won\xe2\x80\x99t be any next holidays for me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Maisie. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m going\r\naway.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere to?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know. My lawyers have written to Mrs. Jennett, and I\xe2\x80\x99ve got to\r\nbe educated somewhere,\xe2\x80\x94in France, perhaps,\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t know where; but I\r\nshall be glad to go away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shan\xe2\x80\x99t like it a bit. I suppose I shall be left. Look here, Maisie,\r\nis it really true you\xe2\x80\x99re going? Then these holidays will be the last I\r\nshall see anything of you; and I go back to school next week. I wish\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe young blood turned his cheeks scarlet. Maisie was picking\r\ngrass-tufts and throwing them down the slope at a yellow sea-poppy\r\nnodding all by itself to the illimitable levels of the mud-flats and\r\nthe milk-white sea beyond.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, after a pause, \xe2\x80\x9cthat I could see you again\r\nsometime.\r\n\r\nYou wish that, too?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but it would have been better if\xe2\x80\x94if\xe2\x80\x94you had\xe2\x80\x94shot straight over\r\nthere\xe2\x80\x94down by the breakwater.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMaisie looked with large eyes for a moment. And this was the boy who\r\nonly ten days before had decorated Amomma\xe2\x80\x99s horns with cut-paper\r\nham-frills and turned him out, a bearded derision, among the public\r\nways! Then she dropped her eyes: this was not the boy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t be stupid,\xe2\x80\x9d she said reprovingly, and with swift instinct\r\nattacked the side-issue. \xe2\x80\x9cHow selfish you are! Just think what I should\r\nhave felt if that horrid thing had killed you! I\xe2\x80\x99m quite miserable\r\nenough already.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy? Because you\xe2\x80\x99re going away from Mrs. Jennett?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFrom me, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNo answer for a long time. Dick dared not look at her. He felt, though\r\nhe did not know, all that the past four years had been to him, and this\r\nthe more acutely since he had no knowledge to put his feelings in\r\nwords.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cI suppose it is.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMaisie, you must know. _I\xe2\x80\x99m_ not supposing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s go home,\xe2\x80\x9d said Maisie, weakly.\r\n\r\nBut Dick was not minded to retreat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t say things,\xe2\x80\x9d he pleaded, \xe2\x80\x9cand I\xe2\x80\x99m awfully sorry for teasing\r\nyou about Amomma the other day. It\xe2\x80\x99s all different now, Maisie, can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nyou see? And you might have told me that you were going, instead of\r\nleaving me to find out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou didn\xe2\x80\x99t. I did tell. Oh, Dick, what\xe2\x80\x99s the use of worrying?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere isn\xe2\x80\x99t any; but we\xe2\x80\x99ve been together years and years, and I didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nknow how much I cared.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t believe you ever did care.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I didn\xe2\x80\x99t; but I do,\xe2\x80\x94I care awfully now, Maisie,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\ngulped,\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cMaisie, darling, say you care too, please.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do, indeed I do; but it won\xe2\x80\x99t be any use.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause I am going away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but if you promise before you go. Only say\xe2\x80\x94will you?\xe2\x80\x9d A second\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cdarling\xe2\x80\x99 came to his lips more easily than the first. There were few\r\nendearments in Dick\xe2\x80\x99s home or school life; he had to find them by\r\ninstinct. Dick caught the little hand blackened with the escaped gas of\r\nthe revolver.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI promise,\xe2\x80\x9d she said solemnly; \xe2\x80\x9cbut if I care there is no need for\r\npromising.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd do you care?\xe2\x80\x9d For the first time in the past few minutes their\r\neyes met and spoke for them who had no skill in speech....\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Dick, don\xe2\x80\x99t! Please don\xe2\x80\x99t! It was all right when we said\r\ngood-morning; but now it\xe2\x80\x99s all different!\xe2\x80\x9d Amomma looked on from afar.\r\n\r\nHe had seen his property quarrel frequently, but he had never seen\r\nkisses exchanged before. The yellow sea-poppy was wiser, and nodded its\r\nhead approvingly. Considered as a kiss, that was a failure, but since\r\nit was the first, other than those demanded by duty, in all the world\r\nthat either had ever given or taken, it opened to them new worlds, and\r\nevery one of them glorious, so that they were lifted above the\r\nconsideration of any worlds at all, especially those in which tea is\r\nnecessary, and sat still, holding each other\xe2\x80\x99s hands and saying not a\r\nword.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can\xe2\x80\x99t forget now,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick, at last. There was that on his cheek\r\nthat stung more than gunpowder.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have forgotten anyhow,\xe2\x80\x9d said Maisie, and they looked at\r\neach other and saw that each was changed from the companion of an hour\r\nago to a wonder and a mystery they could not understand. The sun began\r\nto set, and a night-wind thrashed along the bents of the foreshore.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe shall be awfully late for tea,\xe2\x80\x9d said Maisie. \xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s go home.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s use the rest of the cartridges first,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick; and he helped\r\nMaisie down the slope of the fort to the sea,\xe2\x80\x94a descent that she was\r\nquite capable of covering at full speed. Equally gravely Maisie took\r\nthe grimy hand. Dick bent forward clumsily; Maisie drew the hand away,\r\nand Dick blushed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s very pretty,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPooh!\xe2\x80\x9d said Maisie, with a little laugh of gratified vanity. She stood\r\nclose to Dick as he loaded the revolver for the last time and fired\r\nover the sea with a vague notion at the back of his head that he was\r\nprotecting Maisie from all the evils in the world. A puddle far across\r\nthe mud caught the last rays of the sun and turned into a wrathful red\r\ndisc. The light held Dick\xe2\x80\x99s attention for a moment, and as he raised\r\nhis revolver there fell upon him a renewed sense of the miraculous, in\r\nthat he was standing by Maisie who had promised to care for him for an\r\nindefinite length of time till such date as\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94 A gust of the growing\r\nwind drove the girl\xe2\x80\x99s long black hair across his face as she stood with\r\nher hand on his shoulder calling Amomma \xe2\x80\x9ca little beast,\xe2\x80\x9d and for a\r\nmoment he was in the dark,\xe2\x80\x94a darkness that stung. The bullet went\r\nsinging out to the empty sea.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSpoilt my aim,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, shaking his head. \xe2\x80\x9cThere aren\xe2\x80\x99t any more\r\ncartridges; we shall have to run home.\xe2\x80\x9d But they did not run. They\r\nwalked very slowly, arm in arm. And it was a matter of indifference to\r\nthem whether the neglected Amomma with two pin-fire cartridges in his\r\ninside blew up or trotted beside them; for they had come into a golden\r\nheritage and were disposing of it with all the wisdom of all their\r\nyears.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I shall be\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d quoth Dick, valiantly. Then he checked himself: \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t know what I shall be. I don\xe2\x80\x99t seem to be able to pass any exams,\r\nbut I can make awful caricatures of the masters. Ho! Ho!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBe an artist, then,\xe2\x80\x9d said Maisie. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re always laughing at my trying\r\nto draw; and it will do you good.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll never laugh at anything you do,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll be an artist,\r\nand I\xe2\x80\x99ll do things.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cArtists always want money, don\xe2\x80\x99t they?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve got a hundred and twenty pounds a year of my own. My guardians\r\ntell me I\xe2\x80\x99m to have it when I come of age. That will be enough to begin\r\nwith.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, I\xe2\x80\x99m rich,\xe2\x80\x9d said Maisie. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve got three hundred a year all my own\r\nwhen I\xe2\x80\x99m twenty-one. That\xe2\x80\x99s why Mrs. Jennett is kinder to me than she\r\nis to you. I wish, though, that I had somebody that belonged to\r\nme,\xe2\x80\x94just a father or a mother.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou belong to me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick, \xe2\x80\x9cfor ever and ever.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, we belong\xe2\x80\x94for ever. It\xe2\x80\x99s very nice.\xe2\x80\x9d She squeezed his arm. The\r\nkindly darkness hid them both, and, emboldened because he could only\r\njust see the profile of Maisie\xe2\x80\x99s cheek with the long lashes veiling the\r\ngray eyes, Dick at the front door delivered himself of the words he had\r\nbeen boggling over for the last two hours.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I\xe2\x80\x94love you, Maisie,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, in a whisper that seemed to him to\r\nring across the world,\xe2\x80\x94the world that he would to-morrow or the next\r\nday set out to conquer.\r\n\r\nThere was a scene, not, for the sake of discipline, to be reported,\r\nwhen Mrs. Jennett would have fallen upon him, first for disgraceful\r\nunpunctuality, and secondly for nearly killing himself with a forbidden\r\nweapon.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was playing with it, and it went off by itself,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick, when the\r\npowder-pocked cheek could no longer be hidden, \xe2\x80\x9cbut if you think you\xe2\x80\x99re\r\ngoing to lick me you\xe2\x80\x99re wrong. You are never going to touch me again.\r\n\r\nSit down and give me my tea. You can\xe2\x80\x99t cheat us out of that, anyhow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Jennett gasped and became livid. Maisie said nothing, but\r\nencouraged Dick with her eyes, and he behaved abominably all that\r\nevening. Mrs. Jennett prophesied an immediate judgment of Providence\r\nand a descent into Tophet later, but Dick walked in Paradise and would\r\nnot hear. Only when he was going to bed Mrs. Jennett recovered and\r\nasserted herself. He had bidden Maisie good-night with down-dropped\r\neyes and from a distance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you aren\xe2\x80\x99t a gentleman you might try to behave like one,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs.\r\n\r\nJennett, spitefully. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve been quarrelling with Maisie again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis meant that the usual good-night kiss had been omitted. Maisie,\r\nwhite to the lips, thrust her cheek forward with a fine air of\r\nindifference, and was duly pecked by Dick, who tramped out of the room\r\nred as fire. That night he dreamed a wild dream. He had won all the\r\nworld and brought it to Maisie in a cartridge-box, but she turned it\r\nover with her foot, and, instead of saying \xe2\x80\x9cThank you,\xe2\x80\x9d cried\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cWhere is\r\nthe grass collar you promised for Amomma? Oh, how selfish you are!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER II\r\n\r\n\r\nThen we brought the lances down, then the bugles blew,\r\nWhen we went to Kandahar, ridin\xe2\x80\x99 two an\xe2\x80\x99 two,\r\n    Ridin\xe2\x80\x99, ridin\xe2\x80\x99, ridin\xe2\x80\x99, two an\xe2\x80\x99 two,\r\n        Ta-ra-ra-ra-ra-ra-ra,\r\nAll the way to Kandahar, ridin\xe2\x80\x99 two an\xe2\x80\x99 two.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x94_Barrack-Room Ballad_.\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m not angry with the British public, but I wish we had a few\r\nthousand of them scattered among these rooks. They wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be in such\r\na hurry to get at their morning papers then. Can\xe2\x80\x99t you imagine the\r\nregulation householder\xe2\x80\x94Lover of Justice, Constant Reader,\r\nPaterfamilias, and all that lot\xe2\x80\x94frizzling on hot gravel?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWith a blue veil over his head, and his clothes in strips. Has any man\r\nhere a needle? I\xe2\x80\x99ve got a piece of sugar-sack.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll lend you a packing-needle for six square inches of it then. Both\r\nmy knees are worn through.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy not six square acres, while you\xe2\x80\x99re about it? But lend me the\r\nneedle, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll see what I can do with the selvage. I don\xe2\x80\x99t think\r\nthere\xe2\x80\x99s enough to protect my royal body from the cold blast as it is.\r\nWhat are you doing with that everlasting sketch-book of yours, Dick?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStudy of our Special Correspondent repairing his wardrobe,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick,\r\ngravely, as the other man kicked off a pair of sorely worn\r\nriding-breeches and began to fit a square of coarse canvas over the\r\nmost obvious open space. He grunted disconsolately as the vastness of\r\nthe void developed itself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSugar-bags, indeed! Hi! you pilot man there! lend me all the sails for\r\nthat whale-boat.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA fez-crowned head bobbed up in the stern-sheets, divided itself into\r\nexact halves with one flashing grin, and bobbed down again. The man of\r\nthe tattered breeches, clad only in a Norfolk jacket and a gray flannel\r\nshirt, went on with his clumsy sewing, while Dick chuckled over the\r\nsketch.\r\n\r\nSome twenty whale-boats were nuzzling a sand-bank which was dotted with\r\nEnglish soldiery of half a dozen corps, bathing or washing their\r\nclothes. A heap of boat-rollers, commissariat-boxes, sugar-bags, and\r\nflour- and small-arm-ammunition-cases showed where one of the\r\nwhale-boats had been compelled to unload hastily; and a regimental\r\ncarpenter was swearing aloud as he tried, on a wholly insufficient\r\nallowance of white lead, to plaster up the sun-parched gaping seams of\r\nthe boat herself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFirst the bloomin\xe2\x80\x99 rudder snaps,\xe2\x80\x9d said he to the world in general;\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cthen the mast goes; an\xe2\x80\x99 then, s\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x9chelp me, when she can\xe2\x80\x99t do nothin\xe2\x80\x99\r\nelse, she opens \xe2\x80\x99erself out like a cock-eyes Chinese lotus.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExactly the case with my breeches, whoever you are,\xe2\x80\x9d said the tailor,\r\nwithout looking up. \xe2\x80\x9cDick, I wonder when I shall see a decent shop\r\nagain.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was no answer, save the incessant angry murmur of the Nile as it\r\nraced round a basalt-walled bend and foamed across a rock-ridge half a\r\nmile upstream. It was as though the brown weight of the river would\r\ndrive the white men back to their own country. The indescribable scent\r\nof Nile mud in the air told that the stream was falling and the next\r\nfew miles would be no light thing for the whale-boats to overpass. The\r\ndesert ran down almost to the banks, where, among gray, red, and black\r\nhillocks, a camel-corps was encamped. No man dared even for a day lose\r\ntouch of the slow-moving boats; there had been no fighting for weeks\r\npast, and throughout all that time the Nile had never spared them.\r\nRapid had followed rapid, rock rock, and island-group island-group,\r\ntill the rank and file had long since lost all count of direction and\r\nvery nearly of time. They were moving somewhere, they did not know why,\r\nto do something, they did not know what. Before them lay the Nile, and\r\nat the other end of it was one Gordon, fighting for the dear life, in a\r\ntown called Khartoum. There were columns of British troops in the\r\ndesert, or in one of the many deserts; there were yet more columns\r\nwaiting to embark on the river; there were fresh drafts waiting at\r\nAssioot and Assuan; there were lies and rumours running over the face\r\nof the hopeless land from Suakin to the Sixth Cataract, and men\r\nsupposed generally that there must be some one in authority to direct\r\nthe general scheme of the many movements. The duty of that particular\r\nriver-column was to keep the whale-boats afloat in the water, to avoid\r\ntrampling on the villagers\xe2\x80\x99 crops when the gangs \xe2\x80\x9ctracked\xe2\x80\x99 the boats\r\nwith lines thrown from midstream, to get as much sleep and food as was\r\npossible, and, above all, to press on without delay in the teeth of the\r\nchurning Nile.\r\n\r\nWith the soldiers sweated and toiled the correspondents of the\r\nnewspapers, and they were almost as ignorant as their companions. But\r\nit was above all things necessary that England at breakfast should be\r\namused and thrilled and interested, whether Gordon lived or died, or\r\nhalf the British army went to pieces in the sands. The Soudan campaign\r\nwas a picturesque one, and lent itself to vivid word-painting. Now and\r\nagain a \xe2\x80\x9cSpecial\xe2\x80\x99 managed to get slain,\xe2\x80\x94which was not altogether a\r\ndisadvantage to the paper that employed him,\xe2\x80\x94and more often the\r\nhand-to-hand nature of the fighting allowed of miraculous escapes which\r\nwere worth telegraphing home at eighteenpence the word. There were many\r\ncorrespondents with many corps and columns,\xe2\x80\x94from the veterans who had\r\nfollowed on the heels of the cavalry that occupied Cairo in \xe2\x80\x9982, what\r\ntime Arabi Pasha called himself king, who had seen the first miserable\r\nwork round Suakin when the sentries were cut up nightly and the scrub\r\nswarmed with spears, to youngsters jerked into the business at the end\r\nof a telegraph-wire to take the places of their betters killed or\r\ninvalided.\r\n\r\nAmong the seniors\xe2\x80\x94those who knew every shift and change in the\r\nperplexing postal arrangements, the value of the seediest, weediest\r\nEgyptian garron offered for sale in Cairo or Alexandria, who could talk\r\na telegraph-clerk into amiability and soothe the ruffled vanity of a\r\nnewly appointed staff-officer when press regulations became\r\nburdensome\xe2\x80\x94was the man in the flannel shirt, the black-browed\r\nTorpenhow. He represented the Central Southern Syndicate in the\r\ncampaign, as he had represented it in the Egyptian war, and elsewhere.\r\nThe syndicate did not concern itself greatly with criticisms of attack\r\nand the like. It supplied the masses, and all it demanded was\r\npicturesqueness and abundance of detail; for there is more joy in\r\nEngland over a soldier who insubordinately steps out of square to\r\nrescue a comrade than over twenty generals slaving even to baldness at\r\nthe gross details of transport and commissariat.\r\n\r\nHe had met at Suakin a young man, sitting on the edge of a recently\r\nabandoned redoubt about the size of a hat-box, sketching a clump of\r\nshell-torn bodies on the gravel plain.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you for?\xe2\x80\x9d said Torpenhow. The greeting of the correspondent\r\nis that of the commercial traveller on the road.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy own hand,\xe2\x80\x9d said the young man, without looking up. \xe2\x80\x9cHave you any\r\ntobacco?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTorpenhow waited till the sketch was finished, and when he had looked\r\nat it said, \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s your business here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing; there was a row, so I came. I\xe2\x80\x99m supposed to be doing\r\nsomething down at the painting-slips among the boats, or else I\xe2\x80\x99m in\r\ncharge of the condenser on one of the water-ships. I\xe2\x80\x99ve forgotten\r\nwhich.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve cheek enough to build a redoubt with,\xe2\x80\x9d said Torpenhow, and took\r\nstock of the new acquaintance. \xe2\x80\x9cDo you always draw like that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe young man produced more sketches. \xe2\x80\x9cRow on a Chinese pig-boat,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nhe, sententiously, showing them one after another.\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cChief mate dirked\r\nby a comprador.\xe2\x80\x94Junk ashore off Hakodate.\xe2\x80\x94Somali muleteer being\r\nflogged.\xe2\x80\x94Star-shelled bursting over camp at Berbera.\xe2\x80\x94Slave-dhow being\r\nchased round Tajurrah Bah.\xe2\x80\x94Soldier lying dead in the moonlight outside\r\nSuakin.\xe2\x80\x94throat cut by Fuzzies.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cH\xe2\x80\x99m!\xe2\x80\x9d said Torpenhow, \xe2\x80\x9ccan\xe2\x80\x99t say I care for Verestchagin-and-water\r\nmyself, but there\xe2\x80\x99s no accounting for tastes. Doing anything now, are\r\nyou?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. I\xe2\x80\x99m amusing myself here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTorpenhow looked at the aching desolation of the place. \xe2\x80\x9cFaith, you\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\nqueer notions of amusement. Got any money?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEnough to go on with. Look here: do you want me to do war-work?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_I_ don\xe2\x80\x99t. My syndicate may, though. You can draw more than a little,\r\nand I don\xe2\x80\x99t suppose you care much what you get, do you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot this time. I want my chance first.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTorpenhow looked at the sketches again, and nodded. \xe2\x80\x9cYes, you\xe2\x80\x99re right\r\nto take your first chance when you can get it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe rode away swiftly through the Gate of the Two War-Ships, rattled\r\nacross the causeway into the town, and wired to his syndicate, \xe2\x80\x9cGot man\r\nhere, picture-work. Good and cheap. Shall I arrange? Will do\r\nletterpress with sketches.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe man on the redoubt sat swinging his legs and murmuring, \xe2\x80\x9cI knew the\r\nchance would come, sooner or later. By Gad, they\xe2\x80\x99ll have to sweat for\r\nit if I come through this business alive!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn the evening Torpenhow was able to announce to his friend that the\r\nCentral Southern Agency was willing to take him on trial, paying\r\nexpenses for three months. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd, by the way, what\xe2\x80\x99s your name?\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nTorpenhow.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHeldar. Do they give me a free hand?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99ve taken you on chance. You must justify the choice. You\xe2\x80\x99d better\r\nstick to me. I\xe2\x80\x99m going up-country with a column, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll do what I can\r\nfor you. Give me some of your sketches taken here, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll send \xe2\x80\x99em\r\nalong.\xe2\x80\x9d To himself he said, \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s the best bargain the Central\r\nsouthern has ever made; and they got me cheaply enough.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo it came to pass that, after some purchase of horse-flesh and\r\narrangements financial and political, Dick was made free of the New and\r\nHonourable Fraternity of war correspondents, who all possess the\r\ninalienable right of doing as much work as they can and getting as much\r\nfor it as Providence and their owners shall please. To these things are\r\nadded in time, if the brother be worthy, the power of glib speech that\r\nneither man nor woman can resist when a meal or a bed is in question,\r\nthe eye of a horse-cope, the skill of a cook, the constitution of a\r\nbullock, the digestion of an ostrich, and an infinite adaptability to\r\nall circumstances. But many die before they attain to this degree, and\r\nthe past-masters in the craft appear for the most part in dress-clothes\r\nwhen they are in England, and thus their glory is hidden from the\r\nmultitude.\r\n\r\nDick followed Torpenhow wherever the latter\xe2\x80\x99s fancy chose to lead him,\r\nand between the two they managed to accomplish some work that almost\r\nsatisfied themselves. It was not an easy life in any way, and under its\r\ninfluence the two were drawn very closely together, for they ate from\r\nthe same dish, they shared the same water-bottle, and, most binding tie\r\nof all, their mails went off together. It was Dick who managed to make\r\ngloriously drunk a telegraph-clerk in a palm hut far beyond the Second\r\nCataract, and, while the man lay in bliss on the floor, possessed\r\nhimself of some laboriously acquired exclusive information, forwarded\r\nby a confiding correspondent of an opposition syndicate, made a careful\r\nduplicate of the matter, and brought the result to Torpenhow, who said\r\nthat all was fair in love or war correspondence, and built an excellent\r\ndescriptive article from his rival\xe2\x80\x99s riotous waste of words. It was\r\nTorpenhow who\xe2\x80\x94but the tale of their adventures, together and apart,\r\nfrom Philae to the waste wilderness of Herawi and Muella, would fill\r\nmany books. They had been penned into a square side by side, in deadly\r\nfear of being shot by over-excited soldiers; they had fought with\r\nbaggage-camels in the chill dawn; they had jogged along in silence\r\nunder blinding sun on indefatigable little Egyptian horses; and they\r\nhad floundered on the shallows of the Nile when the whale-boat in which\r\nthey had found a berth chose to hit a hidden rock and rip out half her\r\nbottom-planks.\r\n\r\nNow they were sitting on the sand-bank, and the whale-boats were\r\nbringing up the remainder of the column.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Torpenhow, as he put the last rude stitches into his\r\nover-long-neglected gear, \xe2\x80\x9cit has been a beautiful business.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe patch or the campaign?\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick. \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t think much of either,\r\nmyself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou want the _Euryalus_ brought up above the Third Cataract, don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nyou? and eighty-one-ton guns at Jakdul? Now, _I\xe2\x80\x99m_ quite satisfied with\r\nmy breeches.\xe2\x80\x9d He turned round gravely to exhibit himself, after the\r\nmanner of a clown.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s very pretty. Specially the lettering on the sack. G.B.T.\r\nGovernment Bullock Train. That\xe2\x80\x99s a sack from India.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s my initials,\xe2\x80\x94Gilbert Belling Torpenhow. I stole the cloth on\r\npurpose.\r\n\r\nWhat the mischief are the camel-corps doing yonder?\xe2\x80\x9d Torpenhow shaded\r\nhis eyes and looked across the scrub-strewn gravel.\r\n\r\nA bugle blew furiously, and the men on the bank hurried to their arms\r\nand accoutrements.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x9cPisan soldiery surprised while bathing,\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x99 remarked Dick, calmly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cD\xe2\x80\x99you remember the picture? It\xe2\x80\x99s by Michael Angelo; all beginners copy\r\nit. That scrub\xe2\x80\x99s alive with enemy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe camel-corps on the bank yelled to the infantry to come to them, and\r\na hoarse shouting down the river showed that the remainder of the\r\ncolumn had wind of the trouble and was hastening to take share in it.\r\nAs swiftly as a reach of still water is crisped by the wind, the\r\nrock-strewn ridges and scrub-topped hills were troubled and alive with\r\narmed men.\r\n\r\nMercifully, it occurred to these to stand far off for a time, to shout\r\nand gesticulate joyously. One man even delivered himself of a long\r\nstory. The camel-corps did not fire. They were only too glad of a\r\nlittle breathing-space, until some sort of square could be formed. The\r\nmen on the sand-bank ran to their side; and the whale-boats, as they\r\ntoiled up within shouting distance, were thrust into the nearest bank\r\nand emptied of all save the sick and a few men to guard them. The Arab\r\norator ceased his outcries, and his friends howled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey look like the Mahdi\xe2\x80\x99s men,\xe2\x80\x9d said Torpenhow, elbowing himself into\r\nthe crush of the square; \xe2\x80\x9cbut what thousands of \xe2\x80\x99em there are! The\r\ntribes hereabout aren\xe2\x80\x99t against us, I know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen the Mahdi\xe2\x80\x99s taken another town,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick, \xe2\x80\x9cand set all these\r\nyelping devils free to show us up. Lend us your glass.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOur scouts should have told us of this. We\xe2\x80\x99ve been trapped,\xe2\x80\x9d said a\r\nsubaltern. \xe2\x80\x9cAren\xe2\x80\x99t the camel guns ever going to begin? Hurry up, you\r\nmen!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was no need of any order. The men flung themselves panting\r\nagainst the sides of the square, for they had good reason to know that\r\nwhoso was left outside when the fighting began would very probably die\r\nin an extremely unpleasant fashion. The little hundred-and-fifty-pound\r\ncamel-guns posted at one corner of the square opened the ball as the\r\nsquare moved forward by its right to get possession of a knoll of\r\nrising ground. All had fought in this manner many times before, and\r\nthere was no novelty in the entertainment; always the same hot and\r\nstifling formation, the smell of dust and leather, the same boltlike\r\nrush of the enemy, the same pressure on the weakest side, the few\r\nminutes of hand-to-hand scuffle, and then the silence of the desert,\r\nbroken only by the yells of those whom their handful of cavalry\r\nattempted to pursue. They had become careless. The camel-guns spoke at\r\nintervals, and the square slouched forward amid the protesting of the\r\ncamels. Then came the attack of three thousand men who had not learned\r\nfrom books that it is impossible for troops in close order to attack\r\nagainst breech-loading fire.\r\n\r\nA few dropping shots heralded their approach, and a few horsemen led,\r\nbut the bulk of the force was naked humanity, mad with rage, and armed\r\nwith the spear and the sword. The instinct of the desert, where there\r\nis always much war, told them that the right flank of the square was\r\nthe weakest, for they swung clear of the front. The camel-guns shelled\r\nthem as they passed and opened for an instant lanes through their\r\nmidst, most like those quick-closing vistas in a Kentish hop-garden\r\nseen when the train races by at full speed; and the infantry fire, held\r\ntill the opportune moment, dropped them in close-packing hundreds. No\r\ncivilised troops in the world could have endured the hell through which\r\nthey came, the living leaping high to avoid the dying who clutched at\r\ntheir heels, the wounded cursing and staggering forward, till they\r\nfell\xe2\x80\x94a torrent black as the sliding water above a mill-dam\xe2\x80\x94full on the\r\nright flank of the square.\r\n\r\nThen the line of the dusty troops and the faint blue desert sky\r\noverhead went out in rolling smoke, and the little stones on the heated\r\nground and the tinder-dry clumps of scrub became matters of surpassing\r\ninterest, for men measured their agonised retreat and recovery by these\r\nthings, counting mechanically and hewing their way back to chosen\r\npebble and branch. There was no semblance of any concerted fighting.\r\nFor aught the men knew, the enemy might be attempting all four sides of\r\nthe square at once. Their business was to destroy what lay in front of\r\nthem, to bayonet in the back those who passed over them, and, dying, to\r\ndrag down the slayer till he could be knocked on the head by some\r\navenging gun-butt.\r\n\r\nDick waited with Torpenhow and a young doctor till the stress grew\r\nunendurable. It was hopeless to attend to the wounded till the attack\r\nwas repulsed, so the three moved forward gingerly towards the weakest\r\nside of the square. There was a rush from without, the short\r\n_hough-hough_ of the stabbing spears, and a man on a horse, followed by\r\nthirty or forty others, dashed through, yelling and hacking. The right\r\nflank of the square sucked in after them, and the other sides sent\r\nhelp. The wounded, who knew that they had but a few hours more to live,\r\ncaught at the enemy\xe2\x80\x99s feet and brought them down, or, staggering into a\r\ndiscarded rifle, fired blindly into the scuffle that raged in the\r\ncentre of the square.\r\n\r\nDick was conscious that somebody had cut him violently across his\r\nhelmet, that he had fired his revolver into a black, foam-flecked face\r\nwhich forthwith ceased to bear any resemblance to a face, and that\r\nTorpenhow had gone down under an Arab whom he had tried to \xe2\x80\x9ccollar\r\nlow,\xe2\x80\x9d and was turning over and over with his captive, feeling for the\r\nman\xe2\x80\x99s eyes. The doctor jabbed at a venture with a bayonet, and a\r\nhelmetless soldier fired over Dick\xe2\x80\x99s shoulder: the flying grains of\r\npowder stung his cheek. It was to Torpenhow that Dick turned by\r\ninstinct. The representative of the Central Southern Syndicate had\r\nshaken himself clear of his enemy, and rose, wiping his thumb on his\r\ntrousers. The Arab, both hands to his forehead, screamed aloud, then\r\nsnatched up his spear and rushed at Torpenhow, who was panting under\r\nshelter of Dick\xe2\x80\x99s revolver. Dick fired twice, and the man dropped\r\nlimply. His upturned face lacked one eye. The musketry-fire redoubled,\r\nbut cheers mingled with it. The rush had failed and the enemy were\r\nflying. If the heart of the square were shambles, the ground beyond was\r\na butcher\xe2\x80\x99s shop. Dick thrust his way forward between the maddened men.\r\nThe remnant of the enemy were retiring, as the few\xe2\x80\x94the very few\xe2\x80\x94English\r\ncavalry rode down the laggards.\r\n\r\nBeyond the lines of the dead, a broad blood-stained Arab spear cast\r\naside in the retreat lay across a stump of scrub, and beyond this again\r\nthe illimitable dark levels of the desert. The sun caught the steel and\r\nturned it into a red disc. Some one behind him was saying, \xe2\x80\x9cAh, get\r\naway, you brute!\xe2\x80\x9d Dick raised his revolver and pointed towards the\r\ndesert. His eye was held by the red splash in the distance, and the\r\nclamour about him seemed to die down to a very far-away whisper, like\r\nthe whisper of a level sea. There was the revolver and the red\r\nlight.... and the voice of some one scaring something away, exactly as\r\nhad fallen somewhere before,\xe2\x80\x94a darkness that stung. He fired at random,\r\nand the bullet went out across the desert as he muttered, \xe2\x80\x9cSpoilt my\r\naim. There aren\xe2\x80\x99t any more cartridges. We shall have to run home.\xe2\x80\x9d He\r\nput his hand to his head and brought it away covered with blood.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOld man, you\xe2\x80\x99re cut rather badly,\xe2\x80\x9d said Torpenhow. \xe2\x80\x9cI owe you\r\nsomething for this business. Thanks. Stand up! I say, you can\xe2\x80\x99t be ill\r\nhere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThroughout the night, when the troops were encamped by the whale-boats,\r\na black figure danced in the strong moonlight on the sand-bar and\r\nshouted that Khartoum the accursed one was dead,\xe2\x80\x94was dead,\xe2\x80\x94was\r\ndead,\xe2\x80\x94that two steamers were rock-staked on the Nile outside the city,\r\nand that of all their crews there remained not one; and Khartoum was\r\ndead,\xe2\x80\x94was dead,\xe2\x80\x94was dead!\r\n\r\nBut Torpenhow took no heed. He was watching Dick, who called aloud to\r\nthe restless Nile for Maisie,\xe2\x80\x94and again Maisie!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBehold a phenomenon,\xe2\x80\x9d said Torpenhow, rearranging the blanket. \xe2\x80\x9cHere\r\nis a man, presumably human, who mentions the name of one woman only.\r\nAnd I\xe2\x80\x99ve seen a good deal of delirium, too.\xe2\x80\x94Dick, here\xe2\x80\x99s some fizzy\r\ndrink.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you, Maisie,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER III\r\n\r\n\r\nSo he thinks he shall take to the sea again\r\n    For one more cruise with his buccaneers,\r\nTo singe the beard of the King of Spain,\r\nAnd capture another Dean of Jaen\r\n    And sell him in Algiers.\xe2\x80\x94_A Dutch Picture_.\xe2\x80\x94Longfellow\r\n\r\n\r\nThe Soudan campaign and Dick\xe2\x80\x99s broken head had been some months ended\r\nand mended, and the Central Southern Syndicate had paid Dick a certain\r\nsum on account for work done, which work they were careful to assure\r\nhim was not altogether up to their standard. Dick heaved the letter\r\ninto the Nile at Cairo, cashed the draft in the same town, and bade a\r\nwarm farewell to Torpenhow at the station.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am going to lie up for a while and rest,\xe2\x80\x9d said Torpenhow. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nknow where I shall live in London, but if God brings us to meet, we\r\nshall meet. Are you staying here on the off-chance of another row?\r\nThere will be none till the Southern Soudan is reoccupied by our\r\ntroops. Mark that. Good-bye; bless you; come back when your money\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nspent; and give me your address.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDick loitered in Cairo, Alexandria, Ismailia, and Port Said,\xe2\x80\x94especially\r\nPort Said. There is iniquity in many parts of the world, and vice in\r\nall, but the concentrated essence of all the iniquities and all the\r\nvices in all the continents finds itself at Port Said. And through the\r\nheart of that sand-bordered hell, where the mirage flickers day long\r\nabove the Bitter Lake, move, if you will only wait, most of the men and\r\nwomen you have known in this life. Dick established himself in quarters\r\nmore riotous than respectable. He spent his evenings on the quay, and\r\nboarded many ships, and saw very many friends,\xe2\x80\x94gracious Englishwomen\r\nwith whom he had talked not too wisely in the veranda of Shepherd\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nHotel, hurrying war correspondents, skippers of the contract\r\ntroop-ships employed in the campaign, army officers by the score, and\r\nothers of less reputable trades.\r\n\r\nHe had choice of all the races of the East and West for studies, and\r\nthe advantage of seeing his subjects under the influence of strong\r\nexcitement, at the gaming-tables, saloons, dancing-hells, and\r\nelsewhere. For recreation there was the straight vista of the Canal,\r\nthe blazing sands, the procession of shipping, and the white hospitals\r\nwhere the English soldiers lay. He strove to set down in black and\r\nwhite and colour all that Providence sent him, and when that supply was\r\nended sought about for fresh material. It was a fascinating employment,\r\nbut it ran away with his money, and he had drawn in advance the hundred\r\nand twenty pounds to which he was entitled yearly. \xe2\x80\x9cNow I shall have to\r\nwork and starve!\xe2\x80\x9d thought he, and was addressing himself to this new\r\nfate when a mysterious telegram arrived from Torpenhow in England,\r\nwhich said, \xe2\x80\x9cCome back, quick; you have caught on. Come.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA large smile overspread his face. \xe2\x80\x9cSo soon! that\xe2\x80\x99s a good hearing,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid he to himself. \xe2\x80\x9cThere will be an orgy to-night. I\xe2\x80\x99ll stand or fall\r\nby my luck. Faith, it\xe2\x80\x99s time it came!\xe2\x80\x9d He deposited half of his funds\r\nin the hands of his well-known friends Monsieur and Madame Binat, and\r\nordered himself a Zanzibar dance of the finest. Monsieur Binat was\r\nshaking with drink, but Madame smiles sympathetically\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cMonsieur needs a\r\nchair, of course, and of course Monsieur will sketch; Monsieur amuses\r\nhimself strangely.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBinat raised a blue-white face from a cot in the inner room. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nunderstand,\xe2\x80\x9d he quavered. \xe2\x80\x9cWe all know Monsieur. Monsieur is an artist,\r\nas I have been.\xe2\x80\x9d Dick nodded. \xe2\x80\x9cIn the end,\xe2\x80\x9d said Binat, with gravity,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMonsieur will descend alive into hell, as I have descended.\xe2\x80\x9d And he\r\nlaughed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must come to the dance, too,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick; \xe2\x80\x9cI shall want you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor my face? I knew it would be so. For my face? My God! and for my\r\ndegradation so tremendous! I will not. Take him away. He is a devil. Or\r\nat least do thou, Celeste, demand of him more.\xe2\x80\x9d The excellent Binat\r\nbegan to kick and scream.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll things are for sale in Port Said,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame. \xe2\x80\x9cIf my husband\r\ncomes it will be so much more. Eh, \xe2\x80\x9chow you call\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99alf a sovereign.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe money was paid, and the mad dance was held at night in a walled\r\ncourtyard at the back of Madame Binat\xe2\x80\x99s house. The lady herself, in\r\nfaded mauve silk always about to slide from her yellow shoulders,\r\nplayed the piano, and to the tin-pot music of a Western waltz the naked\r\nZanzibari girls danced furiously by the light of kerosene lamps. Binat\r\nsat upon a chair and stared with eyes that saw nothing, till the whirl\r\nof the dance and the clang of the rattling piano stole into the drink\r\nthat took the place of blood in his veins, and his face glistened. Dick\r\ntook him by the chin brutally and turned that face to the light. Madame\r\nBinat looked over her shoulder and smiled with many teeth. Dick leaned\r\nagainst the wall and sketched for an hour, till the kerosene lamps\r\nbegan to smell, and the girls threw themselves panting on the\r\nhard-beaten ground. Then he shut his book with a snap and moved away,\r\nBinat plucking feebly at his elbow. \xe2\x80\x9cShow me,\xe2\x80\x9d he whimpered. \xe2\x80\x9cI too was\r\nonce an artist, even I!\xe2\x80\x9d Dick showed him the rough sketch. \xe2\x80\x9cAm I that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe screamed. \xe2\x80\x9cWill you take that away with you and show all the world\r\nthat it is I,\xe2\x80\x94Binat?\xe2\x80\x9d He moaned and wept.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMonsieur has paid for all,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame. \xe2\x80\x9cTo the pleasure of seeing\r\nMonsieur again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe courtyard gate shut, and Dick hurried up the sandy street to the\r\nnearest gambling-hell, where he was well known. \xe2\x80\x9cIf the luck holds,\r\nit\xe2\x80\x99s an omen; if I lose, I must stay here.\xe2\x80\x9d He placed his money\r\npicturesquely about the board, hardly daring to look at what he did.\r\nThe luck held.\r\n\r\nThree turns of the wheel left him richer by twenty pounds, and he went\r\ndown to the shipping to make friends with the captain of a decayed\r\ncargo-steamer, who landed him in London with fewer pounds in his pocket\r\nthan he cared to think about.\r\n\r\nA thin gray fog hung over the city, and the streets were very cold; for\r\nsummer was in England.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a cheerful wilderness, and it hasn\xe2\x80\x99t the knack of altering much,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nDick thought, as he tramped from the Docks westward. \xe2\x80\x9cNow, what must I\r\ndo?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe packed houses gave no answer. Dick looked down the long lightless\r\nstreets and at the appalling rush of traffic. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, you rabbit-hutches!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid he, addressing a row of highly respectable semi-detached\r\nresidences. \xe2\x80\x9cDo you know what you\xe2\x80\x99ve got to do later on? You have to\r\nsupply me with men-servants and maid-servants,\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94here he smacked his\r\nlips,\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cand the peculiar treasure of kings. Meantime I\xe2\x80\x99ll find clothes\r\nand boots, and presently I will return and trample on you.\xe2\x80\x9d He stepped\r\nforward energetically; he saw that one of his shoes was burst at the\r\nside. As he stooped to make investigations, a man jostled him into the\r\ngutter. \xe2\x80\x9cAll right,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s another nick in the score. I\xe2\x80\x99ll jostle you later on.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGood clothes and boots are not cheap, and Dick left his last shop with\r\nthe certainty that he would be respectably arrayed for a time, but with\r\nonly fifty shillings in his pocket. He returned to streets by the\r\nDocks, and lodged himself in one room, where the sheets on the bed were\r\nalmost audibly marked in case of theft, and where nobody seemed to go\r\nto bed at all. When his clothes arrived he sought the Central Southern\r\nSyndicate for Torpenhow\xe2\x80\x99s address, and got it, with the intimation that\r\nthere was still some money waiting for him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow much?\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick, as one who habitually dealt in millions.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBetween thirty and forty pounds. If it would be any convenience to\r\nyou, of course we could let you have it at once; but we usually settle\r\naccounts monthly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I show that I want anything now, I\xe2\x80\x99m lost,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to himself.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll I need I\xe2\x80\x99ll take later on.\xe2\x80\x9d Then, aloud, \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s hardly worth while;\r\nand I\xe2\x80\x99m going to the country for a month, too. Wait till I come back,\r\nand I\xe2\x80\x99ll see about it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut we trust, Mr. Heldar, that you do not intend to sever your\r\nconnection with us?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDick\xe2\x80\x99s business in life was the study of faces, and he watched the\r\nspeaker keenly. \xe2\x80\x9cThat man means something,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll do no\r\nbusiness till I\xe2\x80\x99ve seen Torpenhow. There\xe2\x80\x99s a big deal coming.\xe2\x80\x9d So he\r\ndeparted, making no promises, to his one little room by the Docks. And\r\nthat day was the seventh of the month, and that month, he reckoned with\r\nawful distinctness, had thirty-one days in it!\r\n\r\nIt is not easy for a man of catholic tastes and healthy appetites to\r\nexist for twenty-four days on fifty shillings. Nor is it cheering to\r\nbegin the experiment alone in all the loneliness of London. Dick paid\r\nseven shillings a week for his lodging, which left him rather less than\r\na shilling a day for food and drink. Naturally, his first purchase was\r\nof the materials of his craft; he had been without them too long. Half\r\na day\xe2\x80\x99s investigations and comparison brought him to the conclusion\r\nthat sausages and mashed potatoes, twopence a plate, were the best\r\nfood. Now, sausages once or twice a week for breakfast are not\r\nunpleasant. As lunch, even, with mashed potatoes, they become\r\nmonotonous. At dinner they are impertinent. At the end of three days\r\nDick loathed sausages, and, going forth, pawned his watch to revel on\r\nsheep\xe2\x80\x99s head, which is not as cheap as it looks, owing to the bones and\r\nthe gravy. Then he returned to sausages and mashed potatoes. Then he\r\nconfined himself entirely to mashed potatoes for a day, and was unhappy\r\nbecause of pain in his inside. Then he pawned his waistcoat and his\r\ntie, and thought regretfully of money thrown away in times past. There\r\nare few things more edifying unto Art than the actual belly-pinch of\r\nhunger, and Dick in his few walks abroad,\xe2\x80\x94he did not care for exercise;\r\nit raised desires that could not be satisfied\xe2\x80\x94found himself dividing\r\nmankind into two classes,\xe2\x80\x94those who looked as if they might give him\r\nsomething to eat, and those who looked otherwise. \xe2\x80\x9cI never knew what I\r\nhad to learn about the human face before,\xe2\x80\x9d he thought; and, as a reward\r\nfor his humility, Providence caused a cab-driver at a sausage-shop\r\nwhere Dick fed that night to leave half eaten a great chunk of bread.\r\nDick took it,\xe2\x80\x94would have fought all the world for its possession,\xe2\x80\x94and\r\nit cheered him.\r\n\r\nThe month dragged through at last, and, nearly prancing with\r\nimpatience, he went to draw his money. Then he hastened to Torpenhow\xe2\x80\x99s\r\naddress and smelt the smell of cooking meats all along the corridors of\r\nthe chambers. Torpenhow was on the top floor, and Dick burst into his\r\nroom, to be received with a hug which nearly cracked his ribs, as\r\nTorpenhow dragged him to the light and spoke of twenty different things\r\nin the same breath.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you\xe2\x80\x99re looking tucked up,\xe2\x80\x9d he concluded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGot anything to eat?\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick, his eye roaming round the room.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall be having breakfast in a minute. What do you say to sausages?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, anything but sausages! Torp, I\xe2\x80\x99ve been starving on that accursed\r\nhorse-flesh for thirty days and thirty nights.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, what lunacy has been your latest?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDick spoke of the last few weeks with unbridled speech. Then he opened\r\nhis coat; there was no waistcoat below. \xe2\x80\x9cI ran it fine, awfully fine,\r\nbut I\xe2\x80\x99ve just scraped through.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou haven\xe2\x80\x99t much sense, but you\xe2\x80\x99ve got a backbone, anyhow. Eat, and\r\ntalk afterwards.\xe2\x80\x9d Dick fell upon eggs and bacon and gorged till he\r\ncould gorge no more. Torpenhow handed him a filled pipe, and he smoked\r\nas men smoke who for three weeks have been deprived of good tobacco.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOuf!\xe2\x80\x9d said he. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s heavenly! Well?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy in the world didn\xe2\x80\x99t you come to me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCouldn\xe2\x80\x99t; I owe you too much already, old man.
