It\xe2\x80\x99s over and done with now, and none\r\nof the syndicate know how hard up I was. Fire away. What\xe2\x80\x99s the exact\r\nstate of affairs as regards myself?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou had my wire? You\xe2\x80\x99ve caught on here. People like your work\r\nimmensely. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know why, but they do. They say you have a fresh\r\ntouch and a new way of drawing things. And, because they\xe2\x80\x99re chiefly\r\nhome-bred English, they say you have insight. You\xe2\x80\x99re wanted by half a\r\ndozen papers; you\xe2\x80\x99re wanted to illustrate books.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDick grunted scornfully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re wanted to work up your smaller sketches and sell them to the\r\ndealers. They seem to think the money sunk in you is a good investment.\r\n\r\nGood Lord! who can account for the fathomless folly of the public?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99re a remarkably sensible people.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are subject to fits, if that\xe2\x80\x99s what you mean; and you happen to\r\nbe the object of the latest fit among those who are interested in what\r\nthey call Art. Just now you\xe2\x80\x99re a fashion, a phenomenon, or whatever you\r\nplease. I appeared to be the only person who knew anything about you\r\nhere, and I have been showing the most useful men a few of the sketches\r\nyou gave me from time to time. Those coming after your work on the\r\nCentral Southern Syndicate appear to have done your business. You\xe2\x80\x99re in\r\nluck.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHuh! call it luck! Do call it luck, when a man has been kicking about\r\nthe world like a dog, waiting for it to come! I\xe2\x80\x99ll luck \xe2\x80\x99em later on. I\r\nwant a place to work first.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome here,\xe2\x80\x9d said Torpenhow, crossing the landing. \xe2\x80\x9cThis place is a big\r\nbox room really, but it will do for you. There\xe2\x80\x99s your skylight, or your\r\nnorth light, or whatever window you call it, and plenty of room to\r\nthrash about in, and a bedroom beyond. What more do you need?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood enough,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick, looking round the large room that took up a\r\nthird of a top story in the rickety chambers overlooking the Thames. A\r\npale yellow sun shone through the skylight and showed the much dirt of\r\nthe place. Three steps led from the door to the landing, and three more\r\nto Torpenhow\xe2\x80\x99s room. The well of the staircase disappeared into\r\ndarkness, pricked by tiny gas-jets, and there were sounds of men\r\ntalking and doors slamming seven flights below, in the warm gloom.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo they give you a free hand here?\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick, cautiously. He was\r\nIshmael enough to know the value of liberty.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnything you like; latch-keys and license unlimited. We are permanent\r\ntenants for the most part here. \xe2\x80\x99Tisn\xe2\x80\x99t a place I would recommend for a\r\nYoung Men\xe2\x80\x99s Christian Association, but it will serve. I took these\r\nrooms for you when I wired.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re a great deal too kind, old man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou didn\xe2\x80\x99t suppose you were going away from me, did you?\xe2\x80\x9d Torpenhow\r\nput his hand on Dick\xe2\x80\x99s shoulder, and the two walked up and down the\r\nroom, henceforward to be called the studio, in sweet and silent\r\ncommunion. They heard rapping at Torpenhow\xe2\x80\x99s door. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s some ruffian\r\ncome up for a drink,\xe2\x80\x9d said Torpenhow; and he raised his voice cheerily.\r\nThere entered no one more ruffianly than a portly middle-aged gentleman\r\nin a satin-faced frockcoat. His lips were parted and pale, and there\r\nwere deep pouches under the eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWeak heart,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick to himself, and, as he shook hands, \xe2\x80\x9cvery weak\r\nheart. His pulse is shaking his fingers.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe man introduced himself as the head of the Central Southern\r\nSyndicate and \xe2\x80\x9cone of the most ardent admirers of your work, Mr.\r\nHeldar. I assure you, in the name of the syndicate, that we are\r\nimmensely indebted to you; and I trust, Mr. Heldar, you won\xe2\x80\x99t forget\r\nthat we were largely instrumental in bringing you before the public.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nHe panted because of the seven flights of stairs.\r\n\r\nDick glanced at Torpenhow, whose left eyelid lay for a moment dead on\r\nhis cheek.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shan\xe2\x80\x99t forget,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick, every instinct of defence roused in him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve paid me so well that I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t, you know. By the way, when I\r\nam settled in this place I should like to send and get my sketches.\r\nThere must be nearly a hundred and fifty of them with you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is er\xe2\x80\x94is what I came to speak about. I fear we can\xe2\x80\x99t allow it\r\nexactly, Mr. Heldar. In the absence of any specified agreement, the\r\nsketches are our property, of course.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you mean to say that you are going to keep them?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; and we hope to have your help, on your own terms, Mr. Heldar, to\r\nassist us in arranging a little exhibition, which, backed by our name\r\nand the influence we naturally command among the press, should be of\r\nmaterial service to you. Sketches such as yours\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBelong to me. You engaged me by wire, you paid me the lowest rates you\r\ndared. You can\xe2\x80\x99t mean to keep them! Good God alive, man, they\xe2\x80\x99re all\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ve got in the world!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTorpenhow watched Dick\xe2\x80\x99s face and whistled.\r\n\r\nDick walked up and down, thinking. He saw the whole of his little stock\r\nin trade, the first weapon of his equipment, annexed at the outset of\r\nhis campaign by an elderly gentleman whose name Dick had not caught\r\naright, who said that he represented a syndicate, which was a thing for\r\nwhich Dick had not the least reverence. The injustice of the\r\nproceedings did not much move him; he had seen the strong hand prevail\r\ntoo often in other places to be squeamish over the moral aspects of\r\nright and wrong.\r\n\r\nBut he ardently desired the blood of the gentleman in the frockcoat,\r\nand when he spoke again, and when he spoke again it was with a strained\r\nsweetness that Torpenhow knew well for the beginning of strife.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cForgive me, sir, but you have no\xe2\x80\x94no younger man who can arrange this\r\nbusiness with me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI speak for the syndicate. I see no reason for a third party to\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will in a minute. Be good enough to give back my sketches.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe man stared blankly at Dick, and then at Torpenhow, who was leaning\r\nagainst the wall. He was not used to ex-employees who ordered him to be\r\ngood enough to do things.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, it is rather a cold-blooded steal,\xe2\x80\x9d said Torpenhow, critically;\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cbut I\xe2\x80\x99m afraid, I am very much afraid, you\xe2\x80\x99ve struck the wrong man. Be\r\ncareful, Dick; remember, this isn\xe2\x80\x99t the Soudan.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cConsidering what services the syndicate have done you in putting your\r\nname before the world\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis was not a fortunate remark; it reminded Dick of certain vagrant\r\nyears lived out in loneliness and strife and unsatisfied desires. The\r\nmemory did not contrast well with the prosperous gentleman who proposed\r\nto enjoy the fruit of those years.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know quite what to do with you,\xe2\x80\x9d began Dick, meditatively. \xe2\x80\x9cOf\r\ncourse you\xe2\x80\x99re a thief, and you ought to be half killed, but in your\r\ncase you\xe2\x80\x99d probably die. I don\xe2\x80\x99t want you dead on this floor, and,\r\nbesides, it\xe2\x80\x99s unlucky just as one\xe2\x80\x99s moving in. Don\xe2\x80\x99t hit, sir; you\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nonly excite yourself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe put one hand on the man\xe2\x80\x99s forearm and ran the other down the plump\r\nbody beneath the coat. \xe2\x80\x9cMy goodness!\xe2\x80\x9d said he to Torpenhow, \xe2\x80\x9cand this\r\ngray oaf dares to be a thief! I have seen an Esneh camel-driver have\r\nthe black hide taken off his body in strips for stealing half a pound\r\nof wet dates, and _he_ was as tough as whipcord. This things\xe2\x80\x99 soft all\r\nover\xe2\x80\x94like a woman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere are few things more poignantly humiliating than being handled by\r\na man who does not intend to strike. The head of the syndicate began to\r\nbreathe heavily. Dick walked round him, pawing him, as a cat paws a\r\nsoft hearth-rug. Then he traced with his forefinger the leaden pouches\r\nunderneath the eyes, and shook his head. \xe2\x80\x9cYou were going to steal my\r\nthings,\xe2\x80\x94mine, mine, mine!\xe2\x80\x94you, who don\xe2\x80\x99t know when you may die. Write a\r\nnote to your office,\xe2\x80\x94you say you\xe2\x80\x99re the head of it,\xe2\x80\x94and order them to\r\ngive Torpenhow my sketches,\xe2\x80\x94every one of them. Wait a minute: your\r\nhand\xe2\x80\x99s shaking. Now!\xe2\x80\x9d He thrust a pocket-book before him. The note was\r\nwritten. Torpenhow took it and departed without a word, while Dick\r\nwalked round and round the spellbound captive, giving him such advice\r\nas he conceived best for the welfare of his soul. When Torpenhow\r\nreturned with a gigantic portfolio, he heard Dick say, almost\r\nsoothingly, \xe2\x80\x9cNow, I hope this will be a lesson to you; and if you worry\r\nme when I have settled down to work with any nonsense about actions for\r\nassault, believe me, I\xe2\x80\x99ll catch you and manhandle you, and you\xe2\x80\x99ll die.\r\nYou haven\xe2\x80\x99t very long to live, anyhow. Go! _Imshi, Vootsak_,\xe2\x80\x94get out!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nThe man departed, staggering and dazed. Dick drew a long breath: \xe2\x80\x9cPhew!\r\nwhat a lawless lot these people are! The first thing a poor orphan\r\nmeets is gang robbery, organised burglary! Think of the hideous\r\nblackness of that man\xe2\x80\x99s mind! Are my sketches all right, Torp?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; one hundred and forty-seven of them. Well, I _must_ say, Dick,\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ve begun well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe was interfering with me. It only meant a few pounds to him, but it\r\nwas everything to me. I don\xe2\x80\x99t think he\xe2\x80\x99ll bring an action. I gave him\r\nsome medical advice gratis about the state of his body. It was cheap at\r\nthe little flurry it cost him. Now, let\xe2\x80\x99s look at my things.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTwo minutes later Dick had thrown himself down on the floor and was\r\ndeep in the portfolio, chuckling lovingly as he turned the drawings\r\nover and thought of the price at which they had been bought.\r\n\r\nThe afternoon was well advanced when Torpenhow came to the door and saw\r\nDick dancing a wild saraband under the skylight.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI builded better than I knew, Torp,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, without stopping the\r\ndance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99re good! They\xe2\x80\x99re damned good! They\xe2\x80\x99ll go like flame! I shall have\r\nan exhibition of them on my own brazen hook. And that man would have\r\ncheated me out of it! Do you know that I\xe2\x80\x99m sorry now that I didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nactually hit him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo out,\xe2\x80\x9d said Torpenhow,\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cgo out and pray to be delivered from the sin\r\nof arrogance, which you never will be. Bring your things up from\r\nwhatever place you\xe2\x80\x99re staying in, and we\xe2\x80\x99ll try to make this barn a\r\nlittle more shipshape.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd then\xe2\x80\x94oh, then,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick, still capering, \xe2\x80\x9cwe will spoil the\r\nEgyptians!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER IV\r\n\r\n\r\nThe wolf-cub at even lay hid in the corn,\r\n    When the smoke of the cooking hung gray:\r\nHe knew where the doe made a couch for her fawn,\r\n    And he looked to his strength for his prey.\r\n    But the moon swept the smoke-wreaths away.\r\nAnd he turned from his meal in the villager\xe2\x80\x99s close,\r\nAnd he bayed to the moon as she rose.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x94_In Seonee_.\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, and how does success taste?\xe2\x80\x9d said Torpenhow, some three months\r\nlater. He had just returned to chambers after a holiday in the country.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick, as he sat licking his lips before the easel in the\r\nstudio.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI want more,\xe2\x80\x94heaps more. The lean years have passed, and I approve of\r\nthese fat ones.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBe careful, old man. That way lies bad work.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTorpenhow was sprawling in a long chair with a small fox-terrier asleep\r\non his chest, while Dick was preparing a canvas. A dais, a background,\r\nand a lay-figure were the only fixed objects in the place. They rose\r\nfrom a wreck of oddments that began with felt-covered water-bottles,\r\nbelts, and regimental badges, and ended with a small bale of\r\nsecond-hand uniforms and a stand of mixed arms. The mark of muddy feet\r\non the dais showed that a military model had just gone away. The watery\r\nautumn sunlight was falling, and shadows sat in the corners of the\r\nstudio.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick, deliberately, \xe2\x80\x9cI like the power; I like the fun; I\r\nlike the fuss; and above all I like the money. I almost like the people\r\nwho make the fuss and pay the money. Almost. But they\xe2\x80\x99re a queer\r\ngang,\xe2\x80\x94an amazingly queer gang!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey have been good enough to you, at any rate. That tin-pot\r\nexhibition of your sketches must have paid. Did you see that the papers\r\ncalled it the \xe2\x80\x98Wild Work Show\xe2\x80\x99?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind. I sold every shred of canvas I wanted to; and, on my word,\r\nI believe it was because they believed I was a self-taught flagstone\r\nartist.\r\n\r\nI should have got better prices if I worked my things on wool or\r\nscratched them on camel-bone instead of using mere black and white and\r\ncolour. Verily, they are a queer gang, these people. Limited isn\xe2\x80\x99t the\r\nword to describe \xe2\x80\x99em. I met a fellow the other day who told me that it\r\nwas impossible that shadows on white sand should be\r\nblue,\xe2\x80\x94ultramarine,\xe2\x80\x94as they are. I found out, later, that the man had\r\nbeen as far as Brighton beach; but he knew all about Art, confound him.\r\nHe gave me a lecture on it, and recommended me to go to school to learn\r\ntechnique. I wonder what old Kami would have said to that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen were you under Kami, man of extraordinary beginnings?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI studied with him for two years in Paris. He taught by personal\r\nmagnetism. All he ever said was, \xe2\x80\x98_Continuez, mes enfants_,\xe2\x80\x99 and you\r\nhad to make the best you could of that. He had a divine touch, and he\r\nknew something about colour. Kami used to dream colour; I swear he\r\ncould never have seen the genuine article; but he evolved it; and it\r\nwas good.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRecollect some of those views in the Soudan?\xe2\x80\x9d said Torpenhow, with a\r\nprovoking drawl.\r\n\r\nDick squirmed in his place. \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t! It makes me want to get out there\r\nagain. What colour that was! Opal and umber and amber and claret and\r\nbrick-red and sulphur\xe2\x80\x94cockatoo-crest\xe2\x80\x94sulphur\xe2\x80\x94against brown, with a\r\nnigger-black rock sticking up in the middle of it all, and a decorative\r\nfrieze of camels festooning in front of a pure pale turquoise sky.\xe2\x80\x9d He\r\nbegan to walk up and down. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd yet, you know, if you try to give these\r\npeople the thing as God gave it, keyed down to their comprehension and\r\naccording to the powers He has given you\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cModest man! Go on.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHalf a dozen epicene young pagans who haven\xe2\x80\x99t even been to Algiers\r\nwill tell you, first, that your notion is borrowed, and, secondly, that\r\nit isn\xe2\x80\x99t Art.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99This comes of my leaving town for a month. Dickie, you\xe2\x80\x99ve been\r\npromenading among the toy-shops and hearing people talk.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI couldn\xe2\x80\x99t help it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick, penitently. \xe2\x80\x9cYou weren\xe2\x80\x99t here, and it\r\nwas lonely these long evenings. A man can\xe2\x80\x99t work for ever.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA man might have gone to a pub, and got decently drunk.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish I had; but I forgathered with some men of sorts. They said they\r\nwere artists, and I knew some of them could draw,\xe2\x80\x94but they wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ndraw. They gave me tea,\xe2\x80\x94tea at five in the afternoon!\xe2\x80\x94and talked about\r\nArt and the state of their souls. As if their souls mattered. I\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\nheard more about Art and seen less of her in the last six months than\r\nin the whole of my life. Do you remember Cassavetti, who worked for\r\nsome continental syndicate, out with the desert column? He was a\r\nregular Christmas-tree of contraptions when he took the field in full\r\nfig, with his water-bottle, lanyard, revolver, writing-case, housewife,\r\ngig-lamps, and the Lord knows what all. He used to fiddle about with\r\n\xe2\x80\x99em and show us how they worked; but he never seemed to do much except\r\nfudge his reports from the Nilghai. See?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear old Nilghai! He\xe2\x80\x99s in town, fatter than ever. He ought to be up\r\nhere this evening. I see the comparison perfectly. You should have kept\r\nclear of all that man-millinery. Serves you right; and I hope it will\r\nunsettle your mind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt won\xe2\x80\x99t. It has taught me what Art\xe2\x80\x94holy sacred Art\xe2\x80\x94means.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve learnt something while I\xe2\x80\x99ve been away. What is Art?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGive \xe2\x80\x99em what they know, and when you\xe2\x80\x99ve done it once do it again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDick dragged forward a canvas laid face to the wall. \xe2\x80\x9cHere\xe2\x80\x99s a sample\r\nof real Art. It\xe2\x80\x99s going to be a facsimile reproduction for a weekly. I\r\ncalled it \xe2\x80\x9cHis Last Shot.\xe2\x80\x9d It\xe2\x80\x99s worked up from the little water-colour\r\nI made outside El Maghrib. Perhaps he has been going\r\nout of evenings. I must look to this.\xe2\x80\x9d He rang for the bald-headed old\r\nhousekeeper, whom nothing could astonish or annoy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBeeton, did Mr. Heldar dine out at all while I was out of town?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever laid \xe2\x80\x99is dress-clothes out once, sir, all the time. He laughed aloud as he remembered\r\nthe day when he had decked Amomma\xe2\x80\x99s horns with the ham-frills, and\r\nMaisie, white with rage, had cuffed him. How long those four years\r\nseemed in review, and how closely Maisie was connected with every hour\r\nof them! Storm across the sea, and Maisie in a gray dress on the beach,\r\nsweeping her drenched hair out of her eyes and laughing at the homeward\r\nrace of the fishing-smacks; hot sunshine on the mud-flats, and Maisie\r\nsniffing scornfully, with her chin in the air; Maisie flying before the\r\nwind that threshed the foreshore and drove the sand like small shot\r\nabout her ears; Maisie, very composed and independent, telling lies to\r\nMrs. Jennett while Dick supported her with coarser perjuries; Maisie\r\npicking her way delicately from stone to stone, a pistol in her hand\r\nand her teeth firm-set; and Maisie in a gray dress sitting on the grass\r\nbetween the mouth of a cannon and a nodding yellow sea-poppy. The fog was gathering,\r\nand it stifled the roar of the traffic of London beyond the railings.\r\nDick brought all his painfully acquired knowledge of faces to bear on\r\nthe eyes, mouth, and chin underneath the black velvet toque.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s the same Maisie, and it\xe2\x80\x99s the same me,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ve both nice\r\nlittle wills of our own, and one or other of us has to be broken. Therefore, particularly and perpetually,\r\ndamn all varieties of Art. It did me a good turn once, and now it\xe2\x80\x99s in\r\nmy way. I\xe2\x80\x99ll go home and do some Art.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHalf-way to the studio, Dick was smitten with a terrible thought. The\r\nfigure of a solitary woman in the fog suggested it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s all alone in London, with a red-haired impressionist girl, who\r\nprobably has the digestion of an ostrich. This is a good love, and, since it allows, and even encourages,\r\nstrife, recrimination, and brutal sincerity, does not die, but grows,\r\nand is proof against any absence and evil conduct.\r\n\r\nDick was silent after he handed Torpenhow the filled pipe of council.\r\nHe thought of Maisie and her possible needs. It was a new thing to\r\nthink of anybody but Torpenhow, who could think for himself. Here at\r\nlast was an outlet for that cash balance. He could adorn Maisie\r\nbarbarically with jewelry,\xe2\x80\x94a thick gold necklace round that little\r\nneck, bracelets upon the rounded arms, and rings of price upon her\r\nhands,\xe2\x80\x94the cool, temperate, ringless hands that he had taken between\r\nhis own. What business have\r\nyou with luxuries of that kind?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt came of itself. Then Torpenhow,\r\ninsinuatingly\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cDick, is it a woman?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBe hanged if it\xe2\x80\x99s anything remotely resembling a woman; and if you\r\nbegin to talk like that, I\xe2\x80\x99ll hire a red-brick studio with white paint\r\ntrimmings, and begonias and petunias and blue Hungarias to play among\r\nthree-and-sixpenny pot-palms, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll mount all my pics in aniline-dye\r\nplush plasters, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll invite every woman who maunders over what her\r\nguide-books tell her is Art, and you shall receive \xe2\x80\x99em, Torp,\xe2\x80\x94in a\r\nsnuff-brown velvet coat with yellow trousers and an orange tie. You want hammering.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDick shivered. \xe2\x80\x9cAll right,\xe2\x80\x9d said he. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen this island is\r\ndisintegrated, it will call for you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall come round the corner and help to disintegrate it some more.\r\n\r\nWe\xe2\x80\x99re talking nonsense. Come along to a theatre.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VI\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you may lead a thousand men,\r\n    Nor ever draw the rein,\r\nBut ere ye lead the Faery Queen\r\n    \xe2\x80\x99Twill burst your heart in twain.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe has slipped his foot from the stirrup-bar,\r\n    The bridle from his hand,\r\nAnd he is bound by hand and foot\r\n    To the Queen o\xe2\x80\x99 Faery-land.\r\n\r\n_Sir Hoggie and the Fairies_.\r\n\r\n\r\nSome weeks later, on a very foggy Sunday, Dick was returning across the\r\nPark to his studio. \xe2\x80\x9cThis,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cis evidently the thrashing that\r\nTorp meant. It hurts more than I expected; but the Queen can do no\r\nwrong; and she certainly has some notion of drawing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe had just finished a Sunday visit to Maisie,\xe2\x80\x94always under the green\r\neyes of the red-haired impressionist girl, whom he learned to hate at\r\nsight,\xe2\x80\x94and was tingling with a keen sense of shame. Sunday after\r\nSunday, putting on his best clothes, he had walked over to the untidy\r\nhouse north of the Park, first to see Maisie\xe2\x80\x99s pictures, and then to\r\ncriticise and advise upon them as he realised that they were\r\nproductions on which advice would not be wasted. Sunday after Sunday,\r\nand his love grew with each visit, he had been compelled to cram his\r\nheart back from between his lips when it prompted him to kiss Maisie\r\nseveral times and very much indeed. Sunday after Sunday, the head above\r\nthe heart had warned him that Maisie was not yet attainable, and that\r\nit would be better to talk as connectedly as possible upon the\r\nmysteries of the craft that was all in all to her. Therefore it was his\r\nfate to endure weekly torture in the studio built out over the clammy\r\nback garden of a frail stuffy little villa where nothing was ever in\r\nits right place and nobody every called,\xe2\x80\x94to endure and to watch Maisie\r\nmoving to and fro with the teacups. He abhorred tea, but, since it gave\r\nhim a little longer time in her presence, he drank it devoutly, and the\r\nred-haired girl sat in an untidy heap and eyed him without speaking.\r\nShe was always watching him.\r\n\r\nOnce, and only once, when she had left the studio, Maisie showed him an\r\nalbum that held a few poor cuttings from provincial papers,\xe2\x80\x94the\r\nbriefest of hurried notes on some of her pictures sent to outlying\r\nexhibitions. Dick stooped and kissed the paint-smudged thumb on the\r\nopen page. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, my love, my love,\xe2\x80\x9d he muttered, \xe2\x80\x9cdo you value these\r\nthings? Chuck \xe2\x80\x99em into the waste-paper basket!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot till I get something better,\xe2\x80\x9d said Maisie, shutting the book.\r\n\r\nThen Dick, moved by no respect for his public and a very deep regard\r\nfor the maiden, did deliberately propose, in order to secure more of\r\nthese coveted cuttings, that he should paint a picture which Maisie\r\nshould sign.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s childish,\xe2\x80\x9d said Maisie, \xe2\x80\x9cand I didn\xe2\x80\x99t think it of you. It must\r\nbe my work. Mine,\xe2\x80\x94mine,\xe2\x80\x94mine!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo and design decorative medallions for rich brewers\xe2\x80\x99 houses. You are\r\nthoroughly good at that.\xe2\x80\x9d Dick was sick and savage.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBetter things than medallions, Dick,\xe2\x80\x9d was the answer, in tones that\r\nrecalled a gray-eyed atom\xe2\x80\x99s fearless speech to Mrs. Jennett. Dick would\r\nhave abased himself utterly, but that other girl trailed in.\r\n\r\nNext Sunday he laid at Maisie\xe2\x80\x99s feet small gifts of pencils that could\r\nalmost draw of themselves and colours in whose permanence he believed,\r\nand he was ostentatiously attentive to the work in hand. It demanded,\r\namong other things, an exposition of the faith that was in him.\r\n\r\nTorpenhow\xe2\x80\x99s hair would have stood on end had he heard the fluency with\r\nwhich Dick preached his own gospel of Art.\r\n\r\nA month before, Dick would have been equally astonished; but it was\r\nMaisie\xe2\x80\x99s will and pleasure, and he dragged his words together to make\r\nplain to her comprehension all that had been hidden to himself of the\r\nwhys and wherefores of work. There is not the least difficulty in doing\r\na thing if you only know how to do it; the trouble is to explain your\r\nmethod.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI could put this right if I had a brush in my hand,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick,\r\ndespairingly, over the modelling of a chin that Maisie complained would\r\nnot \xe2\x80\x9clook flesh,\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94it was the same chin that she had scraped out with\r\nthe palette knife,\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cbut I find it almost impossible to teach you.\r\nThere\xe2\x80\x99s a queer grim, Dutch touch about your painting that I like; but\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ve a notion that you\xe2\x80\x99re weak in drawing. You foreshorten as though\r\nyou never used the model, and you\xe2\x80\x99ve caught Kami\xe2\x80\x99s pasty way of dealing\r\nwith flesh in shadow. Then, again, though you don\xe2\x80\x99t know it yourself,\r\nyou shirk hard work. Suppose you spend some of your time on line alone.\r\nLine doesn\xe2\x80\x99t allow of shirking. Oils do, and three square inches of\r\nflashy, tricky stuff in the corner of a pic sometimes carry a bad thing\r\noff,\xe2\x80\x94as I know. That\xe2\x80\x99s immoral. Do line-work for a little while, and\r\nthen I can tell more about your powers, as old Kami used to say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMaisie protested; she did not care for the pure line.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick. \xe2\x80\x9cYou want to do your fancy heads with a bunch of\r\nflowers at the base of the neck to hide bad modelling.\xe2\x80\x9d The red-haired\r\ngirl laughed a little. \xe2\x80\x9cYou want to do landscapes with cattle knee-deep\r\nin grass to hide bad drawing. You want to do a great deal more than you\r\ncan do. You have sense of colour, but you want form. Colour\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\ngift,\xe2\x80\x94put it aside and think no more about it,\xe2\x80\x94but form you can be\r\ndrilled into.\r\n\r\nNow, all your fancy heads\xe2\x80\x94and some of them are very good\xe2\x80\x94will keep you\r\nexactly where you are. With line you must go forward or backward, and\r\nit will show up all your weaknesses.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut other people\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d began Maisie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou mustn\xe2\x80\x99t mind what other people do. If their souls were your soul,\r\nit would be different. You stand and fall by your own work, remember,\r\nand it\xe2\x80\x99s waste of time to think of any one else in this battle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDick paused, and the longing that had been so resolutely put away came\r\nback into his eyes. He looked at Maisie, and the look asked as plainly\r\nas words, Was it not time to leave all this barren wilderness of canvas\r\nand counsel and join hands with Life and Love?\r\n\r\nMaisie assented to the new programme of schooling so adorably that Dick\r\ncould hardly restrain himself from picking her up then and there and\r\ncarrying her off to the nearest registrar\xe2\x80\x99s office. It was the implicit\r\nobedience to the spoken word and the blank indifference to the unspoken\r\ndesire that baffled and buffeted his soul. He held authority in that\r\nhouse,\xe2\x80\x94authority limited, indeed, to one-half of one afternoon in\r\nseven, but very real while it lasted. Maisie had learned to appeal to\r\nhim on many subjects, from the proper packing of pictures to the\r\ncondition of a smoky chimney. The red-haired girl never consulted him\r\nabout anything.\r\n\r\nOn the other hand, she accepted his appearances without protest, and\r\nwatched him always. He discovered that the meals of the establishment\r\nwere irregular and fragmentary. They depended chiefly on tea, pickles,\r\nand biscuit, as he had suspected from the beginning. The girls were\r\nsupposed to market week and week about, but they lived, with the help\r\nof a charwoman, as casually as the young ravens. Maisie spent most of\r\nher income on models, and the other girl revelled in apparatus as\r\nrefined as her work was rough. Armed with knowledge, dear-bought from\r\nthe Docks, Dick warned Maisie that the end of semi-starvation meant the\r\ncrippling of power to work, which was considerably worse than death.\r\n\r\nMaisie took the warning, and gave more thought to what she ate and\r\ndrank. When his trouble returned upon him, as it generally did in the\r\nlong winter twilights, the remembrance of that little act of domestic\r\nauthority and his coercion with a hearth-brush of the smoky\r\ndrawing-room chimney stung Dick like a whip-lash.\r\n\r\nHe conceived that this memory would be the extreme of his sufferings,\r\ntill one Sunday, the red-haired girl announced that she would make a\r\nstudy of Dick\xe2\x80\x99s head, and that he would be good enough to sit still,\r\nand\xe2\x80\x94quite as an afterthought\xe2\x80\x94look at Maisie. He sat, because he could\r\nnot well refuse, and for the space of half an hour he reflected on all\r\nthe people in the past whom he had laid open for the purposes of his\r\nown craft. He remembered Binat most distinctly,\xe2\x80\x94that Binat who had once\r\nbeen an artist and talked about degradation.\r\n\r\nIt was the merest monochrome roughing in of a head, but it presented\r\nthe dumb waiting, the longing, and, above all, the hopeless enslavement\r\nof the man, in a spirit of bitter mockery.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll buy it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick, promptly, \xe2\x80\x9cat your own price.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy price is too high, but I dare say you\xe2\x80\x99ll be as grateful if\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d The\r\nwet sketch, fluttered from the girl\xe2\x80\x99s hand and fell into the ashes of\r\nthe studio stove. The\r\nwinter breeze came in from the sea and sang about their ears.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMaisie,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick, \xe2\x80\x9cyour nose is getting a crude Prussian blue at the\r\ntip.\r\n\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ll race you as far as you please for as much as you please.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe looked round cautiously, and with a laugh set off, swiftly as the\r\nulster allowed, till she was out of breath.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe used to run miles,\xe2\x80\x9d she panted. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s absurd that we can\xe2\x80\x99t run\r\nnow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOld age, dear. I ought to know, because those shrieks of yours were\r\nmeant to call up Mrs. Jennett with a cane and\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDick, I never got you a beating on purpose in my life.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, of course you never did. The Nilghai rolled in at mid-day for chess\r\nand polite conversation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s worse than anything I imagined,\xe2\x80\x9d said Torpenhow.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, the everlasting Dick, I suppose! You fuss over him like a hen with\r\none chick. Let him run riot if he thinks it\xe2\x80\x99ll amuse him. You can whip\r\na young pup off feather, but you can\xe2\x80\x99t whip a young man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt isn\xe2\x80\x99t a woman. It\xe2\x80\x99s one woman; and it\xe2\x80\x99s a girl.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere\xe2\x80\x99s your proof?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe got up and went out at eight this morning,\xe2\x80\x94got up in the middle of\r\nthe night, by Jove! a thing he never does except when he\xe2\x80\x99s on service.\r\n\r\nEven then, remember, we had to kick him out of his blankets before the\r\nfight began at El-Maghrib. It\xe2\x80\x99s disgusting.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt looks odd; but maybe he\xe2\x80\x99s decided to buy a horse at last. He might\r\nget up for that, mightn\xe2\x80\x99t he?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBuy a blazing wheelbarrow! He\xe2\x80\x99d have told us if there was a horse in\r\nthe wind. It\xe2\x80\x99s a girl.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t be certain. Perhaps it\xe2\x80\x99s only a married woman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDick has some sense of humour, if you haven\xe2\x80\x99t. Who gets up in the gray\r\ndawn to call on another man\xe2\x80\x99s wife? It\xe2\x80\x99s a girl.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet it be a girl, then. She may teach him that there\xe2\x80\x99s somebody else\r\nin the world besides himself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99ll spoil his hand. No! ho! I\xe2\x80\x99d give something to see Dick \xe2\x80\x9cgo wooing with the\r\nboys.\xe2\x80\x9d Don\xe2\x80\x99t worry about it. These things be with Allah, and we can\r\nonly look on. Get the chessmen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe red-haired girl was lying down in her own room, staring at the\r\nceiling. The footsteps of people on the pavement sounded, as they grew\r\nindistinct in the distance, like a many-times-repeated kiss that was\r\nall one long kiss. Her hands were by her side, and they opened and shut\r\nsavagely from time to time.\r\n\r\nThe charwoman in charge of the scrubbing of the studio knocked at her\r\ndoor: \xe2\x80\x9cBeg y\xe2\x80\x99 pardon, miss, but in cleanin\xe2\x80\x99 of a floor there\xe2\x80\x99s two, not\r\nto say three, kind of soap, which is yaller, an\xe2\x80\x99 mottled, an\xe2\x80\x99\r\ndisinfectink.\r\n\r\nNow, jist before I took my pail into the passage I though it would be\r\npre\xe2\x80\x99aps jest as well if I was to come up \xe2\x80\x99ere an\xe2\x80\x99 ask you what sort of\r\nsoap you was wishful that I should use on them boards. The yaller soap,\r\nmiss\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was nothing in the speech to have caused the paroxysm of fury\r\nthat drove the red-haired girl into the middle of the room, almost\r\nshouting\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you suppose _I_ care what you use? Any kind will do!\xe2\x80\x94_any_ kind!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe woman fled, and the red-haired girl looked at her own reflection in\r\nthe glass for an instant and covered her face with her hands. It was as\r\nthough she had shouted some shameless secret aloud.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VII\r\n\r\n\r\nRoses red and roses white\r\nPlucked I for my love\xe2\x80\x99s delight.\r\nShe would none of all my posies,\xe2\x80\x94\r\nBade me gather her blue roses.\r\n\r\nHalf the world I wandered through,\r\nSeeking where such flowers grew;\r\nHalf the world unto my quest\r\nAnswered but with laugh and jest.\r\n\r\nIt may be beyond the grave\r\nShe shall find what she would have.\r\nMine was but an idle quest,\xe2\x80\x94\r\nRoses white and red are best!\xe2\x80\x94_Blue Roses_.\r\n\r\n\r\nThe sea had not changed. Its waters were low on the mud-banks, and the\r\nMarazion Bell-buoy clanked and swung in the tide-way. On the white\r\nbeach-sand dried stumps of sea-poppy shivered and chattered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t see the old breakwater,\xe2\x80\x9d said Maisie, under her breath.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s be thankful that we have as much as we have. I don\xe2\x80\x99t believe\r\nthey\xe2\x80\x99ve mounted a single new gun on the fort since we were here. Come\r\nand look.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey came to the glacis of Fort Keeling, and sat down in a nook\r\nsheltered from the wind under the tarred throat of a forty-pounder\r\ncannon.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, if Ammoma were only here!\xe2\x80\x9d said Maisie.\r\n\r\nFor a long time both were silent. I daren\xe2\x80\x99t tell a fib. I despise myself\r\nquite enough as it is.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat in the world for?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause\xe2\x80\x94because I take everything that you give me and I give you\r\nnothing in return. It\xe2\x80\x99s mean and selfish of me, and whenever I think of\r\nit it worries me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUnderstand once for all, then, that I can manage my own affairs, and\r\nif I choose to do anything you aren\xe2\x80\x99t to blame. Do I\xe2\x80\x94does this worrying\r\nbore you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. It does not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99d tell me if it did?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should let you know, I think.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you. They always worried just when I was\r\nin the middle of my work, and wanted me to listen to them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you listen?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt first; and they couldn\xe2\x80\x99t understand why I didn\xe2\x80\x99t care. I used to be\r\nproud of the praise, and tell Kami, and\xe2\x80\x94I shall never forget\xe2\x80\x94once Kami\r\nlaughed at me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t like being laughed at, Maisie, do you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hate it. Kami was the man.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd now you say the same thing. It\xe2\x80\x99s so disheartening.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m sorry, but you asked me to speak the truth. It\xe2\x80\x99s strong, it\xe2\x80\x99s patient\r\nsometimes,\xe2\x80\x94not always,\xe2\x80\x94and sometimes there\xe2\x80\x99s power in it, but there\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nno special reason why it should be done at all. I only want success.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re going the wrong way to get it, then. Hasn\xe2\x80\x99t Kami ever told you\r\nso?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t quote Kami to me. I want to know what you think. My work\xe2\x80\x99s bad,\r\nto begin with.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t say that, and I don\xe2\x80\x99t think it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s amateurish, then.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat it most certainly is not. You\xe2\x80\x99re a work-woman, darling, to your\r\nboot-heels, and I respect you for that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t laugh at me behind my back?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, dear. You see, you are more to me than any one else. Put this\r\ncloak thing round you, or you\xe2\x80\x99ll get chilled.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMaisie wrapped herself in the soft marten skins, turning the gray\r\nkangaroo fur to the outside.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is delicious,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, rubbing her chin thoughtfully along the\r\nfur.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell? Why am I wrong in trying to get a little success?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust because you try. Don\xe2\x80\x99t you understand, darling? Good work has\r\nnothing to do with\xe2\x80\x94doesn\xe2\x80\x99t belong to\xe2\x80\x94the person who does it. It\xe2\x80\x99s put\r\ninto him or her from outside.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut how does that affect\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWait a minute. All we can do is to learn how to do our work, to be\r\nmasters of our materials instead of servants, and never to be afraid of\r\nanything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI understand that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEverything else comes from outside ourselves. Very good. If we sit\r\ndown quietly to work out notions that are sent to us, we may or we may\r\nnot do something that isn\xe2\x80\x99t bad. A great deal depends on being master\r\nof the bricks and mortar of the trade. But the instant we begin to\r\nthink about success and the effect of our work\xe2\x80\x94to play with one eye on\r\nthe gallery\xe2\x80\x94we lose power and touch and everything else. At least\r\nthat\xe2\x80\x99s how I have found it. Instead of being quiet and giving every\r\npower you possess to your work, you\xe2\x80\x99re fretting over something which\r\nyou can neither help no hinder by a minute. See?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s so easy for you to talk in that way. People like what you do.\r\nDon\xe2\x80\x99t you ever think about the gallery?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMuch too often; but I\xe2\x80\x99m always punished for it by loss of power. It\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nas simple as the Rule of Three. If we make light of our work by using\r\nit for our own ends, our work will make light of us, and, as we\xe2\x80\x99re the\r\nweaker, we shall suffer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t treat my work lightly. You know that it\xe2\x80\x99s everything to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course; but, whether you realise it or not, you give two strokes\r\nfor yourself to one for your work. It isn\xe2\x80\x99t your fault, darling. I do\r\nexactly the same thing, and know that I\xe2\x80\x99m doing it. Most of the French\r\nschools, and all the schools here, drive the students to work for their\r\nown credit, and for the sake of their pride. I was told that all the\r\nworld was interested in my work, and everybody at Kami\xe2\x80\x99s talked\r\nturpentine, and I honestly believed that the world needed elevating and\r\ninfluencing, and all manner of impertinences, by my brushes. By Jove, I\r\nactually believed that! When my little head was bursting with a notion\r\nthat I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t handle because I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t sufficient knowledge of my\r\ncraft, I used to run about wondering at my own magnificence and getting\r\nready to astonish the world.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut surely one can do that sometimes?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery seldom with malice aforethought, darling. And when it\xe2\x80\x99s done it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nsuch a tiny thing, and the world\xe2\x80\x99s so big, and all but a millionth part\r\nof it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t care. Maisie, come with me and I\xe2\x80\x99ll show you something of\r\nthe size of the world. One can no more avoid working than eating,\xe2\x80\x94that\r\ngoes on by itself,\xe2\x80\x94but try to see what you are working for. I know such\r\nlittle heavens that I could take you to,\xe2\x80\x94islands tucked away under the\r\nLine.\r\n\r\nYou sight them after weeks of crashing through water as black as black\r\nmarble because it\xe2\x80\x99s so deep, and you sit in the fore-chains day after\r\nday and see the sun rise almost afraid because the sea\xe2\x80\x99s so lonely.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho is afraid?\xe2\x80\x94you, or the sun?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe sun, of course. And there are noises under the sea, and sounds\r\noverhead in a clear sky. Then you find your island alive with hot moist\r\norchids that make mouths at you and can do everything except talk.\r\n\r\nThere\xe2\x80\x99s a waterfall in it three hundred feet high, just like a sliver\r\nof green jade laced with silver; and millions of wild bees live up in\r\nthe rocks; and you can hear the fat cocoanuts falling from the palms;\r\nand you order an ivory-white servant to sling you a long yellow hammock\r\nwith tassels on it like ripe maize, and you put up your feet and hear\r\nthe bees hum and the water fall till you go to sleep.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan one work there?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly. One must do something always. You hang your canvas up in a\r\npalm tree and let the parrots criticise. When they scuffle you heave a\r\nripe custard-apple at them, and it bursts in a lather of cream. There\r\nare hundreds of places. Come and see them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t quite like that place. It sounds lazy. Tell me another.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you think of a big, red, dead city built of red sandstone,\r\nwith raw green aloes growing between the stones, lying out neglected on\r\nhoney-coloured sands? There are forty dead kings there, Maisie, each in\r\na gorgeous tomb finer than all the others. You look at the palaces and\r\nstreets and shops and tanks, and think that men must live there, till\r\nyou find a wee gray squirrel rubbing its nose all alone in the\r\nmarket-place, and a jewelled peacock struts out of a carved doorway and\r\nspreads its tail against a marble screen as fine pierced as point-lace.\r\nThen a monkey\xe2\x80\x94a little black monkey\xe2\x80\x94walks through the main square to\r\nget a drink from a tank forty feet deep. He slides down the creepers to\r\nthe water\xe2\x80\x99s edge, and a friend holds him by the tail, in case he should\r\nfall in.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs that all true?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have been there and seen. Then evening comes, and the lights change\r\ntill it\xe2\x80\x99s just as though you stood in the heart of a king-opal. A\r\nlittle before sundown, as punctually as clockwork, a big bristly wild\r\nboar, with all his family following, trots through the city gate,\r\nchurning the foam on his tusks. You climb on the shoulder of a blind\r\nblack stone god and watch that pig choose himself a palace for the\r\nnight and stump in wagging his tail. Then the night-wind gets up, and\r\nthe sands move, and you hear the desert outside the city singing, \xe2\x80\x9cNow\r\nI lay me down to sleep,\xe2\x80\x9d and everything is dark till the moon rises.\r\nMaisie, darling, come with me and see what the world is really like.\r\nIt\xe2\x80\x99s very lovely, and it\xe2\x80\x99s very horrible,\xe2\x80\x94but I won\xe2\x80\x99t let you see\r\nanything horrid,\xe2\x80\x94and it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t care your life or mine for pictures or\r\nanything else except doing its own work and making love. Come, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nshow you how to brew sangaree, and sling a hammock, and\xe2\x80\x94oh, thousands\r\nof things, and you\xe2\x80\x99ll see for yourself what colour means, and we\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nfind out together what love means, and then, maybe, we shall be allowed\r\nto do some good work. Come away!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy?\xe2\x80\x9d said Maisie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow can you do anything until you have seen everything, or as much as\r\nyou can? And besides, darling, I love you. Come along with me. You have\r\nno business here; you don\xe2\x80\x99t belong to this place; you\xe2\x80\x99re half a\r\ngipsy,\xe2\x80\x94your face tells that; and I\xe2\x80\x94even the smell of open water makes\r\nme restless. Come across the sea and be happy!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe had risen to his feet, and stood in the shadow of the gun, looking\r\ndown at the girl. The very short winter afternoon had worn away, and,\r\nbefore they knew, the winter moon was walking the untroubled sea. Long\r\nruled lines of silver showed where a ripple of the rising tide was\r\nturning over the mud-banks. The wind had dropped, and in the intense\r\nstillness they could hear a donkey cropping the frosty grass many yards\r\naway. A faint beating, like that of a muffled drum, came out of the\r\nmoon-haze.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s that?\xe2\x80\x9d said Maisie, quickly. \xe2\x80\x9cIt sounds like a heart beating.\r\nWhere is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDick was so angry at this sudden wrench to his pleadings that he could\r\nnot trust himself to speak, and in this silence caught the sound.\r\nMaisie from her seat under the gun watched him with a certain amount of\r\nfear. She wished so much that he would be sensible and cease to worry\r\nher with over-sea emotion that she both could and could not understand.\r\nShe was not prepared, however, for the change in his face as he\r\nlistened.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a steamer,\xe2\x80\x9d he said,\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9ca twin-screw steamer, by the beat. I can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nmake her out, but she must be standing very close in-shore. Ah!\xe2\x80\x9d as the\r\nred of a rocket streaked the haze, \xe2\x80\x9cshe\xe2\x80\x99s standing in to signal before\r\nshe clears the Channel.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it a wreck?\xe2\x80\x9d said Maisie, to whom these words were as Greek.\r\n\r\nDick\xe2\x80\x99s eyes were turned to the sea. \xe2\x80\x9cWreck! I wonder\r\nwhich steamer it is.\xe2\x80\x9d The note of his voice had changed; he seemed to\r\nbe talking to himself, and Maisie did not approve of it. She\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nlift the Southern Cross in a week,\xe2\x80\x94lucky old tub!\xe2\x80\x94oh, lucky old tub!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe stared intently, and moved up the slope of the fort to get a better\r\nview, but the mist on the sea thickened again, and the beating of the\r\nscrews grew fainter. That steamer\xe2\x80\x99s out of hearing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDick,\xe2\x80\x9d she said quietly, \xe2\x80\x9csuppose I were to come to you now,\xe2\x80\x94be quiet\r\na minute,\xe2\x80\x94just as I am, and caring for you just as much as I do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot as a brother, though? I\xe2\x80\x99m not likely to be foolish.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe earth is full of men who\xe2\x80\x99d sell their souls for three hundred a\r\nyear; and women come and talk, and borrow a five-pound note here and a\r\nten-pound note there; and a woman has no conscience in a money debt.\r\nStick to your money, Maisie, for there\xe2\x80\x99s nothing more ghastly in the\r\nworld than poverty in London. Dick\xe2\x80\x99s experience of the sordid misery of want had entered\r\ninto the deeps of him, and, lest he might find virtue too easy, that\r\nmemory stood behind him, tempting to shame, when dealers came to buy\r\nhis wares. As the Nilghai quaked against his will at the still green\r\nwater of a lake or a mill-dam, as Torpenhow flinched before any white\r\narm that could cut or stab and loathed himself for flinching, Dick\r\nfeared the poverty he had once tasted half in jest. His burden was\r\nheavier than the burdens of his companions.\r\n\r\nMaisie watched the face working in the moonlight.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve plenty of pennies now,\xe2\x80\x9d she said soothingly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall never have enough,\xe2\x80\x9d he began, with vicious emphasis. Then,\r\nlaughing, \xe2\x80\x9cI shall always be three-pence short in my accounts.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy threepence?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI carried a man\xe2\x80\x99s bag once from Liverpool Street Station to\r\nBlackfriar\xe2\x80\x99s Bridge. It was a sixpenny job,\xe2\x80\x94you needn\xe2\x80\x99t laugh; indeed\r\nit was,\xe2\x80\x94and I wanted the money desperately. He only gave me threepence;\r\nand he hadn\xe2\x80\x99t even the decency to pay in silver. Whatever money I make,\r\nI shall never get that odd threepence out of the world.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis was not language befitting the man who had preached of the\r\nsanctity of work. It jarred on Maisie, who preferred her payment in\r\napplause, which, since all men desire it, must be of her right. She\r\nhunted for her little purse and gravely took out a threepenny bit.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere it is,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll pay you, Dickie; and don\xe2\x80\x99t worry any\r\nmore; it isn\xe2\x80\x99t worth while. Are you paid?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am,\xe2\x80\x9d said the very human apostle of fair craft, taking the coin.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m paid a thousand times, and we\xe2\x80\x99ll close that account. It shall live\r\non my watch-chain; and you\xe2\x80\x99re an angel, Maisie.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m very cramped, and I\xe2\x80\x99m feeling a little cold. Good gracious! the\r\ncloak is all white, and so is your moustache! I never knew it was so\r\nchilly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA light frost lay white on the shoulder of Dick\xe2\x80\x99s ulster. He, too, had\r\nforgotten the state of the weather. They laughed together, and with\r\nthat laugh ended all serious discourse.\r\n\r\nThey ran inland across the waste to warm themselves, then turned to\r\nlook at the glory of the full tide under the moonlight and the intense\r\nblack shadows of the furze bushes. It was an additional joy to Dick\r\nthat Maisie could see colour even as he saw it,\xe2\x80\x94could see the blue in\r\nthe white of the mist, the violet that is in gray palings, and all\r\nthings else as they are,\xe2\x80\x94not of one hue, but a thousand. And the\r\nmoonlight came into Maisie\xe2\x80\x99s soul, so that she, usually reserved,\r\nchattered of herself and of the things she took interest in,\xe2\x80\x94of Kami,\r\nwisest of teachers, and of the girls in the studio,\xe2\x80\x94of the Poles, who\r\nwill kill themselves with overwork if they are not checked; of the\r\nFrench, who talk at great length of much more than they will ever\r\naccomplish; of the slovenly English, who toil hopelessly and cannot\r\nunderstand that inclination does not imply power; of the Americans,\r\nwhose rasping voices in the hush of a hot afternoon strain tense-drawn\r\nnerves to breaking-point, and whose suppers lead to indigestion; of\r\ntempestuous Russians, neither to hold nor to bind, who tell the girls\r\nghost-stories till the girls shriek; of stolid Germans, who come to\r\nlearn one thing, and, having mastered that much, stolidly go away and\r\ncopy pictures for evermore. Dick listened enraptured because it was\r\nMaisie who spoke. He knew the old life.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt hasn\xe2\x80\x99t changed much,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cDo they still steal colours at\r\nlunch-time?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot steal. Attract is the word. Of course they do. I\xe2\x80\x99m good\xe2\x80\x94I only\r\nattract ultramarine; but there are students who\xe2\x80\x99d attract flake-white.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve done it myself. You can\xe2\x80\x99t help it when the palettes are hung up.\r\n\r\nEvery colour is common property once it runs down,\xe2\x80\x94even though you do\r\nstart it with a drop of oil. It teaches people not to waste their\r\ntubes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should like to attract some of your colours, Dick. Perhaps I might\r\ncatch your success with them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI mustn\xe2\x80\x99t say a bad word, but I should like to. What in the world,\r\nwhich you\xe2\x80\x99ve just missed a lovely chance of seeing, does success or\r\nwant of success, or a three-storied success, matter compared with\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94 No,\r\nI won\xe2\x80\x99t open that question again. It\xe2\x80\x99s time to go back to town.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m sorry, Dick, but\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re much more interested in that than you are in me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know, I don\xe2\x80\x99t think I am.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat will you give me if I tell you a sure short-cut to everything you\r\nwant,\xe2\x80\x94the trouble and the fuss and the tangle and all the rest? Will\r\nyou promise to obey me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn the first place, you must never forget a meal because you happen to\r\nbe at work. You forgot your lunch twice last week,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick, at a\r\nventure, for he knew with whom he was dealing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no,\xe2\x80\x94only once, really.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s bad enough. And you mustn\xe2\x80\x99t take a cup of tea and a biscuit in\r\nplace of a regular dinner, because dinner happens to be a trouble.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re making fun of me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never was more in earnest in my life. Oh, my love, my love, hasn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nit dawned on you yet what you are to me? Here\xe2\x80\x99s the whole earth in a\r\nconspiracy to give you a chill, or run over you, or drench you to the\r\nskin, or cheat you out of your money, or let you die of overwork and\r\nunderfeeding, and I haven\xe2\x80\x99t the mere right to look after you. Why, I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t even know if you have sense enough to put on warm things when the\r\nweather\xe2\x80\x99s cold.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDick, you\xe2\x80\x99re the most awful boy to talk to\xe2\x80\x94really! How do you suppose\r\nI managed when you were away?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wasn\xe2\x80\x99t here, and I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know. But now I\xe2\x80\x99m back I\xe2\x80\x99d give everything\r\nI have for the right of telling you to come in out of the rain.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour success too?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis time it cost Dick a severe struggle to refrain from bad words.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs Mrs. Jennett used to say, you\xe2\x80\x99re a trial, Maisie! You\xe2\x80\x99ve been\r\ncooped up in the schools too long, and you think every one is looking\r\nat you.\r\n\r\nThere aren\xe2\x80\x99t twelve hundred people in the world who understand\r\npictures. The others pretend and don\xe2\x80\x99t care. Remember, I\xe2\x80\x99ve seen twelve\r\nhundred men dead in toadstool-beds. It\xe2\x80\x99s only the voice of the tiniest\r\nlittle fraction of people that makes success. The real world doesn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ncare a tinker\xe2\x80\x99s\xe2\x80\x94doesn\xe2\x80\x99t care a bit. For aught you or I know, every man\r\nin the world may be arguing with a Maisie of his own.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPoor Maisie!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPoor Dick, I think. Do you believe while he\xe2\x80\x99s fighting for what\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndearer than his life he wants to look at a picture? And even if he did,\r\nand if all the world did, and a thousand million people rose up and\r\nshouted hymns to my honour and glory, would that make up to me for the\r\nknowledge that you were out shopping in the Edgware Road on a rainy day\r\nwithout an umbrella? Now we\xe2\x80\x99ll go to the station.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you said on the beach\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d persisted Maisie, with a certain fear.\r\n\r\nDick groaned aloud: \xe2\x80\x9cYes, I know what I said. My work is everything I\r\nhave, or am, or hope to be, to me, and I believe I\xe2\x80\x99ve learnt the law\r\nthat governs it; but I\xe2\x80\x99ve some lingering sense of fun left,\xe2\x80\x94though\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ve nearly knocked it out of me. I can just see that it isn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\neverything to all the world. Do what I say, and not what I do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMaisie was careful not to reopen debatable matters, and they returned\r\nto London joyously. The terminus stopped Dick in the midst of an\r\neloquent harangue on the beauties of exercise. He would buy Maisie a\r\nhorse,\xe2\x80\x94such a horse as never yet bowed head to bit,\xe2\x80\x94would stable it,\r\nwith a companion, some twenty miles from London, and Maisie, solely for\r\nher health\xe2\x80\x99s sake should ride with him twice or thrice a week.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s absurd,\xe2\x80\x9d said she. \xe2\x80\x9cIt wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be proper.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, who in all London to-night would have sufficient interest or\r\naudacity to call us two to account for anything we chose to do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMaisie looked at the lamps, the fog, and the hideous turmoil. Dick was\r\nright; but horseflesh did not make for Art as she understood it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re very nice sometimes, but you\xe2\x80\x99re very foolish more times. I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\nnot going to let you give me horses, or take you out of your way\r\nto-night. I\xe2\x80\x99ll go home by myself. Only I want you to promise me\r\nsomething. You won\xe2\x80\x99t think any more about that extra threepence, will\r\nyou? Remember, you\xe2\x80\x99ve been paid; and I won\xe2\x80\x99t allow you to be spiteful\r\nand do bad work for a little thing like that. You can be so big that\r\nyou mustn\xe2\x80\x99t be tiny.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis was turning the tables with a vengeance. There remained only to\r\nput Maisie into her hansom.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood-bye,\xe2\x80\x9d she said simply. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll come on Sunday. It has been a\r\nbeautiful day, Dick. Why can\xe2\x80\x99t it be like this always?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause love\xe2\x80\x99s like line-work: you must go forward or backward; you\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t stand still. By the way, go on with your line-work. Good-night,\r\nand, for my\xe2\x80\x94for my sake, take care of yourself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe turned to walk home, meditating. The day had brought him nothing\r\nthat he hoped for, but\xe2\x80\x94surely this was worth many days\xe2\x80\x94it had brought\r\nhim nearer to Maisie. The end was only a question of time now, and the\r\nprize well worth the waiting. By instinct, once more, he turned to the\r\nriver.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd she understood at once,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, looking at the water. \xe2\x80\x9cShe found\r\nout my pet besetting sin on the spot, and paid it off. My God, how she\r\nunderstood! And she said I was better than she was! Better than she\r\nwas!\xe2\x80\x9d He laughed at the absurdity of the notion. \xe2\x80\x9cI wonder if girls\r\nguess at one-half a man\xe2\x80\x99s life. They can\xe2\x80\x99t, or\xe2\x80\x94they wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t marry us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nHe took her gift out of his pocket, and considered it in the light of a\r\nmiracle and a pledge of the comprehension that, one day, would lead to\r\nperfect happiness. Meantime, Maisie was alone in London, with none to\r\nsave her from danger. And the packed wilderness was very full of\r\ndanger.\r\n\r\nDick made his prayer to Fate disjointedly after the manner of the\r\nheathen as he threw the piece of silver into the river. If any evil\r\nwere to befal, let him bear the burden and let Maisie go unscathed,\r\nsince the threepenny piece was dearest to him of all his possessions.\r\nIt was a small coin in itself, but Maisie had given it, and the Thames\r\nheld it, and surely the Fates would be bribed for this once.\r\n\r\nThe drowning of the coin seemed to cut him free from thought of Maisie\r\nfor the moment. He took himself off the bridge and went whistling to\r\nhis chambers with a strong yearning for some man-talk and tobacco after\r\nhis first experience of an entire day spent in the society of a woman.\r\nThere was a stronger desire at his heart when there rose before him an\r\nunsolicited vision of the _Barralong_ dipping deep and sailing free for\r\nthe Southern Cross.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VIII\r\n\r\n\r\nAnd these two, as I have told you,\r\nWere the friends of Hiawatha,\r\nChibiabos, the musician,\r\nAnd the very strong man, Kwasind.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x94_Hiawatha_.\r\n\r\n\r\nTorpenhow was paging the last sheets of some manuscript, while the\r\nNilghai, who had come for chess and remained to talk tactics, was\r\nreading through the first part, commenting scornfully the while.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s picturesque enough and it\xe2\x80\x99s sketchy,\xe2\x80\x9d said he; \xe2\x80\x9cbut as a serious\r\nconsideration of affairs in Eastern Europe, it\xe2\x80\x99s not worth much.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s off my hands at any rate.... Thirty-seven, thirty-eight,\r\nthirty-nine slips altogether, aren\xe2\x80\x99t there? That should make between\r\neleven and twelve pages of valuable misinformation. Heigho!\xe2\x80\x9d Torpenhow\r\nshuffled the writing together and hummed\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\nYoung lambs to sell, young lambs to sell,\r\nIf I\xe2\x80\x99d as much money as I could tell,\r\nI never would cry, Young lambs to sell!\r\n\r\n\r\nDick entered, self-conscious and a little defiant, but in the best of\r\ntempers with all the world.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBack at last?\xe2\x80\x9d said Torpenhow.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMore or less. What have you been doing?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWork. Dickie, you behave as though the Bank of England were behind\r\nyou. Here\xe2\x80\x99s Sunday, Monday, and Tuesday gone and you haven\xe2\x80\x99t done a\r\nline. It\xe2\x80\x99s scandalous.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe notions come and go, my children\xe2\x80\x94they come and go like our\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cbaccy,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered, filling his pipe. \xe2\x80\x9cMoreover,\xe2\x80\x9d he stooped to\r\nthrust a spill into the grate, \xe2\x80\x9cApollo does not always stretch his\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\r\nOh, confound your clumsy jests, Nilghai!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is not the place to preach the theory of direct inspiration,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid the Nilghai, returning Torpenhow\xe2\x80\x99s large and workmanlike bellows\r\nto their nail on the wall. \xe2\x80\x9cWe believe in cobblers\xe2\x80\x99 wax. _La!_\xe2\x80\x94where\r\nyou sit down.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you weren\xe2\x80\x99t so big and fat,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick, looking round for a weapon,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo skylarking in my rooms. You two smashed half my furniture last time\r\nyou threw the cushions about. You might have the decency to say How\r\nd\xe2\x80\x99you do? to Binkie. Look at him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBinkie had jumped down from the sofa and was fawning round Dick\xe2\x80\x99s knee,\r\nand scratching at his boots.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear man!\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick, snatching him up, and kissing him on the black\r\npatch above his right eye. \xe2\x80\x9cDid ums was, Binks? Did that ugly Nilghai\r\nturn you off the sofa? Bite him, Mr. Binkie.\xe2\x80\x9d He pitched him on the\r\nNilghai\xe2\x80\x99s stomach, as the big man lay at ease, and Binkie pretended to\r\ndestroy the Nilghai inch by inch, till a sofa cushion extinguished him,\r\nand panting he stuck out his tongue at the company.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Binkie-boy went for a walk this morning before you were up, Torp.\r\n\r\nI saw him making love to the butcher at the corner when the shutters\r\nwere being taken down\xe2\x80\x94just as if he hadn\xe2\x80\x99t enough to eat in his own\r\nproper house,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBinks, is that a true bill?\xe2\x80\x9d said Torpenhow, severely. The little dog\r\nretreated under the sofa cushion, and showed by the fat white back of\r\nhim that he really had no further interest in the discussion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStrikes me that another disreputable dog went for a walk, too,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nthe Nilghai. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat made you get up so early? Torp said you might be\r\nbuying a horse.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe knows it would need three of us for a serious business like that.\r\nNo, I felt lonesome and unhappy, so I went out to look at the sea, and\r\nwatch the pretty ships go by.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere did you go?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSomewhere on the Channel. It was a thick day, but the sea smelt good.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWherefore put on one\xe2\x80\x99s best trousers to see the _Barralong?_\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nTorpenhow, pointing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause I\xe2\x80\x99ve nothing except these things and my painting duds.\r\nBesides, I wanted to do honour to the sea.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid She make you feel restless?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the Nilghai, keenly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCrazy. Don\xe2\x80\x99t speak of it. I\xe2\x80\x99m sorry I went.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTorpenhow and the Nilghai exchanged a look as Dick, stooping, busied\r\nhimself among the former\xe2\x80\x99s boots and trees.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThese will do,\xe2\x80\x9d he said at last; \xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t say I think much of your\r\ntaste in slippers, but the fit\xe2\x80\x99s the thing.\xe2\x80\x9d He slipped his feet into a\r\npair of sock-like sambhur-skin foot coverings, found a long chair, and\r\nlay at length.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99re my own pet pair,\xe2\x80\x9d Torpenhow said. \xe2\x80\x9cI was just going to put\r\nthem on myself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll your reprehensible selfishness. Just because you see me happy for\r\na minute, you want to worry me and stir me up. Find another pair.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood for you that Dick can\xe2\x80\x99t wear your clothes, Torp. You two live\r\ncommunistically,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Nilghai.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDick never has anything that I can wear. He\xe2\x80\x99s only useful to sponge\r\nupon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cConfound you, have you been rummaging round among my clothes, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Dick. \xe2\x80\x9cI put a sovereign in the tobacco-jar yesterday. How do you\r\nexpect a man to keep his accounts properly if you\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHere the Nilghai began to laugh, and Torpenhow joined him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHid a sovereign yesterday! You\xe2\x80\x99re no sort of financier. You lent me a\r\nfiver about a month back. You and I are arranged for\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy Kismet and our own powers, more\xe2\x80\x99s the pity. In it Dick had drawn all manner of\r\nmoving incidents, experienced by himself or related to him by the\r\nothers, of all the four corners of the earth. But the wider range of\r\nthe Nilghai\xe2\x80\x99s body and life attracted him most. When truth failed he\r\nfell back on fiction of the wildest, and represented incidents in the\r\nNilghai\xe2\x80\x99s career that were unseemly,\xe2\x80\x94his marriages with many African\r\nprincesses, his shameless betrayal, for Arab wives, of an army corps to\r\nthe Mahdi, his tattooment by skilled operators in Burmah, his interview\r\n(and his fears) with the yellow headsman in the blood-stained\r\nexecution-ground of Canton, and finally, the passings of his spirit\r\ninto the bodies of whales, elephants, and toucans. Observe\r\nthe virtuous horror of the lions!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou may as well explain,\xe2\x80\x9d said Torpenhow, and Dick lifted his head\r\nfrom the paper.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe sea reminded me of it,\xe2\x80\x9d he said slowly. \xe2\x80\x9cI wish it hadn\xe2\x80\x99t. It\r\nweighs some few thousand tons\xe2\x80\x94unless you cut it out with a cold\r\nchisel.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t be an idiot. I was loafing from\r\nLima to Auckland in a big, old, condemned passenger-ship turned into a\r\ncargo-boat and owned by a second-hand Italian firm. She was a crazy\r\nbasket. We were cut down to fifteen ton of coal a day, and we thought\r\nourselves lucky when we kicked seven knots an hour out of her. Then we\r\nused to stop and let the bearings cool down, and wonder whether the\r\ncrack in the shaft was spreading.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWere you a steward or a stoker in those days?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was flush for the time being, so I was a passenger, or else I should\r\nhave been a steward, I think,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick, with perfect gravity,\r\nreturning to the procession of angry wives. \xe2\x80\x9cI was the only other\r\npassenger from Lima, and the ship was half empty, and full of rats and\r\ncockroaches and scorpions.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what has this to do with the picture?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWait a minute. She had been in the China passenger trade and her lower\r\ndecks had bunks for two thousand pigtails. Those were all taken down,\r\nand she was empty up to her nose, and the lights came through the port\r\nholes\xe2\x80\x94most annoying lights to work in till you got used to them. I\r\nhadn\xe2\x80\x99t anything to do for weeks. The ship\xe2\x80\x99s charts were in pieces and\r\nour skipper daren\xe2\x80\x99t run south for fear of catching a storm. There was some brown paint and some\r\ngreen paint that they used for the boats, and some black paint for\r\nironwork, and that was all I had.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe passengers must have thought you mad.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere was only one, and it was a woman; but it gave me the notion of\r\nmy picture.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat was she like?\xe2\x80\x9d said Torpenhow.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe was a sort of Negroid-Jewess-Cuban; with morals to match. She\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t read or write, and she didn\xe2\x80\x99t want to, but she used to come\r\ndown and watch me paint, and the skipper didn\xe2\x80\x99t like it, because he was\r\npaying her passage and had to be on the bridge occasionally.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see. So,\r\nyou see, we could never tell when we might be caught, and I had a\r\nsplendid notion to work out in only three keys of colour.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat was the notion?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTwo lines in Poe\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\nNeither the angels in Heaven above nor the demons down under the sea,\r\nCan ever dissever my soul from the soul of the beautiful Annabel Lee.\r\n\r\n\r\nIt came out of the sea\xe2\x80\x94all by itself. I drew that fight, fought out in\r\ngreen water over the naked, choking soul, and the woman served as the\r\nmodel for the devils and the angels both\xe2\x80\x94sea-devils and sea-angels, and\r\nthe soul half drowned between them. It doesn\xe2\x80\x99t sound much, but when\r\nthere was a good light on the lower deck it looked very fine and\r\ncreepy. It was seven by fourteen feet, all done in shifting light for\r\nshifting light.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid the woman inspire you much?\xe2\x80\x9d said Torpenhow.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe and the sea between them\xe2\x80\x94immensely. The eyes of\r\nthe demons scared them, I honestly believe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the woman?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe was scared too when it was finished. Just three colours and no chance of\r\ngetting any more, and the sea outside and unlimited love-making inside,\r\nand the fear of death atop of everything else, O Lord!\xe2\x80\x9d He had ceased\r\nto look at the sketch, but was staring straight in front of him across\r\nthe room.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy don\xe2\x80\x99t you try something of the same kind now?\xe2\x80\x9d said the Nilghai.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause those things come not by fasting and prayer. You can book from here to St. Petersburg if you\r\nchoose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDick crammed head and shoulders out of the window and looked across the\r\nriver.
