Binkie, making himself as large as\r\npossible, spread out upon the sofa with the air of one who is not to be\r\nlightly disturbed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Nilghai to the two pairs of shoulders, \xe2\x80\x9chave you never\r\nseen this place before?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA steam-tug on the river hooted as she towed her barges to wharf. Then\r\nthe boom of the traffic came into the room. Torpenhow nudged Dick.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood place to bank in\xe2\x80\x94bad place to bunk in, Dickie, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDick\xe2\x80\x99s chin was in his hand as he answered, in the words of a general\r\nnot without fame, still looking out on the darkness\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98My God, what a\r\ncity to loot!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBinkie found the night air tickling his whiskers and sneezed\r\nplaintively.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe shall give the Binkie-dog a cold,\xe2\x80\x9d said Torpenhow. \xe2\x80\x9cCome in,\xe2\x80\x9d and\r\nthey withdrew their heads. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll be buried in Kensal Green, Dick, one\r\nof these days, if it isn\xe2\x80\x99t closed by the time you want to go\r\nthere\xe2\x80\x94buried within two feet of some one else, his wife and his\r\nfamily.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAllah forbid! I shall get away before that time comes. Give a man room\r\nto stretch his legs, Mr. Binkie.\xe2\x80\x9d Dick flung himself down on the sofa\r\nand tweaked Binkie\xe2\x80\x99s velvet ears, yawning heavily the while.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll find that wardrobe-case very much out of tune,\xe2\x80\x9d Torpenhow said\r\nto the Nilghai. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s never touched except by you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA piece of gross extravagance,\xe2\x80\x9d Dick grunted. \xe2\x80\x9cThe Nilghai only comes\r\nwhen I\xe2\x80\x99m out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s because you\xe2\x80\x99re always out. Howl, Nilghai, and let him hear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe life of the Nilghai is fraud and slaughter,\r\nHis writings are watered Dickens and water;\r\nBut the voice of the Nilghai raised on high\r\nMakes even the Mahdieh glad to die!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nDick quoted from Torpenhow\xe2\x80\x99s letterpress in the Nungapunga Book.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow do they call moose in Canada, Nilghai?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe man laughed. Singing was his one polite accomplishment, as many\r\nPress-tents in far-off lands had known.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat shall I sing?\xe2\x80\x9d said he, turning in the chair.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x9cMoll Roe in the Morning,\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x99 said Torpenhow, at a venture.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick, sharply, and the Nilghai opened his eyes. The old\r\nchanty whereof he, among a very few, possessed all the words was not a\r\npretty one, but Dick had heard it many times before without wincing.\r\nWithout prelude he launched into that stately tune that calls together\r\nand troubles the hearts of the gipsies of the sea\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFarewell and adieu to you, Spanish ladies,\r\nFarewell and adieu to you, ladies of Spain.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nDick turned uneasily on the sofa, for he could hear the bows of the\r\nBarralong crashing into the green seas on her way to the Southern\r\nCross.\r\n\r\nThen came the chorus\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ll rant and we\xe2\x80\x99ll roar like true British sailors,\r\nWe\xe2\x80\x99ll rant and we\xe2\x80\x99ll roar across the salt seas,\r\nUntil we take soundings in the Channel of Old England\r\nFrom Ushant to Scilly \xe2\x80\x99tis forty-five leagues.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThirty-five-thirty-five,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick, petulantly. \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t tamper with\r\nHoly Writ. Go on, Nilghai.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe first land we made it was called the Deadman,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nand they sang to the end very vigourously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat would be a better song if her head were turned the other way\xe2\x80\x94to\r\nthe Ushant light, for instance,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Nilghai.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFlinging his arms about like a mad windmill,\xe2\x80\x9d said Torpenhow. \xe2\x80\x9cGive us\r\nsomething else, Nilghai. You\xe2\x80\x99re in fine fog-horn form tonight.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGive us the \xe2\x80\x9cGanges Pilot\xe2\x80\x9d; you sang that in the square the night\r\nbefore El-Maghrib. By the way, I wonder how many of the chorus are\r\nalive to-night,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick.\r\n\r\nTorpenhow considered for a minute. \xe2\x80\x9cBy Jove! I believe only you and I.\r\n\r\nRaynor, Vicery, and Deenes\xe2\x80\x94all dead; Vincent caught smallpox in Cairo,\r\ncarried it here and died of it. Yes, only you and I and the Nilghai.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUmph! And yet the men here who\xe2\x80\x99ve done their work in a well-warmed\r\nstudio all their lives, with a policeman at each corner, say that I\r\ncharge too much for my pictures.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are buying your work, not your insurance policies, dear child,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid the Nilghai.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI gambled with one to get at the other. Don\xe2\x80\x99t preach. Go on with the\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPilot.\xe2\x80\x9d Where in the world did you get that song?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOn a tombstone,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Nilghai. \xe2\x80\x9cOn a tombstone in a distant land.\r\nI made it an accompaniment with heaps of base chords.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Vanity! Begin.\xe2\x80\x9d And the Nilghai began\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have slipped my cable, messmates, I\xe2\x80\x99m drifting down with the tide,\r\nI have my sailing orders, while yet an anchor ride.\r\nAnd never on fair June morning have I put out to sea\r\nWith clearer conscience or better hope, or a heart more light and free.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShoulder to shoulder, Joe, my boy, into the crowd like a wedge\r\nStrike with the hangers, messmates, but do not cut with the edge.\r\nCries Charnock, \xe2\x80\x9cScatter the faggots, double that Brahmin in two,\r\nThe tall pale widow for me, Joe, the little brown girl for you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYoung Joe (you\xe2\x80\x99re nearing sixty), why is your hide so dark?\r\nKatie has soft fair blue eyes, who blackened yours?\xe2\x80\x94Why, hark!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nThey were all singing now, Dick with the roar of the wind of the open\r\nsea about his ears as the deep bass voice let itself go.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe morning gun\xe2\x80\x94Ho, steady! the arquebuses to me!\r\nI ha\xe2\x80\x99 sounded the Dutch High Admiral\xe2\x80\x99s heart as my lead doth sound the\r\nsea.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSounding, sounding the Ganges, floating down with the tide,\r\nMoore me close to Charnock, next to my nut-brown bride.\r\nMy blessing to Kate at Fairlight\xe2\x80\x94Holwell, my thanks to you;\r\nSteady! We steer for heaven, through sand-drifts cold and blue.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow what is there in that nonsense to make a man restless?\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick,\r\nhauling Binkie from his feet to his chest.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt depends on the man,\xe2\x80\x9d said Torpenhow.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe man who has been down to look at the sea,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Nilghai.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t know she was going to upset me in this fashion.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s what men say when they go to say good-bye to a woman. It\xe2\x80\x99s more\r\neasy though to get rid of three women than a piece of one\xe2\x80\x99s life and\r\nsurroundings.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut a woman can be\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d began Dick, unguardedly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA piece of one\xe2\x80\x99s life,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Torpenhow. \xe2\x80\x9cNo, she can\xe2\x80\x99t. His face\r\ndarkened for a moment. \xe2\x80\x9cShe says she wants to sympathise with you and\r\nhelp you in your work, and everything else that clearly a man must do\r\nfor himself. Then she sends round five notes a day to ask why the\r\ndickens you haven\xe2\x80\x99t been wasting your time with her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t generalise,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Nilghai. \xe2\x80\x9cBy the time you arrive at five\r\nnotes a day you must have gone through a good deal and behaved\r\naccordingly.\r\n\r\nShouldn\xe2\x80\x99t begin these things, my son.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have gone down to the sea,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick, just a little\r\nanxious to change the conversation. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd you shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have sung.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe sea isn\xe2\x80\x99t sending you five notes a day,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Nilghai.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, but I\xe2\x80\x99m fatally compromised. She\xe2\x80\x99s an enduring old hag, and I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\nsorry I ever met her. Why wasn\xe2\x80\x99t I born and bred and dead in a\r\nthree-pair back?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHear him blaspheming his first love! Why in the world shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t you\r\nlisten to her?\xe2\x80\x9d said Torpenhow.\r\n\r\nBefore Dick could reply the Nilghai lifted up his voice with a shout\r\nthat shook the windows, in \xe2\x80\x9cThe Men of the Sea,\xe2\x80\x9d that begins, as all\r\nknow, \xe2\x80\x9cThe sea is a wicked old woman,\xe2\x80\x9d and after rading through eight\r\nlines whose imagery is truthful, ends in a refrain, slow as the\r\nclacking of a capstan when the boat comes unwillingly up to the bars\r\nwhere the men sweat and tramp in the shingle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Ye that bore us, O restore us!\r\nShe is kinder than ye;\r\nFor the call is on our heart-strings!\xe2\x80\x99\r\nSaid The Men of the Sea.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nThe Nilghai sang that verse twice, with simple cunning, intending that\r\nDick should hear. But Dick was waiting for the farewell of the men to\r\ntheir wives.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Ye that love us, can ye move us?\r\nShe is dearer than ye;\r\nAnd your sleep will be the sweeter,\xe2\x80\x99\r\nSaid The Men of the Sea.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nThe rough words beat like the blows of the waves on the bows of the\r\nrickety boat from Lima in the days when Dick was mixing paints, making\r\nlove, drawing devils and angels in the half dark, and wondering whether\r\nthe next minute would put the Italian captain\xe2\x80\x99s knife between his\r\nshoulder-blades. And the go-fever which is more real than many doctors\xe2\x80\x99\r\ndiseases, waked and raged, urging him who loved Maisie beyond anything\r\nin the world, to go away and taste the old hot, unregenerate life\r\nagain,\xe2\x80\x94to scuffle, swear, gamble, and love light loves with his\r\nfellows; to take ship and know the sea once more, and by her beget\r\npictures; to talk to Binat among the sands of Port Said while Yellow\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTina mixed the drinks; to hear the crackle of musketry, and see the\r\nsmoke roll outward, thin and thicken again till the shining black faces\r\ncame through, and in that hell every man was strictly responsible for\r\nhis own head, and his own alone, and struck with an unfettered arm. It\r\nwas impossible, utterly impossible, but\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Oh, our fathers in the churchyard,\r\nShe is older than ye,\r\nAnd our graves will be the greener,\xe2\x80\x99\r\nSaid The Men of the Sea.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat _is_ there to hinder?\xe2\x80\x9d said Torpenhow, in the long hush that\r\nfollowed the song.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou said a little time since that you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t come for a walk round\r\nthe world, Torp.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was months ago, and I only objected to your making money for\r\ntravelling expenses. You\xe2\x80\x99ve shot your bolt here and it has gone home.\r\nGo away and do some work, and see some things.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGet some of the fat off you; you\xe2\x80\x99re disgracefully out of condition,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid the Nilghai, making a plunge from the chair and grasping a handful\r\nof Dick generally over the right ribs. \xe2\x80\x9cSoft as putty\xe2\x80\x94pure tallow born\r\nof over-feeding. Train it off, Dickie.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re all equally gross, Nilghai. Next time you have to take the field\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ll sit down, wink your eyes, gasp, and die in a fit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind. You go away on a ship. Go to Lima again, or to Brazil.\r\n\r\nThere\xe2\x80\x99s always trouble in South America.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you suppose I want to be told where to go? Great Heavens, the only\r\ndifficulty is to know where I\xe2\x80\x99m to stop. But I shall stay here, as I\r\ntold you before.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you\xe2\x80\x99ll be buried in Kensal Green and turn into adipocere with the\r\nothers,\xe2\x80\x9d said Torpenhow. \xe2\x80\x9cAre you thinking of commissions in hand? Pay\r\nforfeit and go. You\xe2\x80\x99ve money enough to travel as a king if you please.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve the grisliest notions of amusement, Torp. I think I see myself\r\nshipping first class on a six-thousand-ton hotel, and asking the third\r\nengineer what makes the engines go round, and whether it isn\xe2\x80\x99t very\r\nwarm in the stokehold. Ho! ho! I should ship as a loafer if ever I\r\nshipped at all, which I\xe2\x80\x99m not going to do. I shall compromise, and go\r\nfor a small trip to begin with.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s something at any rate. Where will you go?\xe2\x80\x9d said Torpenhow. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\r\nwould do you all the good in the world, old man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Nilghai saw the twinkle in Dick\xe2\x80\x99s eye, and refrained from speech.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall go in the first place to Rathray\xe2\x80\x99s stable, where I shall hire\r\none horse, and take him very carefully as far as Richmond Hill. Then I\r\nshall walk him back again, in case he should accidentally burst into a\r\nlather and make Rathray angry. I shall do that to-morrow, for the sake\r\nof air and exercise.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBah!\xe2\x80\x9d Dick had barely time to throw up his arm and ward off the\r\ncushion that the disgusted Torpenhow heaved at his head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAir and exercise indeed,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Nilghai, sitting down heavily on\r\nDick.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s give him a little of both. Get the bellows, Torp.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt this point the conference broke up in disorder, because Dick would\r\nnot open his mouth till the Nilghai held his nose fast, and there was\r\nsome trouble in forcing the nozzle of the bellows between his teeth;\r\nand even when it was there he weakly tried to puff against the force of\r\nthe blast, and his cheeks blew up with a great explosion; and the enemy\r\nbecoming helpless with laughter he so beat them over the head with a\r\nsoft sofa cushion that that became unsewn and distributed its feathers,\r\nand Binkie, interfering in Torpenhow\xe2\x80\x99s interests, was bundled into the\r\nhalf-empty bag and advised to scratch his way out, which he did after a\r\nwhile, travelling rapidly up and down the floor in the shape of an\r\nagitated green haggis, and when he came out looking for satisfaction,\r\nthe three pillars of his world were picking feathers out of their hair.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA prophet has no honour in his own country,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick, ruefully,\r\ndusting his knees. \xe2\x80\x9cThis filthy fluff will never brush off my legs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was all for your own good,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Nilghai. \xe2\x80\x9cNothing like air and\r\nexercise.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll for your good,\xe2\x80\x9d said Torpenhow, not in the least with reference to\r\npast clowning. \xe2\x80\x9cIt would let you focus things at their proper worth and\r\nprevent your becoming slack in this hothouse of a town. Indeed it\r\nwould, old man. I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have spoken if I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t thought so. Only,\r\nyou make a joke of everything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBefore God I do no such thing,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick, quickly and earnestly. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t know me if you think that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n_I_ don\xe2\x80\x99t think it,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Nilghai.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow can fellows like ourselves, who know what life and death really\r\nmean, dare to make a joke of anything? I know we pretend it, to save\r\nourselves from breaking down or going to the other extreme. Can\xe2\x80\x99t I\r\nsee, old man, how you\xe2\x80\x99re always anxious about me, and try to advise me\r\nto make my work better? Do you suppose I don\xe2\x80\x99t think about that myself?\r\nBut you can\xe2\x80\x99t help me\xe2\x80\x94you can\xe2\x80\x99t help me\xe2\x80\x94not even you. I must play my\r\nown hand alone in my own way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHear, hear,\xe2\x80\x9d from the Nilghai.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the one thing in the Nilghai Saga that I\xe2\x80\x99ve never drawn in the\r\nNungapunga Book?\xe2\x80\x9d Dick continued to Torpenhow, who was a little\r\nastonished at the outburst.\r\n\r\nNow there was one blank page in the book given over to the sketch that\r\nDick had not drawn of the crowning exploit in the Nilghai\xe2\x80\x99s life; when\r\nthat man, being young and forgetting that his body and bones belonged\r\nto the paper that employed him, had ridden over sunburned slippery\r\ngrass in the rear of Bredow\xe2\x80\x99s brigade on the day that the troopers\r\nflung themselves at Caurobert\xe2\x80\x99s artillery, and for aught they knew\r\ntwenty battalions in front, to save the battered 24th German Infantry,\r\nto give time to decide the fate of Vionville, and to learn ere their\r\nremnant came back to Flavigay that cavalry can attack and crumple and\r\nbreak unshaken infantry. Whenever he was inclined to think over a life\r\nthat might have been better, an income that might have been larger, and\r\na soul that might have been considerably cleaner, the Nilghai would\r\ncomfort himself with the thought, \xe2\x80\x9cI rode with Bredow\xe2\x80\x99s brigade at\r\nVionville,\xe2\x80\x9d and take heart for any lesser battle the next day might\r\nbring.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know,\xe2\x80\x9d he said very gravely. \xe2\x80\x9cI was always glad that you left it\r\nout.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI left it out because Nilghai taught me what the Germany army learned\r\nthen, and what Schmidt taught their cavalry. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know German.\r\n\r\nWhat is it? \xe2\x80\x9cTake care of the time and the dressing will take care of\r\nitself.\xe2\x80\x9d I must ride my own line to my own beat, old man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Tempo ist richtung_. You\xe2\x80\x99ve learned your lesson well,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nNilghai.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe must go alone. He speaks truth, Torp.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMaybe I\xe2\x80\x99m as wrong as I can be\xe2\x80\x94hideously wrong. I must find that out\r\nfor myself, as I have to think things out for myself, but I daren\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nturn my head to dress by the next man. It hurts me a great deal more\r\nthan you know not to be able to go, but I cannot, that\xe2\x80\x99s all. I must do\r\nmy own work and live my own life in my own way, because I\xe2\x80\x99m responsible\r\nfor both.\r\n\r\nOnly don\xe2\x80\x99t think I frivol about it, Torp. I have my own matches and\r\nsulphur, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll make my own hell, thanks.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was an uncomfortable pause. Then Torpenhow said blandly, \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\r\ndid the Governor of North Carolina say to the Governor of South\r\nCarolina?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExcellent notion. It is a long time between drinks. There are the\r\nmakings of a very fine prig in you, Dick,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Nilghai.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve liberated my mind, estimable Binkie, with the feathers in his\r\nmouth.\xe2\x80\x9d Dick picked up the still indignant one and shook him tenderly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re tied up in a sack and made to run about blind, Binkie-wee,\r\nwithout any reason, and it has hurt your little feelings. Never mind.\r\n_Sic volo, sic jubeo, stet pro ratione voluntas_, and don\xe2\x80\x99t sneeze in\r\nmy eye because I talk Latin. He\xe2\x80\x99s not pleased.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99d swear at me if he weren\xe2\x80\x99t. I can\xe2\x80\x99t make it out. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t feel it so\r\nmuch when I was with Maisie. These damnable songs did it. He\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nbeginning again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut it was only Herrick\xe2\x80\x99s Nightpiece to Julia that the Nilghai sang,\r\nand before it was ended Dick reappeared on the threshold, not\r\naltogether clothed indeed, but in his right mind, thirsty and at peace.\r\n\r\nThe mood had come and gone with the rising and the falling of the tide\r\nby Fort Keeling.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER IX\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I have taken the common clay\r\n    And wrought it cunningly\r\nIn the shape of a god that was digged a clod,\r\n    The greater honour to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf thou hast taken the common clay,\r\n    And thy hands be not free\r\nFrom the taint of the soil, thou hast made thy spoil\r\n    The greater shame to thee.\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94_The Two Potters_.\r\n\r\n\r\nHe did no work of any kind for the rest of the week. Then came another\r\nSunday. He dreaded and longed for the day always, but since the\r\nred-haired girl had sketched him there was rather more dread than\r\ndesire in his mind.\r\n\r\nHe found that Maisie had entirely neglected his suggestions about\r\nline-work. She had gone off at score filled with some absurd notion for\r\na \xe2\x80\x9cfancy head.\xe2\x80\x9d It cost Dick something to command his temper.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the good of suggesting anything?\xe2\x80\x9d he said pointedly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, but this will be a picture,\xe2\x80\x94a real picture; and I know that Kami\r\nwill let me send it to the Salon. What has taken\r\nher fancy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe description of the Melancolia\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHer folded wings as of a mighty eagle,\r\nBut all too impotent to lift the regal\r\nRobustness of her earth-born strength and pride.\r\n\r\n\r\nAnd here again. (Maisie, get the tea, dear.)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe forehead charged with baleful thoughts and dreams,\r\nThe household bunch of keys, the housewife\xe2\x80\x99s gown,\r\n    Voluminous indented, and yet rigid\r\n    As though a shell of burnished metal frigid,\r\nHer feet thick-shod to tread all weakness down.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nThere was no attempt to conceal the scorn of the lazy voice. Dick\r\nwinced.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut that has been done already by an obscure artist by the name of\r\nDurer,\xe2\x80\x9d said he. \xe2\x80\x9cHow does the poem run?\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThree centuries and threescore years ago,\r\nWith phantasies of his peculiar thought.\r\n\r\n\r\nYou might as well try to rewrite _Hamlet_. Isn\xe2\x80\x99t it selfish of him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHer companion opened her lips as if to speak, shut them again, and went\r\non reading _The City of Dreadful Night_.\r\n\r\nDick was in the Park, walking round and round a tree that he had chosen\r\nas his confidante for many Sundays past. He was swearing audibly, and\r\nwhen he found that the infirmities of the English tongue hemmed in his\r\nrage, he sought consolation in Arabic, which is expressly designed for\r\nthe use of the afflicted. He was not pleased with the reward of his\r\npatient service; nor was he pleased with himself; and it was long\r\nbefore he arrived at the proposition that the queen could do no wrong.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a losing game,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m worth nothing when a whim of hers\r\nis in question. But in a losing game at Port Said we used to double the\r\nstakes and go on. She do a Melancolia! She hasn\xe2\x80\x99t the power, or the\r\ninsight, or the training. Only the desire. She\xe2\x80\x99s cursed with the curse\r\nof Reuben. She won\xe2\x80\x99t do line-work, because it means real work; and yet\r\nshe\xe2\x80\x99s stronger than I am. I\xe2\x80\x99ll make her understand that I can beat her\r\non her own Melancolia. Even then she wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t care. She says I can only\r\ndo blood and bones. I don\xe2\x80\x99t believe she has blood in her veins. All the\r\nsame I love her; and I must go on loving her; and if I can humble her\r\ninordinate vanity I will. I\xe2\x80\x99ll do a Melancolia that shall be something\r\nlike a Melancolia\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cthe Melancolia that transcends all wit.\xe2\x80\x9d I\xe2\x80\x99ll do it\r\nat once, con\xe2\x80\x94bless her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe discovered that the notion would not come to order, and that he\r\ncould not free his mind for an hour from the thought of Maisie\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndeparture. He took very small interest in her rough studies for the\r\nMelancolia when she showed them next week. The Sundays were racing\r\npast, and the time was at hand when all the church bells in London\r\ncould not ring Maisie back to him. Once or twice he said something to\r\nBinkie about \xe2\x80\x9chermaphroditic futilities,\xe2\x80\x9d but the little dog received\r\nso many confidences both from Torpenhow and Dick that he did not\r\ntrouble his tulip-ears to listen.\r\n\r\nDick was permitted to see the girls off. They were going by the Dover\r\nnight-boat; and they hoped to return in August. It was then February,\r\nand Dick felt that he was being hardly used. Maisie was so busy\r\nstripping the small house across the Park, and packing her canvases,\r\nthat she had not time for thought. Dick went down to Dover and wasted a\r\nday there fretting over a wonderful possibility. Would Maisie at the\r\nvery last allow him one small kiss? He reflected that he might capture\r\nher by the strong arm, as he had seem women captured in the Southern\r\nSoudan, and lead her away; but Maisie would never be led. She would\r\nturn her gray eyes upon him and say, \xe2\x80\x9cDick, how selfish you are!\xe2\x80\x9d Then\r\nhis courage would fail him. It would be better, after all, to beg for\r\nthat kiss.\r\n\r\nMaisie looked more than usually kissable as she stepped from the\r\nnight-mail on to the windy pier, in a gray waterproof and a little gray\r\ncloth travelling-cap. The mail-bags\r\nwere thundering into the forehold, and the red-haired girl was watching\r\nthem.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll have a rough passage to-night,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s blowing\r\noutside. I\xe2\x80\x99d like to\r\ncatch you! If I wasn\xe2\x80\x99t afraid o\xe2\x80\x99 being hung I\xe2\x80\x99d kill you. It is not pleasant to live in the company of a\r\nnotion that will not work out, a fox-terrier that cannot talk, and a\r\nwoman who talks too much. He would have answered, but at that moment\r\nthere unrolled itself from one corner of the studio a veil, as it were,\r\nof the flimsiest gauze. He rubbed his eyes, but the gray haze would not\r\ngo.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is disgraceful indigestion. Binkie, we will go to a medicine-man.\r\nWe can\xe2\x80\x99t have our eyes interfered with, for by these we get our bread;\r\nalso mutton-chop bones for little dogs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe doctor was an affable local practitioner with white hair, and he\r\nsaid nothing till Dick began to describe the gray film in the studio.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe all want a little patching and repairing from time to time,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nchirped. \xe2\x80\x9cLike a ship, my dear sir,\xe2\x80\x94exactly like a ship. Sometimes the\r\nhull is out of order, and we consult the surgeon; sometimes the\r\nrigging, and then I advise; sometimes the engines, and we go to the\r\nbrain-specialist; sometimes the look-out on the bridge is tired, and\r\nthen we see an oculist. I should recommend you to see an oculist. A\r\nlittle patching and repairing from time to time is all we want. An\r\noculist, by all means.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDick sought an oculist,\xe2\x80\x94the best in London. He was certain that the\r\nlocal practitioner did not know anything about his trade, and more\r\ncertain that Maisie would laugh at him if he were forced to wear\r\nspectacles.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve neglected the warnings of my lord the stomach too long. Hence\r\nthese spots before the eyes, Binkie. I can see as well as I ever\r\ncould.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs he entered the dark hall that led to the consulting-room a man\r\ncannoned against him. Dick saw the face as it hurried out into the\r\nstreet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s the writer-type. He has the same modelling of the forehead as\r\nTorp. He looks very sick. Probably heard something he didn\xe2\x80\x99t like.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEven as he thought, a great fear came upon Dick, a fear that made him\r\nhold his breath as he walked into the oculist\xe2\x80\x99s waiting room, with the\r\nheavy carved furniture, the dark-green paper, and the sober-hued prints\r\non the wall. He recognised a reproduction of one of his own sketches.\r\n\r\nMany people were waiting their turn before him. His eye was caught by a\r\nflaming red-and-gold Christmas-carol book. Little children came to that\r\neye-doctor, and they needed large-type amusement.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s idolatrous bad Art,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, drawing the book towards himself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFrom the anatomy of the angels, it has been made in Germany.\xe2\x80\x9d He\r\nopened in mechanically, and there leaped to his eyes a verse printed in\r\nred ink\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\nThe next good joy that Mary had,\r\n    It was the joy of three,\r\nTo see her good Son Jesus Christ\r\n    Making the blind to see;\r\nMaking the blind to see, good Lord,\r\n    And happy we may be.\r\nPraise Father, Son, and Holy Ghost\r\n    To all eternity!\r\n\r\n\r\nDick read and re-read the verse till his turn came, and the doctor was\r\nbending above him seated in an arm-chair. The blaze of the\r\ngas-microscope in his eyes made him wince. The doctor\xe2\x80\x99s hand touched\r\nthe scar of the sword-cut on Dick\xe2\x80\x99s head, and Dick explained briefly\r\nhow he had come by it. When the flame was removed, Dick saw the\r\ndoctor\xe2\x80\x99s face, and the fear came upon him again. The doctor wrapped\r\nhimself in a mist of words. Dick caught allusions to \xe2\x80\x9cscar,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cfrontal\r\nbone,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9coptic nerve,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cextreme caution,\xe2\x80\x9d and the \xe2\x80\x9cavoidance of mental\r\nanxiety.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVerdict?\xe2\x80\x9d he said faintly. \xe2\x80\x9cMy business is painting, and I daren\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nwaste time. What do you make of it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAgain the whirl of words, but this time they conveyed a meaning.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan you give me anything to drink?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMany sentences were pronounced in that darkened room, and the prisoners\r\noften needed cheering. Dick found a glass of liqueur brandy in his\r\nhand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs far as I can gather,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, coughing above the spirit, \xe2\x80\x9cyou call\r\nit decay of the optic nerve, or something, and therefore hopeless. What\r\nis my time-limit, avoiding all strain and worry?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps one year.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy God! And if I don\xe2\x80\x99t take care of myself?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI really could not say. One cannot ascertain the exact amount of\r\ninjury inflicted by the sword-cut. The scar is an old one, and\xe2\x80\x94exposure\r\nto the strong light of the desert, did you say?\xe2\x80\x94with excessive\r\napplication to fine work? I really could not say?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI beg your pardon, but it has come without any warning. If you will\r\nlet me, I\xe2\x80\x99ll sit here for a minute, and then I\xe2\x80\x99ll go. You have been\r\nvery good in telling me the truth. Without any warning; without any\r\nwarning.\r\n\r\nThanks.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDick went into the street, and was rapturously received by Binkie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ve got it very badly, little dog! Just as badly as we can get it.\r\nWe\xe2\x80\x99ll go to the Park to think it out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey headed for a certain tree that Dick knew well, and they sat down\r\nto think, because his legs were trembling under him and there was cold\r\nfear at the pit of his stomach.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow could it have come without any warning? It\xe2\x80\x99s as sudden as being\r\nshot. It\xe2\x80\x99s the living death, Binkie. We\xe2\x80\x99re to be shut up in the dark in\r\none year if we\xe2\x80\x99re careful, and we shan\xe2\x80\x99t see anybody, and we shall\r\nnever have anything we want, not though we live to be a hundred!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nBinkie wagged his tail joyously. \xe2\x80\x9cBinkie, we must think. Let\xe2\x80\x99s see how\r\nit feels to be blind.\xe2\x80\x9d Dick shut his eyes, and flaming commas and\r\nCatherine-wheels floated inside the lids. Yet when he looked across the\r\nPark the scope of his vision was not contracted. He could see\r\nperfectly, until a procession of slow-wheeling fireworks defiled across\r\nhis eyeballs.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLittle dorglums, we aren\xe2\x80\x99t at all well. Let\xe2\x80\x99s go home. If only Torp\r\nwere back, now!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Torpenhow was in the south of England, inspecting dockyards in the\r\ncompany of the Nilghai. His letters were brief and full of mystery.\r\n\r\nDick had never asked anybody to help him in his joys or his sorrows. He\r\nargued, in the loneliness of his studio, henceforward to be decorated\r\nwith a film of gray gauze in one corner, that, if his fate were\r\nblindness, all the Torpenhows in the world could not save him. \xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ncall him off his trip to sit down and sympathise with me. I must pull\r\nthrough this business alone,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. He was lying on the sofa, eating\r\nhis moustache and wondering what the darkness of the night would be\r\nlike. Then came to his mind the memory of a quaint scene in the Soudan.\r\nA soldier had been nearly hacked in two by a broad-bladed Arab spear.\r\nFor one instant the man felt no pain. Looking down, he saw that his\r\nlife-blood was going from him. The stupid bewilderment on his face was\r\nso intensely comic that both Dick and Torpenhow, still panting and\r\nunstrung from a fight for life, had roared with laughter, in which the\r\nman seemed as if he would join, but, as his lips parted in a sheepish\r\ngrin, the agony of death came upon him, and he pitched grunting at\r\ntheir feet. Dick laughed again, remembering the horror. It seemed so\r\nexactly like his own case.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I have a little more time allowed me,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. He paced up and\r\ndown the room, quietly at first, but afterwards with the hurried feet\r\nof fear. It was as though a black shadow stood at his elbow and urged\r\nhim to go forward; and there were only weaving circles and floating\r\npin-dots before his eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe need to be calm, Binkie; we must be calm.\xe2\x80\x9d He talked aloud for the\r\nsake of distraction. \xe2\x80\x9cThis isn\xe2\x80\x99t nice at all. What shall we do? We must\r\ndo something. Our time is short. I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have believed that this\r\nmorning; but now things are different. Binkie, where was Moses when the\r\nlight went out?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBinkie smiled from ear to ear, as a well-bred terrier should, but made\r\nno suggestion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x9cWere there but world enough and time, This coyness, Binkie, were not\r\ncrime.... But at my back I always hear\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99 He wiped his forehead, which\r\nwas unpleasantly damp. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat can I do? What can I do? I haven\xe2\x80\x99t any\r\nnotions left, and I can\xe2\x80\x99t think connectedly, but I must do something,\r\nor I shall go off my head.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe hurried walk recommenced, Dick stopping every now and again to drag\r\nforth long-neglected canvases and old note-books; for he turned to his\r\nwork by instinct, as a thing that could not fail. \xe2\x80\x9cYou won\xe2\x80\x99t do, and\r\nyou won\xe2\x80\x99t do,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, at each inspection. \xe2\x80\x9cNo more soldiers. I\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t paint \xe2\x80\x99em. Sudden death comes home too nearly, and this is\r\nbattle and murder for me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe day was failing, and Dick thought for a moment that the twilight of\r\nthe blind had come upon him unaware. \xe2\x80\x9cAllah Almighty!\xe2\x80\x9d he cried\r\ndespairingly, \xe2\x80\x9chelp me through the time of waiting, and I won\xe2\x80\x99t whine\r\nwhen my punishment comes. What can I do now, before the light goes?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was no answer. Dick waited till he could regain some sort of\r\ncontrol over himself. His hands were shaking, and he prided himself on\r\ntheir steadiness; he could feel that his lips were quivering, and the\r\nsweat was running down his face. He was lashed by fear, driven forward\r\nby the desire to get to work at once and accomplish something, and\r\nmaddened by the refusal of his brain to do more than repeat the news\r\nthat he was about to go blind. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a humiliating exhibition,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nthought, \xe2\x80\x9cand I\xe2\x80\x99m glad Torp isn\xe2\x80\x99t here to see. The doctor said I was to\r\navoid mental worry.\r\n\r\nCome here and let me pet you, Binkie.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe little dog yelped because Dick nearly squeezed the bark out of him.\r\n\r\nThen he heard the man speaking in the twilight, and, doglike,\r\nunderstood that his trouble stood off from him\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cAllah is good, Binkie.\r\nNot quite so gentle as we could wish, but we\xe2\x80\x99ll discuss that later. I\r\nthink I see my way to it now. All those studies of Bessie\xe2\x80\x99s head were\r\nnonsense, and they nearly brought your master into a scrape. I hold the\r\nnotion now as clear as crystal,\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cthe Melancolia that transcends all\r\nwit.\xe2\x80\x9d There shall be Maisie in that head, because I shall never get\r\nMaisie; and Bess, of course, because she knows all about Melancolia,\r\nthough she doesn\xe2\x80\x99t know she knows; and there shall be some drawing in\r\nit, and it shall all end up with a laugh. That\xe2\x80\x99s for myself. Shall she\r\ngiggle or grin? No, she shall laugh right out of the canvas, and every\r\nman and woman that ever had a sorrow of their own shall\xe2\x80\x94what is it the\r\npoem says?\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUnderstand the speech and feel a stir\r\nOf fellowship in all disastrous fight.\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn all disastrous fight\xe2\x80\x9d? That\xe2\x80\x99s better than painting the thing merely\r\nto pique Maisie. I can do it now because I have it inside me. Binkie,\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99m going to hold you up by your tail. You\xe2\x80\x99re an omen. Come here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBinkie swung head downward for a moment without speaking.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRather like holding a guinea-pig; but you\xe2\x80\x99re a brave little dog, and\r\nyou don\xe2\x80\x99t yelp when you\xe2\x80\x99re hung up. It is an omen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBinkie went to his own chair, and as often as he looked saw Dick\r\nwalking up and down, rubbing his hands and chuckling. That night Dick\r\nwrote a letter to Maisie full of the tenderest regard for her health,\r\nbut saying very little about his own, and dreamed of the Melancolia to\r\nbe born. Not till morning did he remember that something might happen\r\nto him in the future.\r\n\r\nHe fell to work, whistling softly, and was swallowed up in the clean,\r\nclear joy of creation, which does not come to man too often, lest he\r\nshould consider himself the equal of his God, and so refuse to die at\r\nthe appointed time. He forgot Maisie, Torpenhow, and Binkie at his\r\nfeet, but remembered to stir Bessie, who needed very little stirring,\r\ninto a tremendous rage, that he might watch the smouldering lights in\r\nher eyes.\r\n\r\nHe threw himself without reservation into his work, and did not think\r\nof the doom that was to overtake him, for he was possessed with his\r\nnotion, and the things of this world had no power upon him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re pleased to-day,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bessie.\r\n\r\nDick waved his mahl-stick in mystic circles and went to the sideboard\r\nfor a drink. In the evening, when the exaltation of the day had died\r\ndown, he went to the sideboard again, and after some visits became\r\nconvinced that the eye-doctor was a liar, since he could still see\r\neverything very clearly.\r\n\r\nHe was of opinion that he would even make a home for Maisie, and that\r\nwhether she liked it or not she should be his wife. The mood passed\r\nnext morning, but the sideboard and all upon it remained for his\r\ncomfort.\r\n\r\nAgain he set to work, and his eyes troubled him with spots and dashes\r\nand blurs till he had taken counsel with the sideboard, and the\r\nMelancolia both on the canvas and in his own mind appeared lovelier\r\nthan ever. There was a delightful sense of irresponsibility upon him,\r\nsuch as they feel who walking among their fellow-men know that the\r\ndeath-sentence of disease is upon them, and, seeing that fear is but\r\nwaste of the little time left, are riotously happy. The days passed\r\nwithout event.\r\n\r\nBessie arrived punctually always, and, though her voice seemed to Dick\r\nto come from a distance, her face was always very near. The Melancolia\r\nbegan to flame on the canvas, in the likeness of a woman who had known\r\nall the sorrow in the world and was laughing at it. It was true that\r\nthe corners of the studio draped themselves in gray film and retired\r\ninto the darkness, that the spots in his eyes and the pains across his\r\nhead were very troublesome, and that Maisie\xe2\x80\x99s letters were hard to read\r\nand harder still to answer. He could not tell her of his trouble, and\r\nhe could not laugh at her accounts of her own Melancolia which was\r\nalways going to be finished. But the furious days of toil and the\r\nnights of wild dreams made amends for all, and the sideboard was his\r\nbest friend on earth.\r\n\r\nBessie was singularly dull. She used to shriek with rage when Dick\r\nstared at her between half-closed eyes. Now she sulked, or watched him\r\nwith disgust, saying very little.\r\n\r\nTorpenhow had been absent for six weeks. An incoherent note heralded\r\nhis return. \xe2\x80\x9cNews! great news!\xe2\x80\x9d he wrote. \xe2\x80\x9cThe Nilghai knows, and so\r\ndoes the Keneu. We\xe2\x80\x99re all back on Thursday. Get lunch and clean your\r\naccoutrements.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDick showed Bessie the letter, and she abused him for that he had ever\r\nsent Torpenhow away and ruined her life.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick, brutally, \xe2\x80\x9cyou\xe2\x80\x99re better as you are, instead of\r\nmaking love to some drunken beast in the street.\xe2\x80\x9d He felt that he had\r\nrescued Torpenhow from great temptation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know if that\xe2\x80\x99s any worse than sitting to a drunken beast in a\r\nstudio. _You_ haven\xe2\x80\x99t been sober for three weeks. You\xe2\x80\x99ve been soaking\r\nthe whole time; and yet you pretend you\xe2\x80\x99re better than me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat d\xe2\x80\x99you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMean! You\xe2\x80\x99ll see when Mr. Torpenhow comes back.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was not long to wait. Torpenhow met Bessie on the staircase without\r\na sign of feeling. He had news that was more to him than many Bessies,\r\nand the Keneu and the Nilghai were trampling behind him, calling for\r\nDick.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDrinking like a fish,\xe2\x80\x9d Bessie whispered. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s been at it for nearly a\r\nmonth.\xe2\x80\x9d She followed the men stealthily to hear judgment done.\r\n\r\nThey came into the studio, rejoicing, to be welcomed over effusively by\r\na drawn, lined, shrunken, haggard wreck,\xe2\x80\x94unshaven, blue-white about the\r\nnostrils, stooping in the shoulders, and peering under his eyebrows\r\nnervously. The drink had been at work as steadily as Dick.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs this you?\xe2\x80\x9d said Torpenhow.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll that\xe2\x80\x99s left of me. Sit down. Binkie\xe2\x80\x99s quite well, and I\xe2\x80\x99ve been\r\ndoing some good work.\xe2\x80\x9d He reeled where he stood.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve done some of the worst work you\xe2\x80\x99ve ever done in your life. Man\r\nalive, you\xe2\x80\x99re\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTorpenhow turned to his companions appealingly, and they left the room\r\nto find lunch elsewhere. Then he spoke; but, since the reproof of a\r\nfriend is much too sacred and intimate a thing to be printed, and since\r\nTorpenhow used figures and metaphors which were unseemly, and contempt\r\nuntranslatable, it will never be known what was actually said to Dick,\r\nwho blinked and winked and picked at his hands. After a time the\r\nculprit began to feel the need of a little self-respect. He was quite\r\nsure that he had not in any way departed from virtue, and there were\r\nreasons, too, of which Torpenhow knew nothing. He would explain.\r\n\r\nHe rose, tried to straighten his shoulders, and spoke to the face he\r\ncould hardly see.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are right,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I am right, too. After you went away I\r\nhad some trouble with my eyes. So I went to an oculist, and he turned a\r\ngasogene\xe2\x80\x94I mean a gas-engine\xe2\x80\x94into my eye. That was very long ago. He\r\nsaid, \xe2\x80\x9cScar on the head,\xe2\x80\x94sword-cut and optic nerve.\xe2\x80\x9d Make a note of\r\nthat. So I am going blind. I have some work to do before I go blind,\r\nand I suppose that I must do it. I cannot see much now, but I can see\r\nbest when I am drunk. I did not know I was drunk till I was told, but I\r\nmust go on with my work. If you want to see it, there it is.\xe2\x80\x9d He\r\npointed to the all but finished Melancolia and looked for applause.\r\n\r\nTorpenhow said nothing, and Dick began to whimper feebly, for joy at\r\nseeing Torpenhow again, for grief at misdeeds\xe2\x80\x94if indeed they were\r\nmisdeeds\xe2\x80\x94that made Torpenhow remote and unsympathetic, and for childish\r\nvanity hurt, since Torpenhow had not given a word of praise to his\r\nwonderful picture.\r\n\r\nBessie looked through the keyhole after a long pause, and saw the two\r\nwalking up and down as usual, Torpenhow\xe2\x80\x99s hand on Dick\xe2\x80\x99s shoulder.\r\n\r\nHereat she said something so improper that it shocked even Binkie, who\r\nwas dribbling patiently on the landing with the hope of seeing his\r\nmaster again.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XI\r\n\r\n\r\nThe lark will make her hymn to God,\r\n    The partridge call her brood,\r\nWhile I forget the heath I trod,\r\n    The fields wherein I stood.\r\n\xe2\x80\x99Tis dule to know not night from morn,\r\n    But deeper dule to know\r\nI can but hear the hunter\xe2\x80\x99s horn\r\n    That once I used to blow.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x94_The Only Son_.\r\n\r\n\r\nIt was the third day after Torpenhow\xe2\x80\x99s return, and his heart was heavy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you mean to tell me that you can\xe2\x80\x99t see to work without whiskey?\r\nIt\xe2\x80\x99s generally the other way about.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan a drunkard swear on his honour?\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, if he has been as good a man as you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I give you my word of honour,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick, speaking hurriedly\r\nthrough parched lips. \xe2\x80\x9cOld man, I can hardly see your face now. You\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\nkept me sober for two days,\xe2\x80\x94if I ever was drunk,\xe2\x80\x94and I\xe2\x80\x99ve done no work.\r\n\r\nDon\xe2\x80\x99t keep me back any more. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know when my eyes may give out.\r\n\r\nThe spots and dots and the pains and things are crowding worse than\r\never. Pity you didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nkeep the odd eye. It would have been useful. Any letters for me? Give\r\nme all the ones in fat gray envelopes with a sort of crown thing\r\noutside. They\xe2\x80\x99re of no importance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTorpenhow gave him a letter with a black M. on the envelope flap. Dick\r\nput it into his pocket. There was nothing in it that Torpenhow might\r\nnot have read, but it belonged to himself and to Maisie, who would\r\nnever belong to him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen she finds that I don\xe2\x80\x99t write, she\xe2\x80\x99ll stop writing. It\xe2\x80\x99s better\r\nso. I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t be any use to her now,\xe2\x80\x9d Dick argued, and the tempter\r\nsuggested that he should make known his condition. Every nerve in him\r\nrevolted. \xe2\x80\x9cI have fallen low enough already. I\xe2\x80\x99m not going to beg for\r\npity. Besides, it would be cruel to her.\xe2\x80\x9d He strove to put Maisie out\r\nof his thoughts; but the blind have many opportunities for thinking,\r\nand as the tides of his strength came back to him in the long\r\nemployless days of dead darkness, Dick\xe2\x80\x99s soul was troubled to the core.\r\nAnother letter, and another, came from Maisie. Then there was silence,\r\nand Dick sat by the window, the pulse of summer in the air, and\r\npictured her being won by another man, stronger than himself. His\r\nimagination, the keener for the dark background it worked against,\r\nspared him no single detail that might send him raging up and down the\r\nstudio, to stumble over the stove that seemed to be in four places at\r\nonce. Worst of all, tobacco would not taste in the darkness. The\r\narrogance of the man had disappeared, and in its place were settled\r\ndespair that Torpenhow knew, and blind passion that Dick confided to\r\nhis pillow at night. The intervals between the paroxysms were filled\r\nwith intolerable waiting and the weight of intolerable darkness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome out into the Park,\xe2\x80\x9d said Torpenhow. \xe2\x80\x9cYou haven\xe2\x80\x99t stirred out\r\nsince the beginning of things.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the use? There\xe2\x80\x99s no movement in the dark; and, besides,\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he\r\npaused irresolutely at the head of the stairs,\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9csomething will run over\r\nme.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot if I\xe2\x80\x99m with you. Proceed gingerly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe roar of the streets filled Dick with nervous terror, and he clung\r\nto Torpenhow\xe2\x80\x99s arm. \xe2\x80\x9cFancy having to feel for a gutter with your foot!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe said petulantly, as he turned into the Park. \xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s curse God and\r\ndie.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSentries are forbidden to pay unauthorised compliments. By Jove, there\r\nare the Guards!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDick\xe2\x80\x99s figure straightened. \xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s get near \xe2\x80\x99em. Let\xe2\x80\x99s go in and look.\r\nLet\xe2\x80\x99s get on the grass and run. I can smell the trees.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMind the low railing. That\xe2\x80\x99s all right!\xe2\x80\x9d Torpenhow kicked out a tuft\r\nof grass with his heel. \xe2\x80\x9cSmell that,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cIsn\xe2\x80\x99t it good?\xe2\x80\x9d Dick\r\nsniffed luxuriously. \xe2\x80\x9cNow pick up your feet and run.\xe2\x80\x9d They approached\r\nas near to the regiment as was possible. The clank of bayonets being\r\nunfixed made Dick\xe2\x80\x99s nostrils quiver.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s get nearer. They\xe2\x80\x99re in column, aren\xe2\x80\x99t they?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. How did you know?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFelt it. Oh, my men!\xe2\x80\x94my beautiful men!\xe2\x80\x9d He edged forward as though he\r\ncould see. \xe2\x80\x9cI could draw those chaps once. Who\xe2\x80\x99ll draw \xe2\x80\x99em now?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99ll move off in a minute. Don\xe2\x80\x99t jump when the band begins.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHuh! I\xe2\x80\x99m not a new charger. It\xe2\x80\x99s the silences that hurt. Nearer,\r\nTorp!\xe2\x80\x94nearer! Oh, my God, what wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t I give to see \xe2\x80\x99em for a\r\nminute!\xe2\x80\x94one half-minute!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe could hear the armed life almost within reach of him, could hear the\r\nslings tighten across the bandsman\xe2\x80\x99s chest as he heaved the big drum\r\nfrom the ground.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSticks crossed above his head,\xe2\x80\x9d whispered Torpenhow.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know. _I_ know! Who should know if I don\xe2\x80\x99t? H\xe2\x80\x99sh!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe drum-sticks fell with a boom, and the men swung forward to the\r\ncrash of the band. Dick felt the wind of the massed movement in his\r\nface, heard the maddening tramp of feet and the friction of the pouches\r\non the belts. The big drum pounded out the tune. It was a music-hall\r\nrefrain that made a perfect quickstep\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\nHe must be a man of decent height,\r\n    He must be a man of weight,\r\nHe must come home on a Saturday night\r\n    In a thoroughly sober state;\r\nHe must know how to love me,\r\n    And he must know how to kiss;\r\nAnd if he\xe2\x80\x99s enough to keep us both\r\n    I can\xe2\x80\x99t refuse him bliss.\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the matter?\xe2\x80\x9d said Torpenhow, as he saw Dick\xe2\x80\x99s head fall when\r\nthe last of the regiment had departed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing. I feel a little bit out of the running,\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s all. Torp,\r\ntake me back. Why did you bring me out?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XII\r\n\r\n\r\nThere were three friends that buried the fourth,\r\n    The mould in his mouth and the dust in his eyes\r\nAnd they went south and east, and north,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n    The strong man fights, but the sick man dies.\r\n\r\nThere were three friends that spoke of the dead,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n    The strong man fights, but the sick man dies.\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd would he were with us now,\xe2\x80\x9d they said,\r\n    \xe2\x80\x9cThe sun in our face and the wind in our eyes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x94_Ballad_.\r\n\r\n\r\nThe Nilghai was angry with Torpenhow. Dick had been sent to bed,\xe2\x80\x94blind\r\nmen are ever under the orders of those who can see,\xe2\x80\x94and since he had\r\nreturned from the Park had fluently sworn at Torpenhow because he was\r\nalive, and all the world because it was alive and could see, while he,\r\nDick, was dead in the death of the blind, who, at the best, are only\r\nburdens upon their associates. Torpenhow had said something about a\r\nMrs. Gummidge, and Dick had retired in a black fury to handle and\r\nre-handle three unopened letters from Maisie.\r\n\r\nThe Nilghai, fat, burly, and aggressive, was in Torpenhow\xe2\x80\x99s rooms.\r\n\r\nBehind him sat the Keneu, the Great War Eagle, and between them lay a\r\nlarge map embellished with black-and-white-headed pins.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was wrong about the Balkans,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Nilghai. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I\xe2\x80\x99m not wrong\r\nabout this business. The whole of our work in the Southern Soudan must\r\nbe done over again. The public doesn\xe2\x80\x99t care, of course, but the\r\ngovernment does, and they are making their arrangements quietly. You\r\nknow that as well as I do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI remember how the people cursed us when our troops withdrew from\r\nOmdurman. It was bound to crop up sooner or later. But I can\xe2\x80\x99t go,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Torpenhow. He pointed through the open door; it was a hot night.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan you blame me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Keneu purred above his pipe like a large and very happy cat\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nblame you in the least. It\xe2\x80\x99s uncommonly good of you, and all the rest\r\nof it, but every man\xe2\x80\x94even you, Torp\xe2\x80\x94must consider his work. I know it\r\nsounds brutal, but Dick\xe2\x80\x99s out of the race,\xe2\x80\x94down,\xe2\x80\x94_gastados_, expended,\r\nfinished, done for. He has a little money of his own. He won\xe2\x80\x99t starve,\r\nand you can\xe2\x80\x99t pull out of your slide for his sake. Think of your own\r\nreputation.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDick\xe2\x80\x99s was five times bigger than mine and yours put together.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was because he signed his name to everything he did. It\xe2\x80\x99s all\r\nended now. You must hold yourself in readiness to move out. You can\r\ncommand your own prices, and you do better work than any three of us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t tell me how tempting it is. I\xe2\x80\x99ll stay here to look after Dick\r\nfor a while. He\xe2\x80\x99s as cheerful as a bear with a sore head, but I think\r\nhe likes to have me near him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Nilghai said something uncomplimentary about soft-headed fools who\r\nthrow away their careers for other fools. Torpenhow flushed angrily.\r\nThe constant strain of attendance on Dick had worn his nerves thin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere remains a third fate,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Keneu, thoughtfully. \xe2\x80\x9cConsider\r\nthis, and be not larger fools than necessary. Dick is\xe2\x80\x94or rather was\xe2\x80\x94an\r\nable-bodied man of moderate attractions and a certain amount of\r\naudacity.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOho!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Nilghai, who remembered an affair at Cairo. \xe2\x80\x9cI begin to\r\nsee,\xe2\x80\x94Torp, I\xe2\x80\x99m sorry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTorpenhow nodded forgiveness: \xe2\x80\x9cYou were more sorry when he cut you out,\r\nthough.\xe2\x80\x94Go on, Keneu.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve often thought, when I\xe2\x80\x99ve seen men die out in the desert, that if\r\nthe news could be sent through the world, and the means of transport\r\nwere quick enough, there would be one woman at least at each man\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nbedside.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere would be some mighty quaint revelations. Let us be grateful\r\nthings are as they are,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Nilghai.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet us rather reverently consider whether Torp\xe2\x80\x99s three-cornered\r\nministrations are exactly what Dick needs just now.\xe2\x80\x94What do you think\r\nyourself, Torp?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know they aren\xe2\x80\x99t. We are all Dick\xe2\x80\x99s friends here.\r\nYou\xe2\x80\x99ve been most in his life.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I picked it up when he was off his head.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe greater chance of its being true. I thought we should arrive. Who\r\nis she?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen Torpenhow told a tale in plain words, as a special correspondent\r\nwho knows how to make a verbal _pr\xc3\xa9cis_ should tell it. The men\r\nlistened without interruption.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it possible that a man can come back across the years to his\r\ncalf-love?\xe2\x80\x9d said the Keneu. \xe2\x80\x9cIs it possible?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI give the facts. He says nothing about it now, but he sits fumbling\r\nthree letters from her when he thinks I\xe2\x80\x99m not looking. What am I to\r\ndo?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSpeak to him,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Nilghai.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh yes! Write to her,\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t know her full name, remember,\xe2\x80\x94and ask\r\nher to accept him out of pity. I believe you once told Dick you were\r\nsorry for him, Nilghai. I honestly believe he\xe2\x80\x99d try to kill you; and the\r\nblindness has made him rather muscular.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTorpenhow\xe2\x80\x99s course is perfectly clear,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Keneu. \xe2\x80\x9cHe will go to\r\nVitry-sur-Marne, which is on the Bezieres-Landes Railway,\xe2\x80\x94single track\r\nfrom Tourgas. The Prussians shelled it out in \xe2\x80\x9970 because there was a\r\npoplar on the top of a hill eighteen hundred yards from the church\r\nspire There\xe2\x80\x99s a squadron of cavalry quartered there,\xe2\x80\x94or ought to be.\r\nWhere this studio Torp spoke about may be I cannot tell. That is Torp\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nbusiness. I have given him his route. He will dispassionately explain\r\nthe situation to the girl, and she will come back to Dick,\xe2\x80\x94the more\r\nespecially because, to use Dick\xe2\x80\x99s words, \xe2\x80\x9cthere is nothing but her\r\ndamned obstinacy to keep them apart.\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd they have four hundred and twenty pounds a year between \xe2\x80\x99em. Dick\r\nnever lost his head for figures, even in his delirium. I\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t drag her back by the hair.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOur business\xe2\x80\x94the business for which we draw our money\xe2\x80\x94is to do absurd\r\nand impossible things,\xe2\x80\x94generally with no reason whatever except to\r\namuse the public. Here we have a reason. The rest doesn\xe2\x80\x99t matter. I\r\nshall share these rooms with the Nilghai till Torpenhow returns. There\r\nwill be a batch of unbridled \xe2\x80\x9cspecials\xe2\x80\x9d coming to town in a little\r\nwhile, and these will serve as their headquarters. Another reason for\r\nsending Torpenhow away. It\xe2\x80\x99s your only chance of\r\ngetting away; and Dick will be grateful.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe will,\xe2\x80\x94worse luck! I can but go and try. You\r\nhad better not be here to-morrow afternoon, because the Nilghai and I\r\nwill be in possession. It is an order. Obey.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDick,\xe2\x80\x9d said Torpenhow, next morning, \xe2\x80\x9ccan I do anything for you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo! Leave me alone. He wants a lighter step.\xe2\x80\x9d Then, aloud, \xe2\x80\x9cVery well.\r\nSince you\xe2\x80\x99re so independent, I\xe2\x80\x99m going off for four or five days. Say\r\ngood-bye at least.
