You\xe2\x80\x99ll have to do without me in a little time, and you\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nbe glad I\xe2\x80\x99m gone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDick felt his way back to the big chair, and wondered what these things\r\nmight mean. He did not wish to be tended by the housekeeper, and yet\r\nTorpenhow\xe2\x80\x99s constant tenderness jarred on him. He did not exactly know\r\nwhat he wanted. The darkness would not lift, and Maisie\xe2\x80\x99s unopened\r\nletters felt worn and old from much handling. He could never read them\r\nfor himself as long as life endured; but Maisie might have sent him\r\nsome fresh ones to play with. The Nilghai entered with a gift,\xe2\x80\x94a piece\r\nof red modelling-wax. He fancied that Dick might find interest in using\r\nhis hands. Dick poked and patted the stuff for a few minutes, and, \xe2\x80\x9cIs\r\nit like anything in the world?\xe2\x80\x9d he said drearily. \xe2\x80\x9cTake it away. I may\r\nget the touch of the blind in fifty years. Do you know where Torpenhow\r\nhas gone?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Nilghai knew nothing. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re staying in his rooms till he comes\r\nback. Can we do anything for you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d like to be left alone, please. Don\xe2\x80\x99t think I\xe2\x80\x99m ungrateful; but I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\nbest alone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Nilghai chuckled, and Dick resumed his drowsy brooding and sullen\r\nrebellion against fate. He had long since ceased to think about the\r\nwork he had done in the old days, and the desire to do more work had\r\ndeparted from him. He was exceedingly sorry for himself, and the\r\ncompleteness of his tender grief soothed him. But his soul and his body\r\ncried for Maisie\xe2\x80\x94Maisie who would understand. His mind pointed out that\r\nMaisie, having her own work to do, would not care. His experience had\r\ntaught him that when money was exhausted women went away, and that when\r\na man was knocked out of the race the others trampled on him. \xe2\x80\x9cThen at\r\nthe least,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick, in reply, \xe2\x80\x9cshe could use me as I used Binat,\xe2\x80\x94for\r\nsome sort of a study. I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t ask more than to be near her again,\r\neven though I knew that another man was making love to her. Ugh! what a\r\ndog I am!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA voice on the staircase began to sing joyfully\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen we go\xe2\x80\x94go\xe2\x80\x94go away from here,\r\n    Our creditors will weep and they will wail,\r\nOur absence much regretting when they find that they\xe2\x80\x99ve been getting\r\n    Out of England by next Tuesday\xe2\x80\x99s Indian mail.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nFollowing the trampling of feet, slamming of Torpenhow\xe2\x80\x99s door, and the\r\nsound of voices in strenuous debate, some one squeaked, \xe2\x80\x9cAnd see, you\r\ngood fellows, I have found a new water-bottle\xe2\x80\x94firs\xe2\x80\x99-class patent\xe2\x80\x94eh,\r\nhow you say? Open himself inside out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDick sprang to his feet. He knew the voice well. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s Cassavetti,\r\ncome back from the Continent. Now I know why Torp went away. There\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\nrow somewhere, and\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99m out of it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Nilghai commanded silence in vain. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s for my sake,\xe2\x80\x9d Dick said\r\nbitterly. \xe2\x80\x9cThe birds are getting ready to fly, and they wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t tell\r\nme. I can hear Morten-Sutherland and Mackaye. Half the War\r\nCorrespondents in London are there;\xe2\x80\x94and I\xe2\x80\x99m out of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe stumbled across the landing and plunged into Torpenhow\xe2\x80\x99s room. He\r\ncould feel that it was full of men. \xe2\x80\x9cWhere\xe2\x80\x99s the trouble?\xe2\x80\x9d said he. \xe2\x80\x9cIn\r\nthe Balkans at last? Why didn\xe2\x80\x99t some one tell me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe thought you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be interested,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Nilghai,\r\nshamefacedly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s in the Soudan, as usual.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou lucky dogs! Let me sit here while you talk. I shan\xe2\x80\x99t be a skeleton\r\nat the feast.\xe2\x80\x94Cassavetti, where are you? Your English is as bad as\r\never.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDick was led into a chair. He heard the rustle of the maps, and the\r\ntalk swept forward, carrying him with it. Everybody spoke at once,\r\ndiscussing press censorships, railway-routes, transport, water-supply,\r\nthe capacities of generals,\xe2\x80\x94these in language that would have horrified\r\na trusting public,\xe2\x80\x94ranging, asserting, denouncing, and laughing at the\r\ntop of their voices. There was the glorious certainty of war in the\r\nSoudan at any moment. The Nilghai said so, and it was well to be in\r\nreadiness. The Keneu had telegraphed to Cairo for horses; Cassavetti\r\nhad stolen a perfectly inaccurate list of troops that would be ordered\r\nforward, and was reading it out amid profane interruptions, and the\r\nKeneu introduced to Dick some man unknown who would be employed as war\r\nartist by the Central Southern Syndicate. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s his first outing,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nthe Keneu. \xe2\x80\x9cGive him some tips\xe2\x80\x94about riding camels.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, those camels!\xe2\x80\x9d groaned Cassavetti. \xe2\x80\x9cI shall learn to ride him\r\nagain, and now I am so much all soft! Listen, you good fellows. I know\r\nyour military arrangement very well. There will go the Royal Argalshire\r\nSutherlanders. So it was read to me upon best authority.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA roar of laughter interrupted him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSit down,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Nilghai. \xe2\x80\x9cThe lists aren\xe2\x80\x99t even made out in the\r\nWar Office.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill there be any force at Suakin?\xe2\x80\x9d said a voice.\r\n\r\nThen the outcries redoubled, and grew mixed, thus: \xe2\x80\x9cHow many Egyptian\r\ntroops will they use?\xe2\x80\x94God help the Fellaheen!\xe2\x80\x94There\xe2\x80\x99s a railway in\r\nPlumstead marshes doing duty as a fives-court.\xe2\x80\x94We shall have the\r\nSuakin-Berber line built at last.\xe2\x80\x94Canadian voyageurs are too careful.\r\nGive me a half-drunk Krooman in a whale-boat.\xe2\x80\x94Who commands the Desert\r\ncolumn?\xe2\x80\x94No, they never blew up the big rock in the Ghineh bend. We\r\nshall have to be hauled up, as usual.\xe2\x80\x94Somebody tell me if there\xe2\x80\x99s an\r\nIndian contingent, or I\xe2\x80\x99ll break everybody\xe2\x80\x99s head.\xe2\x80\x94Don\xe2\x80\x99t tear the map\r\nin two.\xe2\x80\x94It\xe2\x80\x99s a war of occupation, I tell you, to connect with the\r\nAfrican companies in the South.\xe2\x80\x94There\xe2\x80\x99s Guinea-worm in most of the\r\nwells on that route.\xe2\x80\x9d Then the Nilghai, despairing of peace, bellowed\r\nlike a fog-horn and beat upon the table with both hands.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what becomes of Torpenhow?\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick, in the silence that\r\nfollowed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTorp\xe2\x80\x99s in abeyance just now. He\xe2\x80\x99s off love-making somewhere, I\r\nsuppose,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Nilghai.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe said he was going to stay at home,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Keneu.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs he?\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick, with an oath. \xe2\x80\x9cHe won\xe2\x80\x99t. I\xe2\x80\x99m not much good now, but\r\nif you and the Nilghai hold him down I\xe2\x80\x99ll engage to trample on him till\r\nhe sees reason. He\xe2\x80\x99ll stay behind, indeed! He\xe2\x80\x99s the best of you all.\r\nThere\xe2\x80\x99ll be some tough work by Omdurman. We shall come there to stay,\r\nthis time.\r\n\r\nBut I forgot. I wish I were going with you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo do we all, Dickie,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Keneu.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I most of all,\xe2\x80\x9d said the new artist of the Central Southern\r\nSyndicate.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCould you tell me\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll give you one piece of advice,\xe2\x80\x9d Dick answered, moving towards the\r\ndoor. \xe2\x80\x9cIf you happen to be cut over the head in a scrimmage, don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nguard.\r\n\r\nTell the man to go on cutting. You\xe2\x80\x99ll find it cheapest in the end.\r\nThanks for letting me look in.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s grit in Dick,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Nilghai, an hour later, when the room\r\nwas emptied of all save the Keneu.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was the sacred call of the war-trumpet. Did you notice how he\r\nanswered to it? Poor fellow! Let\xe2\x80\x99s look at him,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Keneu.\r\n\r\nThe excitement of the talk had died away. Dick was sitting by the\r\nstudio table, with his head on his arms, when the men came in. He did\r\nnot change his position.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt hurts,\xe2\x80\x9d he moaned. \xe2\x80\x9cGod forgive me, but it hurts cruelly; and yet,\r\ny\xe2\x80\x99know, the world has a knack of spinning round all by itself. Shall I\r\nsee Torp before he goes?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes. You\xe2\x80\x99ll see him,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Nilghai.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XIII\r\n\r\n\r\nThe sun went down an hour ago,\r\n    I wonder if I face towards home;\r\nIf I lost my way in the light of day\r\n    How shall I find it now night is come?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x94_Old Song_.\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMaisie, come to bed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s so hot I can\xe2\x80\x99t sleep. Don\xe2\x80\x99t worry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMaisie put her elbows on the window-sill and looked at the moonlight on\r\nthe straight, poplar-flanked road. Summer had come upon Vitry-sur-Marne\r\nand parched it to the bone. The grass was dry-burnt in the meadows, the\r\nclay by the bank of the river was caked to brick, the roadside flowers\r\nwere long since dead, and the roses in the garden hung withered on\r\ntheir stalks. The heat in the little low bedroom under the eaves was\r\nalmost intolerable. The very moonlight on the wall of Kami\xe2\x80\x99s studio\r\nacross the road seemed to make the night hotter, and the shadow of the\r\nbig bell-handle by the closed gate cast a bar of inky black that caught\r\nMaisie\xe2\x80\x99s eye and annoyed her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHorrid thing! It should be all white,\xe2\x80\x9d she murmured. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd the gate\r\nisn\xe2\x80\x99t in the middle of the wall, either. I never noticed that before.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMaisie was hard to please at that hour. First, the heat of the past few\r\nweeks had worn her down; secondly, her work, and particularly the study\r\nof a female head intended to represent the Melancolia and not finished\r\nin time for the Salon, was unsatisfactory; thirdly, Kami had said as\r\nmuch two days before; fourthly,\xe2\x80\x94but so completely fourthly that it was\r\nhardly worth thinking about,\xe2\x80\x94Dick, her property, had not written to her\r\nfor more than six weeks. She was angry with the heat, with Kami, and\r\nwith her work, but she was exceedingly angry with Dick.\r\n\r\nShe had written to him three times,\xe2\x80\x94each time proposing a fresh\r\ntreatment of her Melancolia. Dick had taken no notice of these\r\ncommunications. She had resolved to write no more. When she returned to\r\nEngland in the autumn\xe2\x80\x94for her pride\xe2\x80\x99s sake she could not return\r\nearlier\xe2\x80\x94she would speak to him. She missed the Sunday afternoon\r\nconferences more than she cared to admit. All that Kami said was,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Continuez, mademoiselle, continuez toujours_,\xe2\x80\x9d and he had been\r\nrepeating the wearisome counsel through the hot summer, exactly like a\r\ncicada,\xe2\x80\x94an old gray cicada in a black alpaca coat, white trousers, and\r\na huge felt hat. But Dick had tramped masterfully up and down her\r\nlittle studio north of the cool green London park, and had said things\r\nten times worse than _continuez_, before he snatched the brush out of\r\nher hand and showed her where the error lay. His last letter, Maisie\r\nremembered, contained some trivial advice about not sketching in the\r\nsun or drinking water at wayside farmhouses; and he had said that not\r\nonce, but three times,\xe2\x80\x94as if he did not know that Maisie could take\r\ncare of herself.\r\n\r\nBut what was he doing, that he could not trouble to write? A murmur of\r\nvoices in the road made her lean from the window. A cavalryman of the\r\nlittle garrison in the town was talking to Kami\xe2\x80\x99s cook. The moonlight\r\nglittered on the scabbard of his sabre, which he was holding in his\r\nhand lest it should clank inopportunely. The cook\xe2\x80\x99s cap cast deep\r\nshadows on her face, which was close to the conscript\xe2\x80\x99s. He slid his\r\narm round her waist, and there followed the sound of a kiss.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFaugh!\xe2\x80\x9d said Maisie, stepping back.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s that?\xe2\x80\x9d said the red-haired girl, who was tossing uneasily\r\noutside her bed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly a conscript kissing the cook,\xe2\x80\x9d said Maisie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99ve gone away now.\xe2\x80\x9d She leaned out of the window again, and put a\r\nshawl over her nightgown to guard against chills. There was a very\r\nsmall night-breeze abroad, and a sun-baked rose below nodded its head\r\nas one who knew unutterable secrets. Was it possible that Dick should\r\nturn his thoughts from her work and his own and descend to the\r\ndegradation of Suzanne and the conscript? He could not! The rose nodded\r\nits head and one leaf therewith. It looked like a naughty little devil\r\nscratching its ear.\r\n\r\nDick could not, \xe2\x80\x9cbecause,\xe2\x80\x9d thought Maisie, \xe2\x80\x9che is mine,\xe2\x80\x94mine,\xe2\x80\x94mine. He\r\nsaid he was. I\xe2\x80\x99m sure I don\xe2\x80\x99t care what he does. It will only spoil his\r\nwork if he does; and it will spoil mine too.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe rose continued to nod in the futile way peculiar to flowers. There\r\nwas no earthly reason why Dick should not disport himself as he chose,\r\nexcept that he was called by Providence, which was Maisie, to assist\r\nMaisie in her work. And her work was the preparation of pictures that\r\nwent sometimes to English provincial exhibitions, as the notices in the\r\nscrap-book proved, and that were invariably rejected by the Salon when\r\nKami was plagued into allowing her to send them up. Her work in the\r\nfuture, it seemed, would be the preparation of pictures on exactly\r\nsimilar lines which would be rejected in exactly the same way\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94The\r\nred-haired girl threshed distressfully across the sheets. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s too hot\r\nto sleep,\xe2\x80\x9d she moaned; and the interruption jarred.\r\n\r\nExactly the same way. Then she would divide her years between the\r\nlittle studio in England and Kami\xe2\x80\x99s big studio at Vitry-sur-Marne. No,\r\nshe would go to another master, who should force her into the success\r\nthat was her right, if patient toil and desperate endeavour gave one a\r\nright to anything. Dick had told her that he had worked ten years to\r\nunderstand his craft. She had worked ten years, and ten years were\r\nnothing. Dick had said that ten years were nothing,\xe2\x80\x94but that was in\r\nregard to herself only. He had said\xe2\x80\x94this very man who could not find\r\ntime to write\xe2\x80\x94that he would wait ten years for her, and that she was\r\nbound to come back to him sooner or later. He had said this in the\r\nabsurd letter about sunstroke and diphtheria; and then he had stopped\r\nwriting. He was wandering up and down moonlit streets, kissing cooks.\r\nShe would like to lecture him now,\xe2\x80\x94not in her nightgown, of course, but\r\nproperly dressed, severely and from a height. Yet if he was kissing\r\nother girls he certainly would not care whether she lectured him or\r\nnot. He would laugh at her. Very good.\r\n\r\nShe would go back to her studio and prepare pictures that went, etc.,\r\netc.\r\n\r\nThe mill-wheel of thought swung round slowly, that no section of it\r\nmight be slurred over, and the red-haired girl tossed and turned behind\r\nher.\r\n\r\nMaisie put her chin in her hands and decided that there could be no\r\ndoubt whatever of the villainy of Dick. To justify herself, she began,\r\nunwomanly, to weigh the evidence. There was a boy, and he had said he\r\nloved her. And he kissed her,\xe2\x80\x94kissed her on the cheek,\xe2\x80\x94by a yellow\r\nsea-poppy that nodded its head exactly like the maddening dry rose in\r\nthe garden. Then there was an interval, and men had told her that they\r\nloved her\xe2\x80\x94just when she was busiest with her work. Then the boy came\r\nback, and at their very second meeting had told her that he loved her.\r\nThen he had\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94 But there was no end to the things he had done. He had\r\ngiven her his time and his powers. He had spoken to her of Art,\r\nhousekeeping, technique, teacups, the abuse of pickles as a\r\nstimulant,\xe2\x80\x94that was rude,\xe2\x80\x94sable hair-brushes,\xe2\x80\x94he had given her the best\r\nin her stock,\xe2\x80\x94she used them daily; he had given her advice that she\r\nprofited by, and now and again\xe2\x80\x94a look. Such a look! The look of a\r\nbeaten hound waiting for the word to crawl to his mistress\xe2\x80\x99s feet. In\r\nreturn she had given him nothing whatever, except\xe2\x80\x94here she brushed her\r\nmouth against the open-work sleeve of her nightgown\xe2\x80\x94the privilege of\r\nkissing her once. And on the mouth, too. Disgraceful! Was that not\r\nenough, and more than enough? and if it was not, had he not cancelled\r\nthe debt by not writing and\xe2\x80\x94probably kissing other girls? \xe2\x80\x9cMaisie,\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ll catch a chill. Do go and lie down,\xe2\x80\x9d said the wearied voice of\r\nher companion. \xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t sleep a wink with you at the window.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMaisie shrugged her shoulders and did not answer. She was reflecting on\r\nthe meannesses of Dick, and on other meannesses with which he had\r\nnothing to do. The moonlight would not let her sleep. It lay on the\r\nskylight of the studio across the road in cold silver; she stared at it\r\nintently and her thoughts began to slide one into the other. The shadow\r\nof the big bell-handle in the wall grew short, lengthened again, and\r\nfaded out as the moon went down behind the pasture and a hare came\r\nlimping home across the road. Then the dawn-wind washed through the\r\nupland grasses, and brought coolness with it, and the cattle lowed by\r\nthe drought-shrunk river. Maisie\xe2\x80\x99s head fell forward on the\r\nwindow-sill, and the tangle of black hair covered her arms.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMaisie, wake up. You\xe2\x80\x99ll catch a chill.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, dear; yes, dear.\xe2\x80\x9d She staggered to her bed like a wearied child,\r\nand as she buried her face in the pillows she muttered, \xe2\x80\x9cI think\xe2\x80\x94I\r\nthink....\r\n\r\nBut he ought to have written.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDay brought the routine of the studio, the smell of paint and\r\nturpentine, and the monotone wisdom of Kami, who was a leaden artist,\r\nbut a golden teacher if the pupil were only in sympathy with him.\r\nMaisie was not in sympathy that day, and she waited impatiently for the\r\nend of the work. She knew when it was coming; for Kami would gather his\r\nblack alpaca coat into a bunch behind him, and, with faded blue eyes\r\nthat saw neither pupils nor canvas, look back into the past to recall\r\nthe history of one Binat. \xe2\x80\x9cYou have all done not so badly,\xe2\x80\x9d he would\r\nsay. \xe2\x80\x9cBut you shall remember that it is not enough to have the method,\r\nand the art, and the power, nor even that which is touch, but you shall\r\nhave also the conviction that nails the work to the wall. Of the so\r\nmany I taught,\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94here the students would begin to unfix drawing-pins or\r\nget their tubes together,\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cthe very so many that I have taught, the\r\nbest was Binat. All that comes of the study and the work and the\r\nknowledge was to him even when he came. After he left me he should have\r\ndone all that could be done with the colour, the form, and the\r\nknowledge. Only, he had not the conviction. So to-day I hear no more of\r\nBinat,\xe2\x80\x94the best of my pupils,\xe2\x80\x94and that is long ago. So to-day, too, you\r\nwill be glad to hear no more of me. _Continuez, mesdemoiselles_, and,\r\nabove all, with conviction.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe went into the garden to smoke and mourn over the lost Binat as the\r\npupils dispersed to their several cottages or loitered in the studio to\r\nmake plans for the cool of the afternoon.\r\n\r\nMaisie looked at her very unhappy Melancolia, restrained a desire to\r\ngrimace before it, and was hurrying across the road to write a letter\r\nto Dick, when she was aware of a large man on a white troop-horse. How\r\nTorpenhow had managed in the course of twenty hours to find his way to\r\nthe hearts of the cavalry officers in quarters at Vitry-sur-Marne, to\r\ndiscuss with them the certainty of a glorious revenge for France, to\r\nreduce the colonel to tears of pure affability, and to borrow the best\r\nhorse in the squadron for the journey to Kami\xe2\x80\x99s studio, is a mystery\r\nthat only special correspondents can unravel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI beg your pardon,\xe2\x80\x9d said he. \xe2\x80\x9cIt seems an absurd question to ask, but\r\nthe fact is that I don\xe2\x80\x99t know her by any other name: Is there any young\r\nlady here that is called Maisie?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am Maisie,\xe2\x80\x9d was the answer from the depths of a great sun-hat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI ought to introduce myself,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, as the horse capered in the\r\nblinding white dust. \xe2\x80\x9cMy name is Torpenhow. Dick Heldar is my best\r\nfriend, and\xe2\x80\x94and\xe2\x80\x94the fact is that he has gone blind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBlind!\xe2\x80\x9d said Maisie, stupidly. \xe2\x80\x9cHe can\xe2\x80\x99t be blind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe has been stone-blind for nearly two months.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMaisie lifted up her face, and it was pearly white. \xe2\x80\x9cNo! No! Not blind!\r\nI won\xe2\x80\x99t have him blind!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWould you care to see for yourself?\xe2\x80\x9d said Torpenhow.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow,\xe2\x80\x94at once?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, no! The Paris train doesn\xe2\x80\x99t go through this place till to-night.\r\nThere will be ample time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid Mr. Heldar send you to me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly not. Dick wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t do that sort of thing. He\xe2\x80\x99s sitting in\r\nhis studio, turning over some letters that he can\xe2\x80\x99t read because he\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nblind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was a sound of choking from the sun-hat. Maisie bowed her head\r\nand went into the cottage, where the red-haired girl was on a sofa,\r\ncomplaining of a headache.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDick\xe2\x80\x99s blind!\xe2\x80\x9d said Maisie, taking her breath quickly as she steadied\r\nherself against a chair-back. \xe2\x80\x9cMy Dick\xe2\x80\x99s blind!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat?\xe2\x80\x9d The girl was on the sofa no longer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA man has come from England to tell me. He hasn\xe2\x80\x99t written to me for\r\nsix weeks.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you going to him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI must think.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThink! _I_ should go back to London and see him and I should kiss his\r\neyes and kiss them and kiss them until they got well again! If you\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t go I shall. Oh, what am I talking about? You wicked little idiot!\r\nGo to him at once. Go!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTorpenhow\xe2\x80\x99s neck was blistering, but he preserved a smile of infinite\r\npatience as Maisie\xe2\x80\x99s appeared bareheaded in the sunshine.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am coming,\xe2\x80\x9d said she, her eyes on the ground.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will be at Vitry Station, then, at seven this evening.\xe2\x80\x9d This was\r\nan order delivered by one who was used to being obeyed. Maisie said\r\nnothing, but she felt grateful that there was no chance of disputing\r\nwith this big man who took everything for granted and managed a\r\nsquealing horse with one hand. She returned to the red-haired girl, who\r\nwas weeping bitterly, and between tears, kisses,\xe2\x80\x94very few of\r\nthose,\xe2\x80\x94menthol, packing, and an interview with Kami, the sultry\r\nafternoon wore away.\r\n\r\nThought might come afterwards. Her present duty was to go to Dick,\xe2\x80\x94Dick\r\nwho owned the wondrous friend and sat in the dark playing with her\r\nunopened letters.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what will you do,\xe2\x80\x9d she said to her companion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI? Oh, I shall stay here and\xe2\x80\x94finish your Melancolia,\xe2\x80\x9d she said,\r\nsmiling pitifully. \xe2\x80\x9cWrite to me afterwards.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThat night there ran a legend through Vitry-sur-Marne of a mad\r\nEnglishman, doubtless suffering from sunstroke, who had drunk all the\r\nofficers of the garrison under the table, had borrowed a horse from the\r\nlines, and had then and there eloped, after the English custom, with\r\none of those more mad English girls who drew pictures down there under\r\nthe care of that good Monsieur Kami.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are very droll,\xe2\x80\x9d said Suzanne to the conscript in the moonlight\r\nby the studio wall. \xe2\x80\x9cShe walked always with those big eyes that saw\r\nnothing, and yet she kisses me on both cheeks as though she were my\r\nsister, and gives me\xe2\x80\x94see\xe2\x80\x94ten francs!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe conscript levied a contribution on both gifts; for he prided\r\nhimself on being a good soldier.\r\n\r\nTorpenhow spoke very little to Maisie during the journey to Calais; but\r\nhe was careful to attend to all her wants, to get her a compartment\r\nentirely to herself, and to leave her alone. He was amazed of the ease\r\nwith which the matter had been accomplished.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe safest thing would be to let her think things out. By Dick\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nshowing,\xe2\x80\x94when he was off his head,\xe2\x80\x94she must have ordered him about very\r\nthoroughly. Wonder how she likes being under orders.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMaisie never told. She sat in the empty compartment often with her eyes\r\nshut, that she might realise the sensation of blindness. It was an\r\norder that she should return to London swiftly, and she found herself\r\nat last almost beginning to enjoy the situation. This was better than\r\nlooking after luggage and a red-haired friend who never took any\r\ninterest in her surroundings. But there appeared to be a feeling in the\r\nair that she, Maisie,\xe2\x80\x94of all people,\xe2\x80\x94was in disgrace. Therefore she\r\njustified her conduct to herself with great success, till Torpenhow\r\ncame up to her on the steamer and without preface began to tell the\r\nstory of Dick\xe2\x80\x99s blindness, suppressing a few details, but dwelling at\r\nlength on the miseries of delirium. He stopped before he reached the\r\nend, as though he had lost interest in the subject, and went forward to\r\nsmoke. Maisie was furious with him and with herself.\r\n\r\nShe was hurried on from Dover to London almost before she could ask for\r\nbreakfast, and\xe2\x80\x94she was past any feeling of indignation now\xe2\x80\x94was bidden\r\ncurtly to wait in a hall at the foot of some lead-covered stairs while\r\nTorpenhow went up to make inquiries. Again the knowledge that she was\r\nbeing treated like a naughty little girl made her pale cheeks flame. It\r\nwas all Dick\xe2\x80\x99s fault for being so stupid as to go blind.\r\n\r\nTorpenhow led her up to a shut door, which he opened very softly. Dick\r\nwas sitting by the window, with his chin on his chest. There were three\r\nenvelopes in his hand, and he turned them over and over. The big man\r\nwho gave orders was no longer by her side, and the studio door snapped\r\nbehind her.\r\n\r\nDick thrust the letters into his pocket as he heard the sound. \xe2\x80\x9cHullo,\r\nTorp! Is that you? I\xe2\x80\x99ve been so lonely.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHis voice had taken the peculiar flatness of the blind. Maisie pressed\r\nherself up into a corner of the room. Her heart was beating furiously,\r\nand she put one hand on her breast to keep it quiet. Dick was staring\r\ndirectly at her, and she realised for the first time that he was blind.\r\nShutting her eyes in a railway-carriage to open them when she pleased\r\nwas child\xe2\x80\x99s play. This man was blind though his eyes were wide open.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTorp, is that you? They said you were coming.\xe2\x80\x9d Dick looked puzzled and\r\na little irritated at the silence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; it\xe2\x80\x99s only me,\xe2\x80\x9d was the answer, in a strained little whisper.\r\nMaisie could hardly move her lips.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cH\xe2\x80\x99m!\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick, composedly, without moving. \xe2\x80\x9cThis is a new\r\nphenomenon. Darkness I\xe2\x80\x99m getting used to; but I object to hearing\r\nvoices.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWas he mad, then, as well as blind, that he talked to himself? Maisie\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nheart beat more wildly, and she breathed in gasps. Dick rose and began\r\nto feel his way across the room, touching each table and chair as he\r\npassed. Once he caught his foot on a rug, and swore, dropping on his\r\nknees to feel what the obstruction might be. Maisie remembered him\r\nwalking in the Park as though all the earth belonged to him, tramping\r\nup and down her studio two months ago, and flying up the gangway of the\r\nChannel steamer. The beating of her heart was making her sick, and Dick\r\nwas coming nearer, guided by the sound of her breathing. She put out a\r\nhand mechanically to ward him off or to draw him to herself, she did\r\nnot know which. It touched his chest, and he stepped back as though he\r\nhad been shot.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s Maisie!\xe2\x80\x9d said he, with a dry sob. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you doing here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI came\xe2\x80\x94I came\xe2\x80\x94to see you, please.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDick\xe2\x80\x99s lips closed firmly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWon\xe2\x80\x99t you sit down, then? I\r\nforgot.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Dick, I\xe2\x80\x99m so sorry! I\xe2\x80\x99m not feeling frolicsome.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen, I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell the boys and they\xe2\x80\x99ll drag you like a badger.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease not, old man. On my word, I\xe2\x80\x99d sooner be left alone just now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery good. Can we send anything in to you? Fizz, for instance.\r\n\r\nCassavetti is beginning to sing songs of the Sunny South already.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor one minute Dick considered the proposition seriously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, thanks, I\xe2\x80\x99ve a headache already.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVirtuous child. That\xe2\x80\x99s the effect of emotion on the young. All my\r\ncongratulations, Dick. I also was concerned in the conspiracy for your\r\nwelfare.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo to the devil\xe2\x80\x94oh, send Binkie in here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe little dog entered on elastic feet, riotous from having been made\r\nmuch of all the evening. He had helped to sing the choruses; but\r\nscarcely inside the studio he realised that this was no place for\r\ntail-wagging, and settled himself on Dick\xe2\x80\x99s lap till it was bedtime.\r\nThen he went to bed with Dick, who counted every hour as it struck, and\r\nrose in the morning with a painfully clear head to receive Torpenhow\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nmore formal congratulations and a particular account of the last\r\nnight\xe2\x80\x99s revels.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou aren\xe2\x80\x99t looking very happy for a newly accepted man,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nTorpenhow.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind that\xe2\x80\x94it\xe2\x80\x99s my own affair, and I\xe2\x80\x99m all right. Do you really\r\ngo?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. With the old Central Southern as usual. They wired, and I\r\naccepted on better terms than before.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen do you start?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe day after to-morrow\xe2\x80\x94for Brindisi.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank God.\xe2\x80\x9d Dick spoke from the bottom of his heart.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, that\xe2\x80\x99s not a pretty way of saying you\xe2\x80\x99re glad to get rid of me.\r\nBut men in your condition are allowed to be selfish.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t mean that. Will you get a hundred pounds cashed for me before\r\nyou leave?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a slender amount for housekeeping, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, it\xe2\x80\x99s only for\xe2\x80\x94marriage expenses.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTorpenhow brought him the money, counted it out in fives and tens, and\r\ncarefully put it away in the writing table.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow I suppose I shall have to listen to his ravings about his girl\r\nuntil I go. Heaven send us patience with a man in love!\xe2\x80\x9d he said to\r\nhimself.\r\n\r\nBut never a word did Dick say of Maisie or marriage. He hung in the\r\ndoorway of Torpenhow\xe2\x80\x99s room when the latter was packing and asked\r\ninnumerable questions about the coming campaign, till Torpenhow began\r\nto feel annoyed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re a secretive animal, Dickie, and you consume your own smoke,\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d he said on the last evening.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x94I suppose so. By the way, how long do you think this war will last?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDays, weeks, or months. One can never tell. It may go on for years.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish I were going.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood Heavens! You\xe2\x80\x99re the most unaccountable creature! Hasn\xe2\x80\x99t it\r\noccurred to you that you\xe2\x80\x99re going to be married\xe2\x80\x94thanks to me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course, yes. I\xe2\x80\x99m going to be married\xe2\x80\x94so I am. Going to be married.\r\n\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99m awfully grateful to you. Haven\xe2\x80\x99t I told you that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou might be going to be hanged by the look of you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Torpenhow.\r\n\r\nAnd the next day Torpenhow bade him good-bye and left him to the\r\nloneliness he had so much desired.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XIV\r\n\r\n\r\nYet at the last, ere our spearmen had found him,\r\n    Yet at the last, ere a sword-thrust could save,\r\nYet at the last, with his masters around him,\r\n    He of the Faith spoke as master to slave;\r\nYet at the last, tho\xe2\x80\x99 the Kafirs had maimed him,\r\n    Broken by bondage and wrecked by the reiver,\xe2\x80\x94\r\nYet at the last, tho\xe2\x80\x99 the darkness had claimed him,\r\n    He called upon Allah and died a believer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x94_Kizzilbashi_.\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBeg your pardon, Mr. Heldar, but\xe2\x80\x94but isn\xe2\x80\x99t nothin\xe2\x80\x99 going to happen?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Mr. Beeton.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo!\xe2\x80\x9d Dick had just waked to another morning of blank despair and his\r\ntemper was of the shortest.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Tain\xe2\x80\x99t my regular business, o\xe2\x80\x99 course, sir; and what I say is, \xe2\x80\x9cMind\r\nyour own business and let other people mind theirs;\xe2\x80\x9d but just before\r\nMr. Torpenhow went away he give me to understand, like, that you might\r\nbe moving into a house of your own, so to speak\xe2\x80\x94a sort of house with\r\nrooms upstairs and downstairs where you\xe2\x80\x99d be better attended to, though\r\nI try to act just by all our tenants. Don\xe2\x80\x99t I?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! That must have been a mad-house. I shan\xe2\x80\x99t trouble you to take me\r\nthere yet. Get me my breakfast, please, and leave me alone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope I haven\xe2\x80\x99t done anything wrong, sir, but you know I hope that as\r\nfar as a man can I tries to do the proper thing by all the gentlemen in\r\nchambers\xe2\x80\x94and more particular those whose lot is hard\xe2\x80\x94such as you, for\r\ninstance, Mr. Heldar. You likes soft-roe bloater, don\xe2\x80\x99t you? Soft-roe\r\nbloaters is scarcer than hard-roe, but what I says is, \xe2\x80\x9cNever mind a\r\nlittle extra trouble so long as you give satisfaction to the tenants.\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nMr. Beeton withdrew and left Dick to himself. Torpenhow had been long\r\naway; there was no more rioting in the chambers, and Dick had settled\r\ndown to his new life, which he was weak enough to consider nothing\r\nbetter than death.\r\n\r\nIt is hard to live alone in the dark, confusing the day and night;\r\ndropping to sleep through sheer weariness at mid-day, and rising\r\nrestless in the chill of the dawn. At first Dick, on his awakenings,\r\nwould grope along the corridors of the chambers till he heard some one\r\nsnore. Then he would know that the day had not yet come, and return\r\nwearily to his bedroom.\r\n\r\nLater he learned not to stir till there was a noise and movement in the\r\nhouse and Mr. Beeton advised him to get up. Once dressed\xe2\x80\x94and dressing,\r\nnow that Torpenhow was away, was a lengthy business, because collars,\r\nties, and the like hid themselves in far corners of the room, and\r\nsearch meant head-beating against chairs and trunks\xe2\x80\x94once dressed, there\r\nwas nothing whatever to do except to sit still and brood till the three\r\ndaily meals came. Centuries separated breakfast from lunch and lunch\r\nfrom dinner, and though a man prayed for hundreds of years that his\r\nmind might be taken from him, God would never hear. Rather the mind was\r\nquickened and the revolving thoughts ground against each other as\r\nmillstones grind when there is no corn between; and yet the brain would\r\nnot wear out and give him rest. It continued to think, at length, with\r\nimagery and all manner of reminiscences. It recalled Maisie and past\r\nsuccess, reckless travels by land and sea, the glory of doing work and\r\nfeeling that it was good, and suggested all that might have happened\r\nhad the eyes only been faithful to their duty. When thinking ceased\r\nthrough sheer weariness, there poured into Dick\xe2\x80\x99s soul tide on tide of\r\noverwhelming, purposeless fear\xe2\x80\x94dread of starvation always, terror lest\r\nthe unseen ceiling should crush down upon him, fear of fire in the\r\nchambers and a louse\xe2\x80\x99s death in red flame, and agonies of fiercer\r\nhorror that had nothing to do with any fear of death. Then Dick bowed\r\nhis head, and clutching the arms of his chair fought with his sweating\r\nself till the tinkle of plates told him that something to eat was being\r\nset before him.\r\n\r\nMr. Beeton would bring the meal when he had time to spare, and Dick\r\nlearned to hang upon his speech, which dealt with badly fitted\r\ngas-plugs, waste-pipes out of repair, little tricks for driving\r\npicture-nails into walls, and the sins of the charwoman or the\r\nhousemaids. In the lack of better things the small gossip of a\r\nservant\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x99 hall becomes immensely interesting, and the screwing of a\r\nwasher on a tap an event to be talked over for days.\r\n\r\nOnce or twice a week, too, Mr. Beeton would take Dick out with him when\r\nhe went marketing in the morning to haggle with tradesmen over fish,\r\nlamp-wicks, mustard, tapioca, and so forth, while Dick rested his\r\nweight first on one foot and then on the other and played aimlessly\r\nwith the tins and string-ball on the counter. Then they would perhaps\r\nmeet one of Mr. Beeton\xe2\x80\x99s friends, and Dick, standing aside a little,\r\nwould hold his peace till Mr. Beeton was willing to go on again.\r\n\r\nThe life did not increase his self-respect. He abandoned shaving as a\r\ndangerous exercise, and being shaved in a barber\xe2\x80\x99s shop meant exposure\r\nof his infirmity. He could not see that his clothes were properly\r\nbrushed, and since he had never taken any care of his personal\r\nappearance he became every known variety of sloven. A blind man cannot\r\ndeal with cleanliness till he has been some months used to the\r\ndarkness. If he demand attendance and grow angry at the want of it, he\r\nmust assert himself and stand upright. Then the meanest menial can see\r\nthat he is blind and, therefore, of no consequence. A wise man will\r\nkeep his eyes on the floor and sit still. For amusement he may pick\r\ncoal lump by lump out of the scuttle with the tongs and pile it in a\r\nlittle heap in the fender, keeping count of the lumps, which must all\r\nbe put back again, one by one and very carefully. He may set himself\r\nsums if he cares to work them out; he may talk to himself or to the cat\r\nif she chooses to visit him; and if his trade has been that of an\r\nartist, he may sketch in the air with his forefinger; but that is too\r\nmuch like drawing a pig with the eyes shut. He may go to his\r\nbookshelves and count his books, ranging them in order of their size;\r\nor to his wardrobe and count his shirts, laying them in piles of two or\r\nthree on the bed, as they suffer from frayed cuffs or lost buttons.\r\n\r\nEven this entertainment wearies after a time; and all the times are\r\nvery, very long.\r\n\r\nDick was allowed to sort a tool-chest where Mr. Beeton kept hammers,\r\ntaps and nuts, lengths of gas-pipes, oil-bottles, and string.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I don\xe2\x80\x99t have everything just where I know where to look for it,\r\nwhy, then, I can\xe2\x80\x99t find anything when I do want it. You\xe2\x80\x99ve no idea,\r\nsir, the amount of little things that these chambers uses up,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr.\r\nBeeton. Fumbling at the handle of the door as he went out: \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s hard\r\non you, sir, I _do_ think it\xe2\x80\x99s hard on you. Ain\xe2\x80\x99t you going to do\r\nanything, sir?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll pay my rent and messing. Isn\xe2\x80\x99t that enough?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wasn\xe2\x80\x99t doubting for a moment that you couldn\xe2\x80\x99t pay your way, sir;\r\nbut I \xe2\x80\x99ave often said to my wife, \xe2\x80\x98It\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x99ard on \xe2\x80\x99im because it isn\xe2\x80\x99t as\r\nif he was an old man, nor yet a middle-aged one, but quite a young\r\ngentleman. _That\xe2\x80\x99s_ where it comes so \xe2\x80\x99ard.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose so,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick, absently. This particular nerve through long\r\nbattering had ceased to feel\xe2\x80\x94much.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was thinking,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Mr. Beeton, still making as if to go, \xe2\x80\x9cthat\r\nyou might like to hear my boy Alf read you the papers sometimes of an\r\nevening. He do read beautiful, seeing he\xe2\x80\x99s only nine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should be very grateful,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick. \xe2\x80\x9cOnly let me make it worth his\r\nwhile.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe wasn\xe2\x80\x99t thinking of _that_, sir, but of course it\xe2\x80\x99s in your own\r\n\xe2\x80\x99ands; but only to \xe2\x80\x99ear Alf sing \xe2\x80\x98A Boy\xe2\x80\x99s best Friend is \xe2\x80\x99is Mother!\xe2\x80\x99\r\nAh!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll hear him sing that too. Let him come this evening with the\r\nnewspapers.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAlf was not a nice child, being puffed up with many school-board\r\ncertificates for good conduct, and inordinately proud of his singing.\r\nMr. Beeton remained, beaming, while the child wailed his way through a\r\nsong of some eight eight-line verses in the usual whine of a young\r\nCockney, and, after compliments, left him to read Dick the foreign\r\ntelegrams. Ten minutes later Alf returned to his parents rather pale\r\nand scared.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99E said \xe2\x80\x99e couldn\xe2\x80\x99t stand it no more,\xe2\x80\x9d he explained.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe never said you read badly, Alf?\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Beeton spoke.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. \xe2\x80\x99E said I read beautiful. Said \xe2\x80\x99e never \xe2\x80\x99eard any one read like\r\nthat, but \xe2\x80\x99e said \xe2\x80\x99e couldn\xe2\x80\x99t abide the stuff in the papers.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cP\xe2\x80\x99raps he\xe2\x80\x99s lost some money in the Stocks. Were you readin\xe2\x80\x99 him about\r\nStocks, Alf?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; it was all about fightin\xe2\x80\x99 out there where the soldiers is gone\xe2\x80\x94a\r\ngreat long piece with all the lines close together and very hard words\r\nin it. \xe2\x80\x99E give me \xe2\x80\x99arf a crown because I read so well. And \xe2\x80\x99e says the\r\nnext time there\xe2\x80\x99s anything \xe2\x80\x99e wants read \xe2\x80\x99e\xe2\x80\x99ll send for me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s good hearing, but I do think for all the half-crown\xe2\x80\x94put it into\r\nthe kicking-donkey money-box, Alf, and let me see you do it\xe2\x80\x94he might\r\nhave kept you longer. Why, he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t have begun to understand how\r\nbeautiful you read.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s best left to hisself\xe2\x80\x94gentlemen always are when they\xe2\x80\x99re\r\ndownhearted,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Beeton.\r\n\r\nAlf\xe2\x80\x99s rigorously limited powers of comprehending Torpenhow\xe2\x80\x99s special\r\ncorrespondence had waked the devil of unrest in Dick. He could hear,\r\nthrough the boy\xe2\x80\x99s nasal chant, the camels grunting in the squares\r\nbehind the soldiers outside Suakin; could hear the men swearing and\r\nchaffing across the cooking pots, and could smell the acrid wood-smoke\r\nas it drifted over camp before the wind of the desert.\r\n\r\nThat night he prayed to God that his mind might be taken from him,\r\noffering for proof that he was worthy of this favour the fact that he\r\nhad not shot himself long ago. That prayer was not answered, and indeed\r\nDick knew in his heart of hearts that only a lingering sense of humour\r\nand no special virtue had kept him alive. Suicide, he had persuaded\r\nhimself, would be a ludicrous insult to the gravity of the situation as\r\nwell as a weak-kneed confession of fear.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust for the fun of the thing,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to the cat, who had taken\r\nBinkie\xe2\x80\x99s place in his establishment, \xe2\x80\x9cI should like to know how long\r\nthis is going to last. I can live for a year on the hundred pounds Torp\r\ncashed for me. I must have two or three thousand at least in the\r\nBank\xe2\x80\x94twenty or thirty years more provided for, that is to say. Then I\r\nfall back on my hundred and twenty a year, which will be more by that\r\ntime. Let\xe2\x80\x99s consider.\r\n\r\nTwenty-five\xe2\x80\x94thirty-five\xe2\x80\x94a man\xe2\x80\x99s in his prime then, they\r\nsay\xe2\x80\x94forty-five\xe2\x80\x94a middle-aged man just entering\r\npolitics\xe2\x80\x94fifty-five\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cdied at the comparatively early age of\r\nfifty-five,\xe2\x80\x9d according to the newspapers. Bah! How these Christians\r\nfunk death! Sixty-five\xe2\x80\x94we\xe2\x80\x99re only getting on in years. Seventy-five is\r\njust possible, though. Great hell, cat O! fifty years more of solitary\r\nconfinement in the dark! You\xe2\x80\x99ll die, and Beeton will die, and Torp will\r\ndie, and Mai\xe2\x80\x94everybody else will die, but I shall be alive and kicking\r\nwith nothing to do. I\xe2\x80\x99m very sorry for myself. I should like some one\r\nelse to be sorry for me. Evidently I\xe2\x80\x99m not going mad before I die, but\r\nthe pain\xe2\x80\x99s just as bad as ever. Some day when you\xe2\x80\x99re vivisected, cat O!\r\nthey\xe2\x80\x99ll tie you down on a little table and cut you open\xe2\x80\x94but don\xe2\x80\x99t be\r\nafraid; they\xe2\x80\x99ll take precious good care that you don\xe2\x80\x99t die. You\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nlive, and you\xe2\x80\x99ll be very sorry then that you weren\xe2\x80\x99t sorry for me.\r\nPerhaps Torp will come back or... I wish I could go to Torp and the\r\nNilghai, even though I were in their way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPussy left the room before the speech was ended, and Alf, as he\r\nentered, found Dick addressing the empty hearth-rug.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s a letter for you, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps you\xe2\x80\x99d like me to\r\nread it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLend it to me for a minute and I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe outstretched hand shook just a little and the voice was not\r\nover-steady. It was within the limits of human possibility that\xe2\x80\x94that\r\nwas no letter from Maisie. He knew the heft of three closed envelopes\r\nonly too well. It was a foolish hope that the girl should write to him,\r\nfor he did not realise that there is a wrong which admits of no\r\nreparation though the evildoer may with tears and the heart\xe2\x80\x99s best love\r\nstrive to mend all. It is best to forget that wrong whether it be\r\ncaused or endured, since it is as remediless as bad work once put\r\nforward.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRead it, then,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick, and Alf began intoning according to the\r\nrules of the Board School\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98_I could have given you love, I could have given you loyalty, such as\r\nyou never dreamed of. Do you suppose I cared what you were? But you\r\nchose to whistle everything down the wind for nothing. My only excuse\r\nfor you is that you are so young._\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s all,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, returning the paper to be dropped into the fire.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat was in the letter?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mrs. Beeton, when Alf returned.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know. I think it was a circular or a tract about not whistlin\xe2\x80\x99\r\nat everything when you\xe2\x80\x99re young.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI must have stepped on something when I was alive and walking about\r\nand it has bounced up and hit me. God help it, whatever it is\xe2\x80\x94unless it\r\nwas all a joke. But I don\xe2\x80\x99t know any one who\xe2\x80\x99d take the trouble to play\r\na joke on me.... Love and loyalty for nothing. It sounds tempting\r\nenough.\r\n\r\nI wonder whether I have lost anything really?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDick considered for a long time but could not remember when or how he\r\nhad put himself in the way of winning these trifles at a woman\xe2\x80\x99s hands.\r\n\r\nStill, the letter as touching on matters that he preferred not to think\r\nabout stung him into a fit of frenzy that lasted for a day and night.\r\nWhen his heart was so full of despair that it would hold no more, body\r\nand soul together seemed to be dropping without check through the\r\ndarkness.\r\n\r\nThen came fear of darkness and desperate attempts to reach the light\r\nagain. But there was no light to be reached. When that agony had left\r\nhim sweating and breathless, the downward flight would recommence till\r\nthe gathering torture of it spurred him into another fight as hopeless\r\nas the first. Followed some few minutes of sleep in which he dreamed\r\nthat he saw. Then the procession of events would repeat itself till he\r\nwas utterly worn out and the brain took up its everlasting\r\nconsideration of Maisie and might-have-beens.\r\n\r\nAt the end of everything Mr. Beeton came to his room and volunteered to\r\ntake him out. \xe2\x80\x9cNot marketing this time, but we\xe2\x80\x99ll go into the Parks if\r\nyou like.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBe damned if I do,\xe2\x80\x9d quoth Dick. \xe2\x80\x9cKeep to the streets and walk up and\r\ndown. I like to hear the people round me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis was not altogether true. The blind in the first stages of their\r\ninfirmity dislike those who can move with a free stride and unlifted\r\narms\xe2\x80\x94but Dick had no earthly desire to go to the Parks. Once and only\r\nonce since Maisie had shut her door he had gone there under Alf\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncharge. Alf forgot him and fished for minnows in the Serpentine with\r\nsome companions. After half an hour\xe2\x80\x99s waiting Dick, almost weeping with\r\nrage and wrath, caught a passer-by, who introduced him to a friendly\r\npoliceman, who led him to a four-wheeler opposite the Albert Hall. He\r\nnever told Mr. Beeton of Alf\xe2\x80\x99s forgetfulness, but... this was not the\r\nmanner in which he was used to walk the Parks aforetime.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat streets would you like to walk down, then?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Beeton,\r\nsympathetically. His own ideas of a riotous holiday meant picnicking on\r\nthe grass of Green Park with his family, and half a dozen paper bags\r\nfull of food.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cKeep to the river,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick, and they kept to the river, and the\r\nrush of it was in his ears till they came to Blackfriars Bridge and\r\nstruck thence on to the Waterloo Road, Mr. Beeton explaining the\r\nbeauties of the scenery as he went on.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd walking on the other side of the pavement,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, \xe2\x80\x9cunless I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\nmuch mistaken, is the young woman that used to come to your rooms to be\r\ndrawed. I never forgets a face and I never remembers a name, except\r\npaying tenants, o\xe2\x80\x99 course!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStop her,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s Bessie Broke. Tell her I\xe2\x80\x99d like to speak\r\nto her again. Quick, man!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Beeton crossed the road under the noses of the omnibuses and\r\narrested Bessie then on her way northward. He can\xe2\x80\x99t see. That\xe2\x80\x99s him over there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDick was leaning against the parapet of the bridge as Mr. Beeton\r\npointed him out\xe2\x80\x94a stub-bearded, bowed creature wearing a dirty\r\nmagenta-coloured neckcloth outside an unbrushed coat. It\r\nwas long since a woman of any kind had taken the trouble to speak to\r\nhim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope you\xe2\x80\x99re well, Mr. Heldar?\xe2\x80\x9d said Bessie, a little puzzled. I know their\r\nnice little ways.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA woman may love one man and despise another, but on general feminine\r\nprinciples she will do her best to save the man she despises from being\r\ndefrauded.
