Resolution shall be treated to one kiss if Bessie\r\ndoesn\xe2\x80\x99t object.\xe2\x80\x9d Strangely enough, Bessie objected and Dick laughed. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nsuppose you\xe2\x80\x99re right. Well, come at nine the day after to-morrow and\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ll get your money.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShall I sure?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t bilk, and you won\xe2\x80\x99t know whether I do or not unless you come.\r\n\r\nOh, but it\xe2\x80\x99s long and long to wait! Good-bye, Bessie,\xe2\x80\x94send Beeton here\r\nas you go out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe housekeeper came.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are all the fittings of my rooms worth?\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick, imperiously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Tisn\xe2\x80\x99t for me to say, sir. Some things is very pretty and some is\r\nwore out dreadful.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m insured for two hundred and seventy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cInsurance policies is no criterion, though I don\xe2\x80\x99t say\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, damn your longwindedness! You\xe2\x80\x99ve made your pickings out of me and\r\nthe other tenants. Why, you talked of retiring and buying a\r\npublic-house the other day. Give a straight answer to a straight\r\nquestion.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFifty,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Beeton, without a moment\xe2\x80\x99s hesitation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDouble it; or I\xe2\x80\x99ll break up half my sticks and burn the rest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe felt his way to a bookstand that supported a pile of sketch-books,\r\nand wrenched out one of the mahogany pillars.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s sinful, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said the housekeeper, alarmed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s my own. One hundred or\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne hundred it is. It\xe2\x80\x99ll cost me three and six to get that there\r\npilaster mended.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought so. What an out and out swindler you must have been to\r\nspring that price at once!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope I\xe2\x80\x99ve done nothing to dissatisfy any of the tenants, least of\r\nall you, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind that. Get me the money to-morrow, and see that all my\r\nclothes are packed in the little brown bullock-trunk. I\xe2\x80\x99m going.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut the quarter\xe2\x80\x99s notice?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll pay forfeit. Look after the packing and leave me alone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Beeton discussed this new departure with his wife, who decided that\r\nBessie was at the bottom of it all. Her husband took a more charitable\r\nview.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s very sudden\xe2\x80\x94but then he was always sudden in his ways. Listen to\r\nhim now!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was a sound of chanting from Dick\xe2\x80\x99s room.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ll never come back any more, boys,\r\nWe\xe2\x80\x99ll never come back no more;\r\nWe\xe2\x80\x99ll go to the deuce on any excuse,\r\nAnd never come back no more!\r\nOh say we\xe2\x80\x99re afloat or ashore, boys,\r\nOh say we\xe2\x80\x99re afloat or ashore;\r\nBut we\xe2\x80\x99ll never come back any more, boys,\r\nWe\xe2\x80\x99ll never come back no more!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Beeton! Mr. Beeton! Where the deuce is my pistol?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuick, he\xe2\x80\x99s going to shoot himself\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99avin\xe2\x80\x99 gone mad!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Beeton.\r\n\r\nMr. Beeton addressed Dick soothingly, but it was some time before the\r\nlatter, threshing up and down his bedroom, could realise the intention\r\nof the promises to \xe2\x80\x9cfind everything to-morrow, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, you copper-nosed old fool\xe2\x80\x94you impotent Academician!\xe2\x80\x9d he shouted at\r\nlast. \xe2\x80\x9cDo you suppose I want to shoot myself? Take the pistol in your\r\nsilly shaking hand then. If _you_ touch it, it will go off, because\r\nit\xe2\x80\x99s loaded. It\xe2\x80\x99s among my campaign-kit somewhere\xe2\x80\x94in the parcel at the\r\nbottom of the trunk.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLong ago Dick had carefully possessed himself of a forty-pound weight\r\nfield-equipment constructed by the knowledge of his own experience. It\r\nwas this put-away treasure that he was trying to find and rehandle. Mr.\r\nBeeton whipped the revolver out of its place on the top of the package,\r\nand Dick drove his hand among the _khaki_ coat and breeches, the blue\r\ncloth leg-bands, and the heavy flannel shirts doubled over a pair of\r\nswan-neck spurs. Under these and the water-bottle lay a sketch-book and\r\na pigskin case of stationery.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThese we don\xe2\x80\x99t want; you can have them, Mr. Beeton. Everything else\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ll keep. Pack \xe2\x80\x99em on the top right-hand side of my trunk. When you\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\ndone that come into the studio with your wife. I want you both. Wait a\r\nminute; get me a pen and a sheet of notepaper.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt is not an easy thing to write when you cannot see, and Dick had\r\nparticular reasons for wishing that his work should be clear. So he\r\nbegan, following his right hand with his left: \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x9cThe badness of this\r\nwriting is because I am blind and cannot see my pen.\xe2\x80\x9d H\xe2\x80\x99mph!\xe2\x80\x94even a\r\nlawyer can\xe2\x80\x99t mistake that. It must be signed, I suppose, but it needn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nbe witnessed. Now an inch lower\xe2\x80\x94why did I never learn to use a\r\ntype-writer?\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cThis is the last will and testament of me, Richard\r\nHeldar. I am in sound bodily and mental health, and there is no\r\nprevious will to revoke.\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94That\xe2\x80\x99s all right. Damn the pen! Whereabouts\r\non the paper was I?\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cI leave everything that I possess in the world,\r\nincluding four thousand pounds, and two thousand seven hundred and\r\ntwenty eight pounds held for me\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94oh, I can\xe2\x80\x99t get this straight.\xe2\x80\x9d He\r\ntore off half the sheet and began again with the caution about the\r\nhandwriting. Then: \xe2\x80\x9cI leave all the money I possess in the world\r\nto\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94here followed Maisie\xe2\x80\x99s name, and the names of the two banks that\r\nheld the money.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt mayn\xe2\x80\x99t be quite regular, but no one has a shadow of a right to\r\ndispute it, and I\xe2\x80\x99ve given Maisie\xe2\x80\x99s address. Come in, Mr. Beeton. This\r\nis my signature; I want you and your wife to witness it. Thanks.\r\nTo-morrow you must take me to the landlord and I\xe2\x80\x99ll pay forfeit for\r\nleaving without notice, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll lodge this paper with him in case\r\nanything happens while I\xe2\x80\x99m away. Now we\xe2\x80\x99re going to light up the studio\r\nstove. Stay with me, and give me my papers as I want \xe2\x80\x99em.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNo one knows until he has tried how fine a blaze a year\xe2\x80\x99s accumulation\r\nof bills, letters, and dockets can make. Dick stuffed into the stove\r\nevery document in the studio\xe2\x80\x94saving only three unopened letters;\r\ndestroyed sketch-books, rough note-books, new and half-finished\r\ncanvases alike.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat a lot of rubbish a tenant gets about him if he stays long enough\r\nin one place, to be sure,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Beeton, at last.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe does. Is there anything more left?\xe2\x80\x9d Dick felt round the walls.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot a thing, and the stove\xe2\x80\x99s nigh red-hot.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExcellent, and you\xe2\x80\x99ve lost about a thousand pounds\xe2\x80\x99 worth of sketches.\r\n\r\nHo! ho! Quite a thousand pounds\xe2\x80\x99 worth, if I can remember what I used\r\nto be.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d politely. Mr. Beeton was quite sure that Dick had gone mad,\r\notherwise he would have never parted with his excellent furniture for a\r\nsong. The canvas things took up storage room and were much better out\r\nof the way.\r\n\r\nThere remained only to leave the little will in safe hands: that could\r\nnot be accomplished to to-morrow. Dick groped about the floor picking\r\nup the last pieces of paper, assured himself again and again that there\r\nremained no written word or sign of his past life in drawer or desk,\r\nand sat down before the stove till the fire died out and the\r\ncontracting iron cracked in the silence of the night.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XV\r\n\r\n\r\nWith a heart of furious fancies,\r\n    Whereof I am commander;\r\nWith a burning spear and a horse of air,\r\n    To the wilderness I wander.\r\nWith a knight of ghosts and shadows\r\n    I summoned am to tourney\xe2\x80\x94\r\nTen leagues beyond the wide world\xe2\x80\x99s end,\r\n    Methinks it is no journey.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x94_Tom a\xe2\x80\x99 Bedlam\xe2\x80\x99s Song_.\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood-bye, Bess; I promised you fifty. Here\xe2\x80\x99s a hundred\xe2\x80\x94all that I got\r\nfor my furniture from Beeton. That will keep you in pretty frocks for\r\nsome time. You\xe2\x80\x99ve been a good little girl, all things considered, but\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ve given me and Torpenhow a fair amount of trouble.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGive Mr. Torpenhow my love if you see him, won\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course I will, dear. Now take me up the gang-plank and into the\r\ncabin. Once aboard the lugger and the maid is\xe2\x80\x94and I am free, I mean.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho\xe2\x80\x99ll look after you on this ship?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe head-steward, if there\xe2\x80\x99s any use in money. The doctor when we come\r\nto Port Said, if I know anything of P. and O. doctors. After that, the\r\nLord will provide, as He used to do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBess found Dick his cabin in the wild turmoil of a ship full of\r\nleavetakers and weeping relatives. Then he kissed her, and laid himself\r\ndown in his bunk until the decks should be clear. He who had taken so\r\nlong to move about his own darkened rooms well understood the geography\r\nof a ship, and the necessity of seeing to his own comforts was as wine\r\nto him.\r\n\r\nBefore the screw began to thrash the ship along the Docks he had been\r\nintroduced to the head-steward, had royally tipped him, secured a good\r\nplace at table, opened out his baggage, and settled himself down with\r\njoy in the cabin. It was scarcely necessary to feel his way as he moved\r\nabout, for he knew everything so well. Then God was very kind: a deep\r\nsleep of weariness came upon him just as he would have thought of\r\nMaisie, and he slept till the steamer had cleared the mouth of the\r\nThames and was lifting to the pulse of the Channel.\r\n\r\nThe rattle of the engines, the reek of oil and paint, and a very\r\nfamiliar sound in the next cabin roused him to his new inheritance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, it\xe2\x80\x99s good to be alive again!\xe2\x80\x9d He yawned, stretched himself\r\nvigorously, and went on deck to be told that they were almost abreast\r\nof the lights of Brighton. This is no more open water than Trafalgar\r\nSquare is a common; the free levels begin at Ushant; but none the less\r\nDick could feel the healing of the sea at work upon him already. A\r\nboisterous little cross-swell swung the steamer disrespectfully by the\r\nnose; and one wave breaking far aft spattered the quarterdeck and the\r\npile of new deck-chairs. He heard the foam fall with the clash of\r\nbroken glass, was stung in the face by a cupful, and sniffing\r\nluxuriously, felt his way to the smoking-room by the wheel. There a\r\nstrong breeze found him, blew his cap off and left him bareheaded in\r\nthe doorway, and the smoking-room steward, understanding that he was a\r\nvoyager of experience, said that the weather would be stiff in the\r\nchops off the Channel and more than half a gale in the Bay. These\r\nthings fell as they were foretold, and Dick enjoyed himself to the\r\nutmost. It is allowable and even necessary at sea to lay firm hold upon\r\ntables, stanchions, and ropes in moving from place to place. On land\r\nthe man who feels with his hands is patently blind. At sea even a blind\r\nman who is not sea-sick can jest with the doctor over the weakness of\r\nhis fellows. Dick told the doctor many tales\xe2\x80\x94and these are coin of more\r\nvalue than silver if properly handled\xe2\x80\x94smoked with him till unholy hours\r\nof the night, and so won his short-lived regard that he promised Dick a\r\nfew hours of his time when they came to Port Said.\r\n\r\nAnd the sea roared or was still as the winds blew, and the engines sang\r\ntheir song day and night, and the sun grew stronger day by day, and Tom\r\nthe Lascar barber shaved Dick of a morning under the opened\r\nhatch-grating where the cool winds blew, and the awnings were spread\r\nand the passengers made merry, and at last they came to Port Said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick, to the doctor, \xe2\x80\x9cto Madame Binat\xe2\x80\x99s\xe2\x80\x94if you know\r\nwhere that is.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhew!\xe2\x80\x9d said the doctor, \xe2\x80\x9cI do. There\xe2\x80\x99s not much to choose between \xe2\x80\x99em;\r\nbut I suppose you\xe2\x80\x99re aware that that\xe2\x80\x99s one of the worst houses in the\r\nplace. They\xe2\x80\x99ll rob you to begin with, and knife you later.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot they. Take me there, and I can look after myself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo he was brought to Madame Binat\xe2\x80\x99s and filled his nostrils with the\r\nwell-remembered smell of the East, that runs without a change from the\r\nCanal head to Hong-Kong, and his mouth with the villainous Lingua\r\nFranca of the Levant. The heat smote him between the shoulder-blades\r\nwith the buffet of an old friend, his feet slipped on the sand, and his\r\ncoat-sleeve was warm as new-baked bread when he lifted it to his nose.\r\n\r\nMadame Binat smiled with the smile that knows no astonishment when Dick\r\nentered the drinking-shop which was one source of her gains. But for a\r\nlittle accident of complete darkness he could hardly realise that he\r\nhad ever quitted the old life that hummed in his ears. Somebody opened\r\na bottle of peculiarly strong Schiedam. The smell reminded Dick of\r\nMonsieur Binat, who, by the way, had spoken of art and degradation.\r\n\r\nBinat was dead; Madame said as much when the doctor departed,\r\nscandalised, so far as a ship\xe2\x80\x99s doctor can be, at the warmth of Dick\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nreception. Dick was delighted at it. \xe2\x80\x9cThey remember me here after a\r\nyear. They have forgotten me across the water by this time. Madame, I\r\nwant a long talk with you when you\xe2\x80\x99re at liberty. It is good to be back\r\nagain.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn the evening she set an iron-topped caf\xc3\xa9-table out on the sands, and\r\nDick and she sat by it, while the house behind them filled with riot,\r\nmerriment, oaths, and threats. The stars came out and the lights of the\r\nshipping in the harbour twinkled by the head of the Canal.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. The war is good for trade, my friend; but what dost thou do here?\r\nWe have not forgotten thee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was over there in England and I went blind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut there was the glory first. We heard of it here, even here\xe2\x80\x94I and\r\nBinat; and thou hast used the head of Yellow \xe2\x80\x9cTina\xe2\x80\x94she is still\r\nalive\xe2\x80\x94so often and so well that \xe2\x80\x9cTina laughed when the papers arrived\r\nby the mail-boats. It was always something that we here could recognise\r\nin the paintings. And then there was always the glory and the money for\r\nthee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not poor\xe2\x80\x94I shall pay you well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot to me. Thou hast paid for everything.\xe2\x80\x9d Under her breath, \xe2\x80\x9cMon\r\nDieu, to be blind and so young! What horror!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDick could not see her face with the pity on it, or his own with the\r\ndiscoloured hair at the temples. He did not feel the need of pity; he\r\nwas too anxious to get to the front once more, and explained his\r\ndesire.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd where? The Canal is full of the English ships. Sometimes they fire\r\nas they used to do when the war was here\xe2\x80\x94ten years ago. Beyond Cairo\r\nthere is fighting, but how canst thou go there without a\r\ncorrespondent\xe2\x80\x99s passport? And in the desert there is always fighting,\r\nbut that is impossible also,\xe2\x80\x9d said she.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI must go to Suakin.\xe2\x80\x9d He knew, thanks to Alf\xe2\x80\x99s readings, that\r\nTorpenhow was at work with the column that was protecting the\r\nconstruction of the Suakin-Berber line. P. and O. steamers do not touch\r\nat that port, and, besides, Madame Binat knew everybody whose help or\r\nadvice was worth anything. They were not respectable folk, but they\r\ncould cause things to be accomplished, which is much more important\r\nwhen there is work toward.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut at Suakin they are always fighting. That desert breeds men\r\nalways\xe2\x80\x94and always more men. And they are so bold! Why to Suakin?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy friend is there.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThy friend! Chtt! Thy friend is death, then.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMadame Binat dropped a fat arm on the table-top, filled Dick\xe2\x80\x99s glass\r\nanew, and looked at him closely under the stars. There was no need that\r\nhe should bow his head in assent and say\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cNo. He is a man, but\xe2\x80\x94if it\r\nshould arrive... blamest thou?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI blame?\xe2\x80\x9d she laughed shrilly. \xe2\x80\x9cWho am I that I should blame any\r\none\xe2\x80\x94except those who try to cheat me over their consommations. But it\r\nis very terrible.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI must go to Suakin. Think for me. A great deal has changed within the\r\nyear, and the men I knew are not here. The Egyptian lighthouse steamer\r\ngoes down the Canal to Suakin\xe2\x80\x94and the post-boats\xe2\x80\x94But even then\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo not think any longer. _I_ know, and it is for me to think. Thou\r\nshalt go\xe2\x80\x94thou shalt go and see thy friend. Be wise. Sit here until the\r\nhouse is a little quiet\xe2\x80\x94I must attend to my guests\xe2\x80\x94and afterwards go to\r\nbed. Thou shalt go, in truth, thou shalt go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo-morrow?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs soon as may be.\xe2\x80\x9d She was talking as though he were a child.\r\n\r\nHe sat at the table listening to the voices in the harbour and the\r\nstreets, and wondering how soon the end would come, till Madame Binat\r\ncarried him off to bed and ordered him to sleep. The house shouted and\r\nsang and danced and revelled, Madame Binat moving through it with one\r\neye on the liquor payments and the girls and the other on Dick\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ninterests. To this latter end she smiled upon scowling and furtive\r\nTurkish officers of fellaheen regiments, was gracious to Cypriote\r\ncommissariat underlings, and more than kind to camel agents of no\r\nnationality whatever.\r\n\r\nIn the early morning, being then appropriately dressed in a flaming red\r\nsilk ball-dress, with a front of tarnished gold embroidery and a\r\nnecklace of plate-glass diamonds, she made chocolate and carried it in\r\nto Dick.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is only I, and I am of discreet age, eh? Drink and eat the roll\r\ntoo. Thus in France mothers bring their sons, when those behave wisely,\r\nthe morning chocolate.\xe2\x80\x9d She sat down on the side of the bed\r\nwhispering:\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cIt is all arranged. Thou wilt go by the lighthouse boat.\r\nThat is a bribe of ten pounds English. The captain is never paid by the\r\nGovernment. The boat comes to Suakin in four days. There will go with\r\nthee George, a Greek muleteer. Another bribe of ten pounds. I will pay;\r\nthey must not know of thy money. George will go with thee as far as he\r\ngoes with his mules. Then he comes back to me, for his well-beloved is\r\nhere, and if I do not receive a telegram from Suakin saying that thou\r\nart well, the girl answers for George.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you.\xe2\x80\x9d He reached out sleepily for the cup. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are much too\r\nkind, Madame.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf there were anything that I might do I would say, stay here and be\r\nwise; but I do not think that would be best for thee.\xe2\x80\x9d She looked at\r\nher liquor-stained dress with a sad smile. \xe2\x80\x9cNay, thou shalt go, in\r\ntruth, thou shalt go. It is best so. My boy, it is best so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe stooped and kissed Dick between the eyes. \xe2\x80\x9cThat is for\r\ngood-morning,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, going away. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen thou art dressed we will\r\nspeak to George and make everything ready. But first we must open the\r\nlittle trunk. Give me the keys.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe amount of kissing lately has been simply scandalous. I shall\r\nexpect Torp to kiss me next. He is more likely to swear at me for\r\ngetting in his way, though. Well, it won\xe2\x80\x99t last long.\xe2\x80\x94Ohe, Madame, help\r\nme to my toilette of the guillotine! There will be no chance of\r\ndressing properly out yonder.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe was rummaging among his new campaign-kit, and rowelling his hands\r\nwith the spurs. There are two ways of wearing well-oiled ankle-jacks,\r\nspotless blue bands, _khaki_ coat and breeches, and a perfectly\r\npipeclayed helmet. The right way is the way of the untired man, master\r\nof himself, setting out upon an expedition, well pleased.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEverything must be very correct,\xe2\x80\x9d Dick explained. \xe2\x80\x9cIt will become\r\ndirty afterwards, but now it is good to feel well dressed. Is\r\neverything as it should be?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe patted the revolver neatly hidden under the fulness of the blouse on\r\nthe right hip and fingered his collar.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can do no more,\xe2\x80\x9d Madame said, between laughing and crying. \xe2\x80\x9cLook at\r\nthyself\xe2\x80\x94but I forgot.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am very content.\xe2\x80\x9d He stroked the creaseless spirals of his leggings.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow let us go and see the captain and George and the lighthouse boat.\r\n\r\nBe quick, Madame.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut thou canst not be seen by the harbour walking with me in the\r\ndaylight. Figure to yourself if some English ladies\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere are no English ladies; and if there are, I have forgotten them.\r\n\r\nTake me there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn spite of this burning impatience it was nearly evening ere the\r\nlighthouse boat began to move. Madame had said a great deal both to\r\nGeorge and the captain touching the arrangements that were to be made\r\nfor Dick\xe2\x80\x99s benefit. Very few men who had the honour of her acquaintance\r\ncared to disregard Madame\xe2\x80\x99s advice. That sort of contempt might end in\r\nbeing knifed by a stranger in a gambling hell upon surprisingly short\r\nprovocation.\r\n\r\nFor six days\xe2\x80\x94two of them were wasted in the crowded Canal\xe2\x80\x94the little\r\nsteamer worked her way to Suakin, where she was to pick up the\r\nsuperintendent of the lighthouse; and Dick made it his business to\r\npropitiate George, who was distracted with fears for the safety of his\r\nlight-of-love and half inclined to make Dick responsible for his own\r\ndiscomfort. When they arrived George took him under his wing, and\r\ntogether they entered the red-hot seaport, encumbered with the material\r\nand wastage of the Suakin-Berger line, from locomotives in disconsolate\r\nfragments to mounds of chairs and pot-sleepers.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you keep with me,\xe2\x80\x9d said George, \xe2\x80\x9cnobody will ask for passports or\r\nwhat you do. They are all very busy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; but I should like to hear some of the Englishmen talk. They might\r\nremember me. I was known here a long time ago\xe2\x80\x94when I was some one\r\nindeed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA long time ago is a very long time ago here. The graveyards are full.\r\n\r\nNow listen. This new railway runs out so far as Tanai-el-Hassan\xe2\x80\x94that is\r\nseven miles. Then there is a camp. They say that beyond Tanai-el-Hassan\r\nthe English troops go forward, and everything that they require will be\r\nbrought to them by this line.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! Base camp. I see. That\xe2\x80\x99s a better business than fighting Fuzzies\r\nin the open.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor this reason even the mules go up in the iron-train.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIron what?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is all covered with iron, because it is still being shot at.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAn armoured train. Better and better! Go on, faithful George.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I go up with my mules to-night. Only those who particularly\r\nrequire to go to the camp go out with the train. They begin to shoot\r\nnot far from the city.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe dears\xe2\x80\x94they always used to!\xe2\x80\x9d Dick snuffed the smell of parched\r\ndust, heated iron, and flaking paint with delight. Certainly the old\r\nlife was welcoming him back most generously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen I have got my mules together I go up to-night, but you must first\r\nsend a telegram of Port Said, declaring that I have done you no harm.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMadame has you well in hand. Would you stick a knife into me if you\r\nhad the chance?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have no chance,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Greek. \xe2\x80\x9c_She_ is there with that woman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see. It\xe2\x80\x99s a bad thing to be divided between love of woman and the\r\nchance of loot. I sympathise with you, George.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey went to the telegraph-office unquestioned, for all the world was\r\ndesperately busy and had scarcely time to turn its head, and Suakin was\r\nthe last place under sky that would be chosen for holiday-ground. On\r\ntheir return the voice of an English subaltern asked Dick what he was\r\ndoing. The blue goggles were over his eyes and he walked with his hand\r\non George\xe2\x80\x99s elbow as he replied\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cEgyptian Government\xe2\x80\x94mules. My orders\r\nare to give them over to the A. C. G. at Tanai-el-Hassan. Any occasion\r\nto show my papers?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, certainly not. I beg your pardon. I\xe2\x80\x99d no right to ask, but not\r\nseeing your face before I\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI go out in the train to-night, I suppose,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick, boldly. \xe2\x80\x9cThere\r\nwill be no difficulty in loading up the mules, will there?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can see the horse-platforms from here. You must have them loaded\r\nup early.\xe2\x80\x9d The young man went away wondering what sort of broken-down\r\nwaif this might be who talked like a gentleman and consorted with Greek\r\nmuleteers. Dick felt unhappy. To outface an English officer is no small\r\nthing, but the bluff loses relish when one plays it from the utter\r\ndark, and stumbles up and down rough ways, thinking and eternally\r\nthinking of what might have been if things had fallen out otherwise,\r\nand all had been as it was not.\r\n\r\nGeorge shared his meal with Dick and went off to the mule-lines. His\r\ncharge sat alone in a shed with his face in his hands. Before his\r\ntight-shut eyes danced the face of Maisie, laughing, with parted lips.\r\nThere was a great bustle and clamour about him. He grew afraid and\r\nalmost called for George.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI say, have you got your mules ready?\xe2\x80\x9d It was the voice of the\r\nsubaltern over his shoulder.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy man\xe2\x80\x99s looking after them. The\xe2\x80\x94the fact is I\xe2\x80\x99ve a touch of\r\nophthalmia and can\xe2\x80\x99t see very well.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy Jove! that\xe2\x80\x99s bad. You ought to lie up in hospital for a while. I\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\nhad a turn of it myself. It\xe2\x80\x99s as bad as being blind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo I find it. When does this armoured train go?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt six o\xe2\x80\x99clock. It takes an hour to cover the seven miles.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre the Fuzzies on the rampage\xe2\x80\x94eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAbout three nights a week. Fact is I\xe2\x80\x99m in acting command of the\r\nnight-train. It generally runs back empty to Tanai for the night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBig camp at Tanai, I suppose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPretty big. It has to feed our desert-column somehow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs that far off?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBetween thirty and forty miles\xe2\x80\x94in an infernal thirsty country.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs the country quiet between Tanai and our men?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMore or less. I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t care to cross it alone, or with a\r\nsubaltern\xe2\x80\x99s command for the matter of that, but the scouts get through\r\nit in some extraordinary fashion.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey always did.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you been here before, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was through most of the trouble when it first broke out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn the service and cashiered,\xe2\x80\x9d was the subaltern\xe2\x80\x99s first thought, so\r\nhe refrained from putting any questions.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s your man coming up with the mules. It seems rather queer\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat I should be mule-leading?\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t mean to say so, but it is. Forgive me\xe2\x80\x94it\xe2\x80\x99s beastly\r\nimpertinence I know, but you speak like a man who has been at a public\r\nschool. There\xe2\x80\x99s no mistaking the tone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am a public school man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought so. I say, I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to hurt your feelings, but you\xe2\x80\x99re a\r\nlittle down on your luck, aren\xe2\x80\x99t you? I saw you sitting with your head\r\nin your hands, and that\xe2\x80\x99s why I spoke.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThanks. I am about as thoroughly and completely broke as a man need\r\nbe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSuppose\xe2\x80\x94I mean I\xe2\x80\x99m a public school man myself. Couldn\xe2\x80\x99t I perhaps\xe2\x80\x94take\r\nit as a loan y\xe2\x80\x99know and\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re much too good, but on my honour I\xe2\x80\x99ve as much money as I want.\r\n\r\n... I tell you what you could do for me, though, and put me under an\r\neverlasting obligation. Let me come into the bogie truck of the train.\r\n\r\nThere is a fore-truck, isn\xe2\x80\x99t there?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. How d\xe2\x80\x99you know?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve been in an armoured train before. Only let me see\xe2\x80\x94hear some of\r\nthe fun I mean, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll be grateful. I go at my own risk as a\r\nnon-combatant.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe young man thought for a minute. \xe2\x80\x9cAll right,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re\r\nsupposed to be an empty train, and there\xe2\x80\x99s no one to blow me up at the\r\nother end.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGeorge and a horde of yelling amateur assistants had loaded up the\r\nmules, and the narrow-gauge armoured train, plated with three-eighths\r\ninch boiler-plate till it looked like one long coffin, stood ready to\r\nstart.\r\n\r\nTwo bogie trucks running before the locomotive were completely covered\r\nin with plating, except that the leading one was pierced in front for\r\nthe muzzle of a machine-gun, and the second at either side for lateral\r\nfire.\r\n\r\nThe trucks together made one long iron-vaulted chamber in which a score\r\nof artillerymen were rioting.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhitechapel\xe2\x80\x94last train! Ah, I see yer kissin\xe2\x80\x99 in the first class\r\nthere!\xe2\x80\x9d somebody shouted, just as Dick was clambering into the forward\r\ntruck.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLordy! \xe2\x80\x99Ere\xe2\x80\x99s a real live passenger for the Kew, Tanai, Acton, and\r\nEalin\xe2\x80\x99 train. _Echo_, sir. Speshul edition! _Star_, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cShall I get\r\nyou a foot-warmer?\xe2\x80\x9d said another.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThanks. I\xe2\x80\x99ll pay my footing,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick, and relations of the most\r\namiable were established ere silence came with the arrival of the\r\nsubaltern, and the train jolted out over the rough track.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is an immense improvement on shooting the unimpressionable Fuzzy\r\nin the open,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick, from his place in the corner.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, but he\xe2\x80\x99s still unimpressed. There he goes!\xe2\x80\x9d said the subaltern, as\r\na bullet struck the outside of the truck. \xe2\x80\x9cWe always have at least one\r\ndemonstration against the night-train. Generally they attack the\r\nrear-truck, where my junior commands. He gets all the fun of the fair.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot to-night though! Listen!\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick. A flight of heavy-handed\r\nbullets was succeeded by yelling and shouts. The children of the desert\r\nvalued their nightly amusement, and the train was an excellent mark.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it worth giving them half a hopper full?\xe2\x80\x9d the subaltern asked of\r\nthe engine, which was driven by a Lieutenant of Sappers.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should think so! This is my section of the line. They\xe2\x80\x99ll be playing\r\nold Harry with my permanent way if we don\xe2\x80\x99t stop \xe2\x80\x99em.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRight O!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Hrrmph!_\xe2\x80\x9d said the machine gun through all its five noses as the\r\nsubaltern drew the lever home. The empty cartridges clashed on the\r\nfloor and the smoke blew back through the truck. There was\r\nindiscriminate firing at the rear of the train, and return fire from\r\nthe darkness without and unlimited howling. Dick stretched himself on\r\nthe floor, wild with delight at the sounds and the smells.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGod is very good\xe2\x80\x94I never thought I\xe2\x80\x99d hear this again. Give \xe2\x80\x99em hell,\r\nmen. Oh, give \xe2\x80\x99em hell!\xe2\x80\x9d he cried.\r\n\r\nThe train stopped for some obstruction on the line ahead and a party\r\nwent out to reconnoitre, but came back, cursing, for spades. The\r\nchildren of the desert had piled sand and gravel on the rails, and\r\ntwenty minutes were lost in clearing it away. Then the slow progress\r\nrecommenced, to be varied with more shots, more shoutings, the steady\r\nclack and kick of the machine guns, and a final difficulty with a\r\nhalf-lifted rail ere the train came under the protection of the roaring\r\ncamp at Tanai-el-Hassan.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, you see why it takes an hour and a half to fetch her through,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid the subaltern, unshipping the cartridge-hopper above his pet gun.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was a lark, though. I only wish it had lasted twice as long. How\r\nsuperb it must have looked from outside!\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick, sighing\r\nregretfully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt palls after the first few nights. By the way, when you\xe2\x80\x99ve settled\r\nabout your mules, come and see what we can find to eat in my tent. I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\nBennil of the Gunners\xe2\x80\x94in the artillery lines\xe2\x80\x94and mind you don\xe2\x80\x99t fall\r\nover my tent-ropes in the dark.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut it was all dark to Dick. He could only smell the camels, the\r\nhay-bales, the cooking, the smoky fires, and the tanned canvas of the\r\ntents as he stood, where he had dropped from the train, shouting for\r\nGeorge. There was a sound of light-hearted kicking on the iron skin of\r\nthe rear trucks, with squealing and grunting. George was unloading the\r\nmules.\r\n\r\nThe engine was blowing off steam nearly in Dick\xe2\x80\x99s ear; a cold wind of\r\nthe desert danced between his legs; he was hungry, and felt tired and\r\ndirty\xe2\x80\x94so dirty that he tried to brush his coat with his hands. That was\r\na hopeless job; he thrust his hands into his pockets and began to count\r\nover the many times that he had waited in strange or remote places for\r\ntrains or camels, mules or horses, to carry him to his business. In\r\nthose days he could see\xe2\x80\x94few men more clearly\xe2\x80\x94and the spectacle of an\r\narmed camp at dinner under the stars was an ever fresh pleasure to the\r\neye. There was colour, light, and motion, without which no man has much\r\npleasure in living. This night there remained for him only one more\r\njourney through the darkness that never lifts to tell a man how far he\r\nhas travelled. Then he would grip Torpenhow\xe2\x80\x99s hand again\xe2\x80\x94Torpenhow, who\r\nwas alive and strong, and lived in the midst of the action that had\r\nonce made the reputation of a man called Dick Heldar: not in the least\r\nto be confused with the blind, bewildered vagabond who seemed to answer\r\nto the same name. Yes, he would find Torpenhow, and come as near to the\r\nold life as might be. Afterwards he would forget everything: Bessie,\r\nwho had wrecked the Melancolia and so nearly wrecked his life; Beeton,\r\nwho lived in a strange unreal city full of tin-tacks and gas-plugs and\r\nmatters that no men needed; that irrational being who had offered him\r\nlove and loyalty for nothing, but had not signed her name; and most of\r\nall Maisie, who, from her own point of view, was undeniably right in\r\nall she did, but oh, at this distance, so tantalisingly fair.\r\n\r\nGeorge\xe2\x80\x99s hand on his arm pulled him back to the situation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what now?\xe2\x80\x9d said George.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh yes of course. What now? Take me to the camel-men. Take me to where\r\nthe scouts sit when they come in from the desert. They sit by their\r\ncamels, and the camels eat grain out of a black blanket held up at the\r\ncorners, and the men eat by their side just like camels. Take me\r\nthere!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe camp was rough and rutty, and Dick stumbled many times over the\r\nstumps of scrub. The scouts were sitting by their beasts, as Dick knew\r\nthey would. The light of the dung-fires flickered on their bearded\r\nfaces, and the camels bubbled and mumbled beside them at rest. It was\r\nno part of Dick\xe2\x80\x99s policy to go into the desert with a convoy of\r\nsupplies. That would lead to impertinent questions, and since a blind\r\nnon-combatant is not needed at the front, he would probably be forced\r\nto return to Suakin.\r\n\r\nHe must go up alone, and go immediately.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow for one last bluff\xe2\x80\x94the biggest of all,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cPeace be with\r\nyou, brethren!\xe2\x80\x9d The watchful George steered him to the circle of the\r\nnearest fire. The heads of the camel-sheiks bowed gravely, and the\r\ncamels, scenting a European, looked sideways curiously like brooding\r\nhens, half ready to get to their feet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA beast and a driver to go to the fighting line to-night,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA Mulaid?\xe2\x80\x9d said a voice, scornfully naming the best baggage-breed that\r\nhe knew.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA Bisharin,\xe2\x80\x9d returned Dick, with perfect gravity. \xe2\x80\x9cA Bisharin without\r\nsaddle-galls. Therefore no charge of thine, shock-head.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTwo or three minutes passed. Then\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cWe be knee-haltered for the night.\r\nThere is no going out from the camp.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot for money?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cH\xe2\x80\x99m! Ah! English money?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnother depressing interval of silence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow much?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTwenty-five pounds English paid into the hand of the driver at my\r\njourney\xe2\x80\x99s end, and as much more into the hand of the camel-sheik here,\r\nto be paid when the driver returns.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis was royal payment, and the sheik, who knew that he would get his\r\ncommission on this deposit, stirred in Dick\xe2\x80\x99s behalf.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor scarcely one night\xe2\x80\x99s journey\xe2\x80\x94fifty pounds. Land and wells and good\r\ntrees and wives to make a man content for the rest of his days. Who\r\nspeaks?\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI,\xe2\x80\x9d said a voice. \xe2\x80\x9cI will go\xe2\x80\x94but there is no going from the camp.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFool! I know that a camel can break his knee-halter, and the sentries\r\ndo not fire if one goes in chase. Twenty-five pounds and another\r\ntwenty-five pounds. But the beast must be a good Bisharin; I will take\r\nno baggage-camel.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen the bargaining began, and at the end of half an hour the first\r\ndeposit was paid over to the sheik, who talked in low tones to the\r\ndriver.\r\n\r\nDick heard the latter say: \xe2\x80\x9cA little way out only. Any baggage-beast\r\nwill serve. Am I a fool to waste my cattle for a blind man?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd though I cannot see\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94Dick lifted his voice a little\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cyet I carry\r\nthat which has six eyes, and the driver will sit before me. If we do\r\nnot reach the English troops in the dawn he will be dead.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut where, in God\xe2\x80\x99s name, are the troops?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUnless thou knowest let another man ride. Dost thou know? Remember it\r\nwill be life or death to thee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know,\xe2\x80\x9d said the driver, sullenly. \xe2\x80\x9cStand back from my beast. I am\r\ngoing to slip him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot so swiftly. George, hold the camel\xe2\x80\x99s head a moment. I want to feel\r\nhis cheek.\xe2\x80\x9d The hands wandered over the hide till they found the\r\nbranded half-circle that is the mark of the Biharin, the light-built\r\nriding-camel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is well. Cut this one loose. Remember no blessing of God comes on\r\nthose who try to cheat the blind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe men chuckled by the fires at the camel-driver\xe2\x80\x99s discomfiture. He\r\nhad intended to substitute a slow, saddle-galled baggage-colt.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStand back!\xe2\x80\x9d one shouted, lashing the Biharin under the belly with a\r\nquirt. Dick obeyed as soon as he felt the nose-string tighten in his\r\nhand,\xe2\x80\x94and a cry went up, \xe2\x80\x9cIllaha! Aho! He is loose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWith a roar and a grunt the Biharin rose to his feet and plunged\r\nforward toward the desert, his driver following with shouts and\r\nlamentation.\r\n\r\nGeorge caught Dick\xe2\x80\x99s arm and hurried him stumbling and tripping past a\r\ndisgusted sentry who was used to stampeding camels.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the row now?\xe2\x80\x9d he cried.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEvery stitch of my kit on that blasted dromedary,\xe2\x80\x9d Dick answered,\r\nafter the manner of a common soldier.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo on, and take care your throat\xe2\x80\x99s not cut outside\xe2\x80\x94you and your\r\ndromedary\xe2\x80\x99s.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe outcries ceased when the camel had disappeared behind a hillock,\r\nand his driver had called him back and made him kneel down.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMount first,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick. Then climbing into the second seat and gently\r\nscrewing the pistol muzzle into the small of his companion\xe2\x80\x99s back, \xe2\x80\x9cGo\r\non in God\xe2\x80\x99s name, and swiftly. Good-bye, George. Remember me to Madame,\r\nand have a good time with your girl. Get forward, child of the Pit!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA few minutes later he was shut up in a great silence, hardly broken by\r\nthe creaking of the saddle and the soft pad of the tireless feet. Dick\r\nadjusted himself comfortably to the rock and pitch of the pace, girthed\r\nhis belt tighter, and felt the darkness slide past. For an hour he was\r\nconscious only of the sense of rapid progress.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA good camel,\xe2\x80\x9d he said at last.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe was never underfed. He is my own and clean bred,\xe2\x80\x9d the driver\r\nreplied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo on.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHis head dropped on his chest and he tried to think, but the tenor of\r\nhis thoughts was broken because he was very sleepy. In the half doze it\r\nseemed that he was learning a punishment hymn at Mrs. Jennett\xe2\x80\x99s. He had\r\ncommitted some crime as bad as Sabbath-breaking, and she had locked him\r\nup in his bedroom. But he could never repeat more than the first two\r\nlines of the hymn\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\nWhen Israel of the Lord beloved\r\nOut of the land of bondage came.\r\n\r\n\r\nHe said them over and over thousands of times. The driver turned in the\r\nsaddle to see if there were any chance of capturing the revolver and\r\nending the ride. Dick roused, struck him over the head with the butt,\r\nand stormed himself wide awake. Somebody hidden in a clump of\r\ncamel-thorn shouted as the camel toiled up rising ground. A shot was\r\nfired, and the silence shut down again, bringing the desire to sleep.\r\nDick could think no longer. He was too tired and stiff and cramped to\r\ndo more than nod uneasily from time to time, waking with a start and\r\npunching the driver with the pistol.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs there a moon?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked drowsily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is near her setting.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish that I could see her. Halt the camel. At least let me hear the\r\ndesert talk.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe man obeyed. Out of the utter stillness came one breath of wind. It\r\nrattled the dead leaves of a shrub some distance away and ceased. A\r\nhandful of dry earth detached itself from the edge of a rail trench and\r\ncrumbled softly to the bottom.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo on. The night is very cold.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThose who have watched till the morning know how the last hour before\r\nthe light lengthens itself into many eternities. It seemed to Dick that\r\nhe had never since the beginning of original darkness done anything at\r\nall save jolt through the air. Once in a thousand years he would finger\r\nthe nailheads on the saddle-front and count them all carefully.\r\nCenturies later he would shift his revolver from his right hand to his\r\nleft and allow the eased arm to drop down at his side. From the safe\r\ndistance of London he was watching himself thus employed,\xe2\x80\x94watching\r\ncritically. Yet whenever he put out his hand to the canvas that he\r\nmight paint the tawny yellow desert under the glare of the sinking\r\nmoon, the black shadow of a camel and the two bowed figures atop, that\r\nhand held a revolver and the arm was numbed from wrist to collar-bone.\r\nMoreover, he was in the dark, and could see no canvas of any kind\r\nwhatever.\r\n\r\nThe driver grunted, and Dick was conscious of a change in the air.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI smell the dawn,\xe2\x80\x9d he whispered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is here, and yonder are the troops. Have I done well?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe camel stretched out its neck and roared as there came down wind the\r\npungent reek of camels in the square.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo on. We must get there swiftly. Go on.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are moving in their camp. There is so much dust that I cannot see\r\nwhat they do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAm I in better case? Go forward.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey could hear the hum of voices ahead, the howling and the bubbling\r\nof the beasts and the hoarse cries of the soldiers girthing up for the\r\nday.\r\n\r\nTwo or three shots were fired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs that at us? Surely they can see that I am English,\xe2\x80\x9d Dick spoke\r\nangrily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, it is from the desert,\xe2\x80\x9d the driver answered, cowering in his\r\nsaddle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo forward, my child! Well it is that the dawn did not uncover us an\r\nhour ago.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe camel headed straight for the column and the shots behind\r\nmultiplied. The children of the desert had arranged that most\r\nuncomfortable of surprises, a dawn attack for the English troops, and\r\nwere getting their distance by snap-shots at the only moving object\r\nwithout the square.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat luck! What stupendous and imperial luck!\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cjust\r\nbefore the battle, mother.\xe2\x80\x9d Oh, God has been most good to me!\r\n\r\nOnly\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94the agony of the thought made him screw up his eyes for an\r\ninstant\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cMaisie...\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAllahu! We are in,\xe2\x80\x9d said the man, as he drove into the rearguard and\r\nthe camel knelt.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho the deuce are you? Despatches or what? What\xe2\x80\x99s the strength of the\r\nenemy behind that ridge? How did you get through?\xe2\x80\x9d asked a dozen\r\nvoices. For all answer Dick took a long breath, unbuckled his belt, and\r\nshouted from the saddle at the top of a wearied and dusty voice,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTorpenhow! Ohe, Torp! Coo-ee, Tor-pen-how.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA bearded man raking in the ashes of a fire for a light to his pipe\r\nmoved very swiftly towards that cry, as the rearguard, facing about,\r\nbegan to fire at the puffs of smoke from the hillocks around. Gradually\r\nthe scattered white cloudlets drew out into the long lines of banked\r\nwhite that hung heavily in the stillness of the dawn before they turned\r\nover wave-like and glided into the valleys. The soldiers in the square\r\nwere coughing and swearing as their own smoke obstructed their view,\r\nand they edged forward to get beyond it. A wounded camel leaped to its\r\nfeet and roared aloud, the cry ending in a bubbling grunt. Some one had\r\ncut its throat to prevent confusion. Then came the thick sob of a man\r\nreceiving his death-wound from a bullet; then a yell of agony and\r\nredoubled firing.\r\n\r\nThere was no time to ask any questions.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGet down, man! Get down behind the camel!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. Put me, I pray, in the forefront of the battle.\xe2\x80\x9d Dick turned his\r\nface to Torpenhow and raised his hand to set his helmet straight, but,\r\nmiscalculating the distance, knocked it off. Torpenhow saw that his\r\nhair was gray on the temples, and that his face was the face of an old\r\nman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome down, you damned fool! Dickie, come off!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd Dick came obediently, but as a tree falls, pitching sideways from\r\nthe Bisharin\xe2\x80\x99s saddle at Torpenhow\xe2\x80\x99s feet. His luck had held to the\r\nlast, even to the crowning mercy of a kindly bullet through his head.\r\n\r\nTorpenhow knelt under the lee of the camel, with Dick\xe2\x80\x99s body in his\r\narms.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n*** END OF THE PROJECT GUTENBERG EBOOK THE LIGHT THAT FAILED ***\r\n\r\n***** This file should be named 2876-0.txt or 2876-0.zip *****\r\nThis and all associated files of various formats will be found in:\r\n    https://www.gutenberg.org/2/8/7/2876/\r\n\r\nUpdated editions will replace the previous one--the old editions will\r\nbe renamed.\r\n\r\nCreating the works from print editions not protected by U.S. copyright\r\nlaw means that no one owns a United States copyright in these works,\r\nso the Foundation (and you!) can copy and distribute it in the\r\nUnited States without permission and without paying copyright\r\nroyalties.
